The Ways of Many Waters
Collects the dashing ballads and verses of sailor-men's
and wharf-men’s life which E. J. Brady has contributed
to The Bulletin and the Sunday Times, Sydney, it i 3
the first attempt on the part of an Australian verse-
writer to chant the life on the sea and the margins of
the sea ; though there is one horse-poem—the story of
“ How Jack Bowlin Steered ‘ Jones.’ ” The volume is
bound in buckram, with gilt top, in a size that will go
into a pocket without squeezing; and the price is ss,
post-free.

THE RED PAGE.
Education and Atmosphere.
THE vyork of a great artist is unlaboured—or
so it seems. The' word is a haunting
felicity, the colour a miracle, the music a
creating passion. It is an aspiration
towards the ideal of humanity. The poet, for
instance, enters by sympathy into the sorrow of
parting, the tenderness of the past, the hope of
the future, and gives to these emotions melodious
expression. He is the friend of all-reticent dream-
ing souls.
But the artist cannot live, in the highest sense,
without poetic atmosphere. It is breath and,
inspiration: a harmonising power. I have
talked with labourers in the harvest, and
as the twilight came on I have seen the
hush of those solemn fields creep into their
blood, and insensibly lift and purify their
thought and its expression. Not otherwise is the
effect of that sweet, spiritual atmosphere oreated
by the yearnings of suffering man. And this
penetrating air is lacking in a new country ; for
it is the pensive work of time and genius.
In No. 1103 of 'the Bulletin there are quoted
some very beautiful lines by the Irish poet, W.
B. Yeats. When I read them I repeated to
myself that old farewell of the sixteenth century
poet:
How cease, my lute : this is the laste
Labour that thou and I shall waste,
And ended is that we beg-un.
And is my song both sung and past;
My lute, he still, for I am done.
Yeats’s poetry is impregnate with the wistful
atmosphere of an older and more beautiful world.
And the question rises in the mind : “Is a Yeats
possible to Australia?” I think not. For con-
sider those old eternities that lie behind this young
Celtic writer. There is Erin of the soft rain and
the round green hills—the ancient dwellings of
the fairy Hill Folk ; there is the Inner Ireland,
mournful, veiled, and grey, with holy wells and
high places; there are the Tir-nan-oge, the land
of Immortal Youth and of the Living Heart, and
Moy Mell, the Plain of Pleasure; and at the back
of all a region of dead autumnal colour, where
the voices of the elements are familiar, and speak
of Death and Sleep. All these, and more, go to
the making of Yeats, and we—we have lost them,
and, I fear, the power to enjoy them.
We stand at the parting of the ways. Let us
fearlessly face the fact that Christ, as a living
force, has gone out of the lives of the majority of
men. This is not the place to discuss the Master’s
claims ; but we must note the loss, for poetic
NATURAL GREATNESS.
The descendant of a line of peasants.
atmosphere is in one sense diffused religious life.
Christ and his passion freed the heart, gave
strength and bearing, sobered and purified, and
imparted inward peace. The processipn of his
I lntercou F se with high and eternal
tents nf hi a arg,e sadness and deep-spirited eon-
nearnei of L WOvda ’ j he ideal of sacrifice ; the
and earth ; the awed waiting;

atTi?«° eS^ eloU f d [ uthe ears of Australia sitting
, ° f the wa 7 s - Is she > with Hecate*
daughter of Perseus, thinking delicate thoughts?
Alas . voices and thoughts are no longer concerned
and occupied with the things that are best. The
w -tien, the immaculate grace of a Yeats, are
st m the tumult of war and social discontent.
t\/t S consolation to the conquered ?
Must the heart be broken and the eyes filled with
tears before song can flow? These are difficult
questions to answer; one thing is certain, the
accent of the finest poetry comes from the con-
quest of grief. Yet it is possible, helped by the
nascent power of its birth, to create in
t f na T l ew Commonwealth an atmosphere that
shall inevitably make for all that is pure and
musical in life. It will not have the essential
aivineness that proceeds from suffering Christ to
be our ideal; it may not have the pathos of that
exhaled by the yew and the cypress ; but it will
nave, for those who breathe it, quiet, comfort and
s°y* dt will be brought into existence by a
new education, which will be in essence an attempt
to preserve inviolate the delicate, but enduring
impressions of a guarded and happy childhood
and youth. It will recognise that a happy youth
is the best preparation for a happy old age. The
certificate,” that-enemy to originality, that in-
fluence fatal to the making of character, will be
taken out, of the life of tne child. At present,
youth, priceless youth, is sacrificed to the inspec-
tor; and the examination, not the Lord, is the
child s portion. We are agreed that the virtue of
a University career lies in its uselessness. Let
the early years of his life be such a sacred time,
unrobbed of its freshness and wonder, and we
shall have solved the problem of a University
training for the poor man’s offspring.
The requirements of the examination of to-day
are such that teacher and taught are for ever
hurried on through the pleasant fields where they
would fain linger.
You desire to identify yourself with “ the glory
that was Greece and the grandeur that was
Rome”; or you are anxious to walk in quiet
HEREDITARY GREATNESS.
The descendant of a line of princes.
From the drawing by “Cynicus."
places, to let your children drink at the fountain
in Perugia, or to lead their feet in the charmed
footprints of St. Francis over the Umbrian hills.
You must not, the Examination is in November.
Instead of bread you must give your children
stones —weary and barren statements. I say a
child is infinitely better educated who has
grown up by the fireside listening to the
sweet old ballads of love and death crooned
by the grandmother at her spinning-wheel.
There is no golden leisure in the school-lives of
the children. I go back to my old school-reader
to find this tyranny foretold. There I read the
story of Merrymind and his Fiddle—a tiny
masterpiece, or made so by the fond enchantment
of years. The boy, Merrymind, amid the laughter
of his father and his brothers and sisters, buys
a stringless fiddle at the fair with his
hoarded shilling. Only his mother under-
stands him. He goes out into the world
to seek the fairy gold-spinners and their
strings, for ordinary ones snap at once
when stretched along this wonderful
fiddle. He enters, knowingly, the
Valley of Toil, which none may
leave during life. But there is a
tradition that the Valley was once
a place of joy and song. One
night Merrymind sees the gold-
spinners in his hut, and in gratitude
(1 forget for what service) they leave
on the eartl] a couple of long shining
golden threads. With these he
strings his fiddle and goes fiddling up
the valley, and brings again the Golden
Age. Who will be Merrymind to free
the children in this Valley of Toil ?
Yes, the greatest advance in the
Twentieth Century will be the reform of
education. Surely it is self-evident that
the minds of the children should be under
the care of the most cultured men of the
nation. It is a safe prophecy that in those
days the schoolmaster will be honoured and loved
above any man in the State —be he pastor,
doctor, scientist, soldier, or statesman. These
masters of the future will not cram the chil-
dren : they will talk to them, drawing freely
upon the world’s most beautiful traditions.
1 can imagine some of the work of such
a school. Will the children, for instance,
be tortured with those senseless sums
. about clocks, so much in favour with
inspectors? There will be no clock for these
happy youngsters. Under the spell of the mas-
ter’s voice time will fleet noiselessly and sweetly
like the moving shadow on a dial in sunny grass
lands. Without diminishing their joy in life, each
master will convey to the souls of his charge some-
thing of that pure and austere melancholy which is
Virgil’s enduring charm. He will teach them,
perhaps using Newman’s own perfect words, that
lines from the classics, “ the birth of some chance
morning or evening at an lonian festival, or among
the Sabine hills,” will at length, after years, come
home to them, and ‘ ‘ pierce them with their sad
earnestness and vivid exactness.” In that little
collection of passages culled from the writers of
antiquity, there will be a few Odes from the works
of Horace dealing with the delights of spring, and
the warm indoors of winter, and the pleasures of
an ancestral farm.
O fons Bandusiae.
They will also read some of those chapters in Latin
authors which speak so nobly of fortitude and
virtuous living and unfeared death. Nor will
they, I believe, withhold from their pupils those
intuitions of “goodness, beauty, truth, and love
at their divinest in Jesus Christ.” Throughout,
the education will be limited—but exquisite in
emotion. Only by emotion can we change and
mould character. As for the fruits of emotionless
systems of education, are not their records given
in full in the crowded pages of our daily news-
papers ?
Such an education will be costly, for you must
get the best men and trust them. ' Yet it will be
iess expensive than war and will in the end make
war impossible. It will not come for many years,
for the people believe, that education means the
memorising of long weary lists of names and “cut
and dried ” statements. But it will come! and
with it an atmosphere of feeling in which life
shall blossom naturally into art and song.

JAMES HKDBLRTHWAITYE.
The Case of Kipling.
IN the operation of “putting Kipling where
he belongs” (8., 27/4/1), consider “The
Strange Ride of Morrdwbie Jukes” (“The
Phantom ’Rickshaw and Other Eerie
Tales ”). This story deals with the adventures of
Jukes in the place whither are brought Hindus
who “die at home and do not die when they
come to the ghat to be burnt,” having revived
after being to all intents and purposes defunct.
Said place, according to Kipling, consists of “a
horseshoe-shaped crater of sand, opening bn one
side directly on to the shoals of the Sutlej ” river.
It is closed in on three sides with “ steeply-graded
sandwalls, about thirty-five feet high,” whose
slope is “ about 65°. ” The river front is beset with
quicksands, and a picket-boat is stationed in the
stream by day, making escape in that direction
impossible. An; exit by scaling the walls is also
impracticable, since the climber is sent rolling
down to the pit’s bottom by the avalanches of
sand brought down by his struggles. And yet, so
great is the desire for liberty, that the prisoners
vainly attempt the escalade
at intervals with the same
result—overwhelming down-
pour of sand.
Now, the question is this
—Why should not this con-
stant flow of sand gradually
fill up the crater, thus allow-
ing the , inmates to walk
out without further trouble ?
Originally, the shape of the
crater is, roughly speaking,
that of a flower-pot—what
is to prevent it ultimately
•assuming the shape of a
saucer ? f lf this, process
took too long to accom-
plish, the prisoners could,
by, judiciously “rushing”
one particular place, form
a climbable slope at that
spot in a much shorter time.
But Kipling merely says
that the walls cannot be
climbed.
[Further, question whether
loose sand would maintain
the angle of 05° when sub-
jected to, the natural in-
fluences of rain and wind.
Left alone, the “crater”
would surely acquire the
saucer shape. Kipling seems
to have been misled by a
loose parallel with the a -lion trap. But the ant-
lion is often in wait foi .„s victims; and Kipling
forgets the essential element of time. The ant-
lion is always constructing new traps.—-it.]
As regards the river frontage, Jukes finds that
there is a way through the quicksands. This had
been discovered by a previous denizen of the pit,
who had almost completed a' plan of the route
when he was killed. Owing to the presence of
the guard-.boat by day, his researches had been
pursued at night. Jukes finds the corpse, and
takes from its coat-pocket a notebook in which
the particulars are recorded. The unit of rheasure-
ment is a gun-barrel, and the path to liberty
starts, from a point designated the “crow-clump ”
which is midway between the “horns” of the
crater where it opens on the quicksands. Follow-
ing are the directions referred to
“Four out from crow-clump j ‘out' means in
the direction of the river] ; three left ; nine out :
two right ; three back ; two left; fourteen out •
two left ; seven out ; one left'; nine back ; two
right ; six back ; four right; seven back.”
Now, if you take the trouble to map this out
(adopting, of oourse, an unvarying unit of measure-
ment), you will find that the track doubles back
H l . recrosses itself in such a way as proves that
Kipling has just set down the measurements hap-
hazard, without troubling to verify them. Exactly
the same result, as regards distance from the
starting-point, is obtainable from the following
formula, which considerably shortens the track :
—Four out, three left, twelve out, three right,
seven back. (See annexed drawing, reduced pro-
portionately from a plotting scale of J-inch units.)
Even then, the explorer is not much “ for’arder,”
being, in a straight line, only “ nine out ” from
his starting point. And since he must bear either
to the right or the left to get round one of the
“ horns’" of the crater, he is very little the better
for his abstruse calculations. After going so far,
Kipling evidently gets tired, and crawls out of
the difficulty by allowing Jukes to be hauled out
of the pit on a rope thrown by one of his servants,
who has discovered his master’s whereabouts.
Some of Kipling’s yarns are like oysters —they
must be swallowed whole. It doesn’t do to
dissect them.
As to verse-inaccuracies, in “The Native
Born” occurs a line, “We screamed with the
painted lories as we rode
on the dusty plains,” which
occasioned some discussion in
the “ Aboriginalities ” column
some time ago, it being as-
serted that the lory is not given
to screaming, nor is it found
on “ the dusty plains.” Again,
in “The Broken-Link Handi-
cap” (“Plain Tales from the
Hills ”) Kipling speaks of the
Maribymong Plate as a “ jump-
race,” and says that the jumps
are constructed of masonry and
jarrah. Surely Victoria does n’t
import jarrah all the way from
Westralia for the building of
“ jumps.”
(1) Track as per Kipling’s Formula.
(2) Track as per Improved Formula.
A, A,' Respective points of departure.
E, E,’ Respective points of arrival.
It will be noticed that in No. (1) the track is needlessly
retraced from B to C, and that there is no occasion for it
to go further “ out ” than D.
As regards Kipling’s imitations, compare “The
Last Rhyme of True Thomas” and “The Sea
Wife” (“The Seven Seas ”) with “Thomas the
Rhymer’’and “The Wife of Usher’s Well” re-
spectively. The latter are Scottish Border bal-
lads. In “An American” the first verse is so
obviously an adaptation of Robert Browning’s
Brahrna that it can hardly be called mere
“conveying.” The original poems are by no
means unknown ; and, qud imitations, practically
avowed, these are on a different footing from work
purporting to be original; but the instances
go to reinforce the argument that “what ’e
thought ’e might require, ’e went an’'took.”
To conclude, Kipling’s easy knack of using
technicalities, and his airy, off-hand habit of
throwing in a local allusion, correct or otherwise,
where it will do most good, go far to convince his
readers that his knowledge of this earth, its con-
tents and their adjuncts, is exact to the smallest
detail. Above all, he has a splendid ear for words
and phrases, which, when used in his tricky,
casual, slap-dash fashion, will give the impression
that he is thoroughly conversant with whatever
subject he may choose'to deal with ; and the man
in the street, whose acquaintance with things
foreign is at best superficial, is gulled by the smart
use of Such tags and catchwords into the belief
that Kipling’s omniscience and infallibility are
beyond question.—Jm\

The late Dr. Tanner was not a, literary man
(says /I'he A naiLmii) but was capable of flights when
he took up his pen. Once, when he had asked in
the House whether it was true that the Duke of
Cambridge had resigned his position as Com-
mander-in-Chief, a Major Jones, of Penzance, was
so outraged that he challenged Dr. Tanner to a
duel, and the following telegraphic correspondence
took place— -
In reply to your despicable question about the Duke of
Cambridge, I designate you a coward. Delighted to give
you satisfaction across the water. Pistols.
To which Dr. Tanner at once replied—
Wire received. Will meet you to-morrow in Constan-
tinople, under the. Tower of Galata, midnight. Being
challenged, prefer torpedoes. Bring another ass
Tanner.
Ma jor Jones answered—
Midnight meetings are for Moonlighters. You know
what that means. Ask Colonel Saunderson who shows
the white feather. •
Finally, Dr. Tanner wired—
Never suggested Colonel Saunderson showed any white
feather. Would strongly recommend bromide of potas-
sium this vwm weather for the staggers. Further reply
useless. Will not spend another Tanner.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Vanguard of the West.
Hark ! to the ringing of axes, bringing
Death to the mountain pine;
Hark ! to the thunder which rends asunder
The bonds of the prisoned mine.
List ! to the clamour of drill and hammer
In the tunnels adown below,
Where men are working, and death is lurking,
And the flickering lights are low.
Hark ! the big fall’s thunder down hills of wonde
Through the march of the lonely years ;
Hark, the spell is broken, the axe has spoken
Your challenge, O pioneers !
On hillsides bitter their keen blades glitter
As they ring on the mountain wood ;
And the winds will sorrow thro’ many a morrow
Where the king of the myrtles stood.
You may trace their marches thro’ forest arches,
Seared summits their steps have seen ;
Where the snow-stream races in treacherous places
Their calico camps have been.
They have stemmed the torrent and spanned the
current
Of waters that Westward rolled,
Ere the Mint might measure her hoarded treasure,
Or the wealth of the West be told.
Till her rock-bands yielded the wealth they
shielded,
And elfman, and imp, and gnome,
Have listened, quaking, the Titans shaking
The halls of their island home.
The fog is lifting, the clouds are drifting,
And far as the eye can roam
Over hills dividing, thro’ vapors hiding,
Glimmers Pacific’s foam.
By Heemskirk’s shoulder, gray cliff and boulder
Are bathed in the crimson light
Of the sunset glory ; day and my story
Are drifting towards “ Good night.”
T.
M.E.J.P.
Splittin’.
There’s a graft that wears the muscle and the
sturdy spirit kills,
And writes the sign of misery upon the grafter’s
phiz :
That’s choppin’ curly billets for the blessed fire-
wood mills,-
Or splittin’ bloomin’ engine-wood for dealers in
the biz.
For it’s swing the axe and bullock, or it’s handle
it with tact,
And draw upon experience and all the splittin’
knacks;
Graft from Jackass round to'Mopoke, and you 11
realise the fact
That graft can choke ambition in the man that
swings the axe.
Then it’s pick a tree and scarf it till the axe
an echo rings—
It’s chippin’ free you’re jolly, for you think
you are in luck ;
But your curses are a caution, and they scorch the
face of things.
When the agent wires “No business, and demur-
rage on the truck.”
So, it’s drive the saw and draw it till your muscles
ache again, . . ,
And swing it clear for safety when the yieldm
tree-bole cracks;
Belt the wedges —“double-bank” ’em till you
tremble from the strain,
And the trees sing “Sweatin’ wages ” to the man
that swings the axe.
Go splittin’on the “common,” never thinkin’ of
the “ Crown,”
Till a bobby comes along with, “ Got a license,
there, me mahn ? ”
You prevaricate, and shuffle, but your name is
taken down,
And you scoot to dodge the summons and con-
viction while you can. _ ?
So, it’s burst the blocks that quarter, and its
“back” the blocks that won’t,
And pay for teams to cart ’em on the soggy,
boggy tracks. t ;
There’s tucker if you bullock, and there isn tif
you don’t,
And no one troubles much about the man that
swings the axe.
The dealers are exultant when a strike in New
South Wales
Sends up the price of steamin’-coal and booms
the firewood trade;
And they skite about the profits, and they batten
on the sales, . ,
While splitters heedless devils at a starvin
rate are paid.
So the curly trees are failin’ and the olankin cross-
cuts S°> . , . , , ... ~
And human hearts are witherin’ where life all
comfort lacks;
For below the reach of pleasure, and too spiritless
for woe,
And surely God-forgotten, is the man that swings
the axe.
v.
BEN SUN.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Wa,or Hand.
In conseqiience of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside. N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of ljd. postage, the subscription
to Tiie Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —l \d. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
* NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tjie Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with pottage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have Tun Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.

to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.

Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
•whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To A mateurs in, lllaelc and White.
Anyone siipplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

after the arrival of each mail, from Messrs. Gordon and

SATURDAY, JUNE 15, 1901.
The Delusive Cornstalk Balance-sheet.
In New South Wales another financial year
ends on 30th June. Premier See is appeal-
ing to the country for a renewal of his lease
of office on the ground, among other grounds,
that it has been a good year—that the Corn-
stalk land has prospered during his occu-
pancy of the Premier’s billet, and the public
finances are solvent. Would-be Premier
Lee asks for the billet* on the ground that
the finances will be equally solvent in his
keeping. As to the prosperity and solvency
of the country. See and Lee are beautifully
unanimous. Neither the See party, nor the
Lee party, nor the third section of the
Assembly, which is the M‘Gowen party, gets
within cannon-range of the obvious, glaring
truth—which is, that the N.S. W. finances are
now the most disgraceful setof public accounts
in Australasia ; that they would be a dis-
credit to a fan tan establishment or the
table of a three-card spieler; and that the
ledgers of the worst bank which burst in the
post-boom days and let its promoters drop
through into a well-earned public building
didn’t compare at all badly with them in
point of honesty.
* * * *
At time of writing, eleven months revenue
and expenditure accounts (up to the end of
May) are available. The N.S.W. revenue
for those eleven months is £9,7(58,311 —an
increase of £521,148 as compared with the
eleven months to 31st May, 1900. That
looks well on the surface of things, and
every politician points to the figures with a
large and politically-dirty hand, and asks
how, in view of this glorious increase,
anyone can doubt the progress and pros-
perity of New South Wales. Yet even the
gorgeous revenue increase of £521,148 isn't
all that it seems. It doesn’t signify £521,148
worth of genuine expansion, by any means.
The principal causes of the movement are
these four :
(1) The Government has resumed a large part of Syd-
ney, and includes the rents, &c. (£148,735 up to now), in
its revenue. Yet these are really trust moneys ; they are
other people’s rents collected from other people’s pro-
perties. The Government hasn’t paid for the properties
yet; it hasn’t even paid the back interest on the monev
it owes for them ; nor has it charged itself with the back
interest.
(2) The Government got a great increase of revenue
through the rush of railway and tramway traffic at the
Commonwealth and ducal celebration periods. These
extra receipts were included in the revenue, but the out-
lay on decorations , &c.,which brought that extra traffic, is
not included among the expenditure.
(3) The railway receipts are swelled by the. building of
new lines with borrowed money. If new lines are
which bring in £lOO,OOO a year revenue, and
swallow up £150,000 a year in working expenses and in-
terest on cost, that is a loss of £50,000 a year—but all the
same it makes the revenue £lOO,OOO more. The unthink-
ing multitude is perfectly satisfied with the assurance
that there is an extra £lOO,OOO and that it is a proof of
the prosperity of the State. The fact that the extra
£lOO,OOO revenue may.be bought with £150,000 or £250,000
extra expenditure is the other side of the case—that side
that hardly anybody looks at.
(4) The whole revenue of N.S.W. is just now inflated,
bogus and artificial. N.S.W. is approaching the top of a
new borrowing boom. Its borrowings for the last twelve
months are close on £3,500,000, which isn’t much short of
the worst and maddest days of bygone profligacy. And
the reckless shovelling-out of loan money has inflated
every branch of trade, and created an increase of revenue
which has no relation whatever to real progress.
* • « *
While the N.S.W. public income for the
11 months has increased by £521,148 —in-
cluding £148,735 acquired by the barefaced
appropriation of other people’s rents without
any provision being made for paying these
people the interest due them, and while
N.S.W. has a reasonably legitimate revenue
increase of £372,413, expenditure has gone
up, as compared with the corresponding 11
months of last year, by £512,877. This is
the acknowledged expenditure, which, as
will presently appear, is very much less than
the real expenditure. Therefore, the in-
crease in expenditure is £140,464 more than
the legitimate, or reasonably legitimate, in-
crease in revenue. The alleged revenue is
£9,768,311, including the rents of other
people’s property, which the State holds in
trust. Without them it is £9,619,576. The
admitted expenditure is £9,786,581. There-
fore, on one basis, there is a deficit of
£18,270 ; on the other and true basis there
is a deficit of £167,005.
* * * * *
T’: j trouble is that there is £473,628
of expenditure—of money actually paid
away—which the See Government doesn't
put among its expenditure at all. These are
the items as they appear on page 4379 of
the N.S.W. Government Gazette, in
the statement of how the trust funds are
misapplied :
Debit Balances, Bank of New South Wales.
Imperial Contingent Bushmen’s Account £1,154 910
Imperial Contingent to South Africa,
No. 2 Account 63,799 12 6
N.S.W. Naval Contingent to China .. 36,326 14 6
N.S.W. Military Contingents to South
Africa 155,147 2 4
N.S.W. Permanent and Volunteer Mili-
tary and Naval Forces 39,293 4 9
Expenses in connection with the Plague 76,859 19 0
Federal Celebrations Account .. .. 92,281 11 5
Federal Expenditure Account .. .. 8,764 19 6
£473,627 13 9
These amounts have been already paid, and
there are others (including the ducal cele-
bration foolishness) to be paid. They haven’t
been charged to loans ; even the unspeak-
able See Government hardly dare call them
“ permanent and reproductive works.”
They haven’t been added to the stand-
ing deficit ; it would look bad to make
such a huge addition to the great accumulated
shortage. They certainly haven’t been
charged to ordinary expenditure, though the
corresponding receipts—the great increase
of railway and tram revenue, and the smaller
increase in customs revenue which arose
through the Federal celebrations, the ducal
celebrations, the show at the despatch of
contingents, &c., have been most religiously
included in the revenue. The items don’t
even appear to be all extra or unusual ex-
penses ; “N.S. W. Permanent and Volunteer
Military and Naval Forces ” seems to be
just ordinary Defence expenditure. The
only possible excuse that can be given
for keeping these matters in suspense,
and temporarily covering them up by
shameful misuse of the trust funds, is
that the accounts are not all in yet.
That is no excuse ; they can be charged to
revenue each month as they come in, if the
See Government wants to show exactly how
the public finances stand which, apparently,
it doesn’t)' There is no corresponding diffi-
culty about crediting partial payments on
the other side of the accounts. When these
items are put where they should be the
figures show up thus :
Revenue, including the money received from the re-
sumed area, which is really trust money at present, and
should be set aside to pay interest on the cost of pur-
chase, £9,768,311.
Revenue, not including that wholly unjustifiable item,
but with the accounts placed on an honest basis,
£9,619,576.
Deficit, if income from resumed area is included,
£491,898.
Deficit, income from resumed area not included,
£640,633.
*•* » *
Even this is only the outer fringe.of the
great N.S.W. public fraud. In N.S.W. (as
in all the other Australias) there is no limit
as to what can be charged to loans under the
impudent and lying designation of “Per-
manent and Reproductive Works.” And as
there hasn’t been a Treasurer in N.S.W.
for many years whose political honesty
was worth mentioning, the loan funds
are used to pay for road repairs, patches
on worn-out buildings, salaries of various
public servants, and all manner of items of
ordinary every-day expenditure. These are
called “Permanent and Reproductive
Works,” and are put, as such, in the loan
account because it saves the weary, per-
functory person in charge of the finances
from telling the people unpalatable truths,
and hustling round for new sources of
revenue, and making himself unpopular by
levying enough taxation to keep the State
solvent. These, according to State Statisti-
cian Coghlan, are the Cornstalk loan moneys
spent in recent years on works which were
not permanent nor reproductive, and which
were only called so by a mendacity that a
decent Ananias would be ashamed of—the
amounts charged to loans instead of, as they
should have been, to revenue, in order to
make bogus surpluses, or make deficits look
smaller than they were. In short, these are
the amounts of the great political lie, fraud,
and misappropriation of N.S.W. :
June, r iB96 .. £559,552 JuneTlB99 .. £833,491
„ 1897 .. 562,658 „ 1900 .. 879,282
„ 189 S .. 650,514 „ 1901 .. ?
As the total loan expenditure for the 11
months to May, 1901, is already £479,765
more than for the corresponding 11 months to
May, 1901, it will probably be found that
the misappropriation of loan funds this year
for purely revenue purposes, will make an
even million. That will bring John See’s
deficit, according to any fair and honest
computation, up to about £1,640,633.
* * * *
And this doesn't yet Exhaust the list of
mean and bankrupt dodges which make up
N.S.W. finance. There remains the great
Cash Basis swindle. In the old days
before that national calamity and public
enemy, George Reid, invented the Cash
Basis, the accounts belonging to the
financial year which remained unpaid at
the end of the financial year were debited to
that year. When the year closed the Trea-
surer said: “The State has received (say)
£9,000,000 during the year; it has paid
away (say) £8,700,000 ; it owes, for interest
on its debts up to this date, for salaries due
but unpaid, &c. (say), £350,000. There-
fore, the shortage is £50.000.” That is the
principle on which Britain and every other
solvent State keeps its accounts. It is
also the principle on which every bank and
public company keeps its accounts ; the
directors would speedily get into bank-
ruptcy, to say nothing of gaol, if they
kept them any other way. George Reid,
however, struck the great device of keeping
the books on a quite different principle,
which he called the Cash Basis. He simply
said : “ The State has received £9,000,000 ;
it has paid away £8,700,000 ; therefore, it
has £300,000 surplus. It owes £350,000, or
£700,000, or something, but we won’t men-
tion that. Hereafter debts will be treated
as non-existent.”
» * * *
Ever since the huge public lie of the Cash
Basis—so called because it isn’t a Cash
Basis—was introduced, the great apparent
object of every N.S.W. Treasurer has been
to leave as many as possible of this year’s
accounts over to next year—-the more that
are left over the larger the fraudulent sur-
plus. When his unpaid but due accounts
are added on, Premier See’s real deficit at
30bh June, 1901, promises favorably to be
well over £2,000,000. As far back as three
months ago, the difficulty of collecting
money at the N.S.W. Treasury began to be
a subject of conversation, as it always is
now about that season of the year, and
people know by this sign that the Cash
Basis surplus is again in preparation.
* * * *
That is how things are this year. Next
year N.S.W. commences to pay its Old-Age
Pensions, which means a demand on the
Treasury for about £350,000 or £400,000
per annum. There will be a counterbalanc-
ing economy, but the saving will be largely
in the matter of private charity. Probably
the extra demand on the Treasury will run
to £300,000 a year. Next year, also, N.S.W.
will have.to pay almost all of tvw years’ in-
terest on the purchase money of the resumed
area against practically nothing paid this
year. Next year the Government is pledged
to begin doing something to keep the Civil
Service Superannuation Fund from going
absolutely stone-broke, and consigning
many State pensioners (men who paid
for their own pensions, and are
only left in the lurch because the State
made unjustifiable uses of their money)
to the Benevolent Asylum. This probably
means another £lOO,OOO a year. It is simi-
larly pledged in the matter of the Police
Superannuation Fund. It also undertook,
in a vague soit of way, to increase the
annual payment in reduction of the deficit
from £150,000 to £300,000 or £400,000 a
year, which means another huge outlay. And
next year the interest bill will be fully
£lOO,OOO more than it was this year, by
reason of this year's furious borrowing.
Even if there are no wars nor plague nor
costly celebrations next year, the N S.W.
finances promise, by reason of all these extla
outgoings, to be vastly worse than they are
now. And their present condition is one of
blank insolvency, partly disguised by crude,
raw devices which are too thin to impose on
any educated lunatic who has lucid intervals.
* * * *
All the same, the four Sydney dailies rose
up at the end of last month to state the
satisfactory state of the finances was “ nmst
gratifying.” Three of them had to tell that
incredible fiction because it is their allege 1
Freetrade tariff which, by producing a toi.aliy
insufficient revenue, makes either huge de-
ficits or huge lying about the state of the
finances inevitable, and they weren’t going
to say that their Freetrade tariff was
an utter failure. The fourth had to
tell the same tale, because it is the
mouthpiece of the Lyne-Sbe gang of in-
capables, and it couldn’t admit that, under
any circumstances, things could g«> wrong
with its crowd in office. Since the fis
question first assumed an acute form in N.S.
Wales, the finances of than State —at one
time reasonably honest —have degenerated
into the worst poli'ical fake and fraud in all
Australasia. The Reid 'party bid for office
by reducing taxation till the revenue was
fully a million below the real expenditure ;
the Lyne-Ske party Rids for office by keeping
taxation down. Neither party dare tell the
truth about the Treasury, because each
one lives by professing that its policy
can keep the country more solvent on
less money than the other party s policy.
The people of N.S.W. have been taught
to have an insane pride in the fact
that theirs is the lowest-taxed State in Aus-
tralasia, and to regard the fact that they
don’t raise enough money to pay their way
as somehow showing that they are greater
and better and more glorious in every way
than the people of other States. The poli-
tician who shook up this feeling of bloated
superiority by telling them that there is
only 20s. in a £ in N.S.W. as anywhere
else, and that being the least-taxed people
they are also the people who have the big-
gest deficit, and the biggest financial fake,
and who are now making about the best
pace towards insolvency, would probably
have a bad time. So the See party and the
Lee party go on assuring N.S.W. that the
condition of the Treasury is most gratifying,
and even talk, now and then, about what is
the best thing to do with the “surplus.”
As for the Labor party it has no visible
financial policy or financial conscience at all,
but one dreadful member of it (Fkank
Cotton, to wit) lately circularised the press
demanding that the surplus (!!!) should be
applied towards a wholesale reduction of
N.S.W. railway freights and charges—which
was something that might almost be called
humor under the circumstances.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More about the Coast Hospital Scandal.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., writes on the above
subject:
In your summary of the Glynn case you have over-
looked what I consider its four worst features: (1) the
design of the Public Service Board to avoid any investi-
gation ; (2) the admission of that body that Glynn is now
being punished for having had the matter ventilated in
Parliament ; (3) the willingness of the head Of the
Department of Public Health (Dr. Ashburton Thompson)
that Kidman should get the contract for the ensuing
year ; and (4) the apparent efforts of the Public Service
Board to manipulate the whole of their investigations,
and report so as to whitewash and shield their nominee
and pet, Dr. Thompson.
For weeks after Glynn had made his appeal formulat-
ing his charges (subsequently proved to the hilt and
amplified) the Board absolutely ignored it, and when
Glynn in desperation asked me to take a hand, the chair
man calmly told me that as he (Glynn) was not then a
member of the Public Service (having been kicked out),
the Board “ could not take any cognisance of his appeal.”
It required three interviews and a lot of straight talk to
obtain even the abortive “ departmental inquiry ” by a
person named Brodie, who reported that “Glynn’s
charges emanated from personal animus towards his
superior officer Willman.”
On the second point as to why Glynn, after having
proved to the complete satisfaction of the Board that
he was dismissed only and solely for doing
his duty in condemning bad food" supplied by
the contractor, has not, during all these months, been re-
instated in the Service, the last report of the Board is
quite explicit (this is the report The Bulletin complains
of not having seen). It is not that Glynn is incompetent,
or unreliable, or dishonest, or intemperate. It is some-
thing much worse in the eyes of the Board than any or
all of these. “He has adopted an improper course in
endeavoring to obtain his re-appointment.” The said
“ improper ” course was Glynn’s action in dragging the
whole evil-smelling scandal before Parliament when jus-
tice could not be otherwise obtained. In the opinion of
the men who control our public service the supplying of
putrid fish, slimy vegetables, weevilly sago and rotten
eggs to sick and dying people is a venial offence, but
exposing these practices to the public is such an impro-
per course as to bar for ever the doors of our public
service to the sinner.
But to my mind the worst feature of the whole scandal,
as revealed by the investigation, is the fact that after the
shocking state of things referred to had been proved and
exposed, the head of the Department (Dr. Thompson) still
made no move towards cancelling the contract, and was
apparently willing that the Tender Board should give
Kidman the contract for the ensuing year ! This seems
hardly credible, but it is a fact. I found it necessary to
get the Premier (Lyne) to interfere over the head of Dr.
Thompson to prevent this being done.
The contracts for the Military Hospital and the Quar-
antine Station for the ensuing year were actually given
to Arthur Kidman without my knowledge, and, I believe,
without that of the Premier. In this connection I
would like to be just to the Government by say-
ing that, all through, I have found both the pre-
sent Premier and his predecessor willing to do all in
their power to clean up this business. Premier Lyne
acted promptly in squelching Kidman’S contract, and, to
my own knowledge, twice interviewed the chairman of
the Public Service Board to endeavor to get that body to
reinstate Glynn, who is now suffering for the mistake of
the Reid Government in placing the Board in a position
to defy Ministers and flout Parliament. My own com-
plaint against the Govt, is its failure up to the present to
keep the promise of the. late Premier, made me on the
floor of the House, to appoint a Royal Commission to in-
vestigate the whole matter, and its apparent willingness
to accept as final, the weak and inconclusive verdict of the
Board, and to acquiesce in the efforts of that body to
shield the Head of the Dept, of Public Health by throwing
overboard one or two of his subordinates.

The Marine Sweater.
Big-Hat O’Malley lias given notice in the House
of Representatives of a motion that it is desirable
toprohibitsubsidised foreign steamship companies,
manned by either white or colored labor, from
carrying passengers or goods between Common-
wealth ports, and that inter-state navigation
should be regulated. There is common sense in-
side O’Malley’s hat, but his resolution does not
go far enough. What is wanted is :
1. The prohibition of all vessels owned by.white States
yet manned by colored crews, trading to-Australian ports’.
2. The prohibition of all save Australian vessels trading
between Australian ports—that is, 'carrying passengers
and cargo from one Australian port to another. ' °
Until this is done the Australian shipowner can
never be brought under the operation of a mini-
mum-wage law, nor can he be compelled to limit
the hours of his seamen and firemen and other
hands to 48 or GO, or any reasonable number per
week. He would simply be killed off by low
wage and long hour competition from outside
Canada has long since had laws which prohibit
Yankee or any other outside steamers carrying
goods or passengers .between ports of the
Dominion, and the consequence is that Yankee
ships are only employed in the trade between
American and Dominion ports. The Canadian

coastal trade is all in the bandn of Canadians, and
the XJ.S. shipowners are powerless to interfere in
a business that pays good dividends and wages to
Canadian capitalists and workers. The United
States has just as drastic laws, and it was only
the other day that the application of them
to the Hawaiian Islands knocked the Maoriland
Union line out of the Sydney-Honolulu-Frisco
service, which is now carried on by an all-Ameri-
can co. The Yankee line takes hundreds of pas-
sengers between Maoriland and Sydney, and the
big German, French, P. and 0., and Orient liners
are always crowded with passengers from Sydney
to Melbourne, Adelaide, and Westralia. Under
proper navigation laws all that traffic would be
conserved to Australian owners, and then a
minimum wage and limited hours of labor could
be enforced, and a fair share of the profits would
be paid out to Australian workers to make times
better generally. Of course, this idea would be
fatal to Freetrade doctrines—but then everything
that is much good to the workers is fatal to Free-
trade doctrines.

Something 1 New: An Honest Politician.
Hume Cook, M.H.R. for Bourke (Vic.) :
Are we going to continue a policy of borrowing money
and spending it upon public works, or are we for the
most part to attempt to carry on public works out of
revenue ? I feel it is not only fiscal protection we re-
quire in order to develop Australia. We require financial
protection also. We require protection from those who
would insist upon very large borrowings and spendings
without any regard 'to the future, or to the safe and
sound development of Australia. ... I hope, therefore,
that we shall not follow the old policy of borrow, boom,
and burst, and that we shall pay for our public works
from revenue, and, rather than add to our already very-
large national debt, try to get rid of some of our present
debts.
The Bulletin is pleased to find that there*js at
least one man in the House of Representatives
who agrees with it on this point. This
paper has been called a pessimist, but so far
from that it is almost the only really hopeful
publication in Australia. While the great mass
of papers and politicians have held that the Aus-
tralian is by nature a poor, miserable, inferior
animal, who can’t be expected to tax himself
sufficiently to pay his way and do something
towards wiping off his debts out of revenue, like
the Englishman and American and Dane and
Dutchman, The Bulletin has been a voice
crying in the wilderness to say that the
Australian is as good as any of them, if he
could only learn to hang most of his editors and
politicians, and think for himself. Scared Parlia-
mentarians and weak-kneed newspapers have per-
sistently preached the doctrine of reducing taxa-
tion,- and borrowing, and letting things slide, till
the Australian has almost come to believe that he
is a naturally slack, shiftless, incompetent indivi-
dual who must be always crying at the door of the
money-lender for aid. The Bulletin, on the other
hand, has always maintained that he is as strong,
and as capable of being honest and solvent, as any-
body else, if he only knew it. It is glad to find
another optionist of the same way of thinking.
Hume Cook is an oasis of hope in a desert of dis-
pirited politicians.

Still Another Fiscal Fake.
Sydney Telegraph, the other day, published
this table to show how Foreign-trade helps to
boom population :
Now from April, 1891, when the last census was
taken, to 2nd December, 1891, N.S.W. was under
the Parkes semi-Protective tariff, which pro-
tected about half the industries of N.S.W. From
2nd December, 1891, to Ist January, 1896, it was
under the Dibbs Protective tariff, which protected
practically everything. From Ist January, 1896,
it was under the Reid tariff, which protected
sugar, treacle, molasses, beer, wine, tobacco,
cigars, cigarettes, newspapers, biscuits, confec-
tionery, &c. So N.S.W. was Protectionist for
nearly half of the 10 years in which its increase of
population is calmly set down by the shoddy
penny fake as the blessed result of Freetrade,
and semi - Protectionist the rest of th
time. And, according to Coghlan’s estimate
(N.S.W. Statistical Register for 1899, page 614)
the biggest part of the population increase was in
the first half of the ten years—the more Pro-
tectionist half. If N.S.W. was, as the Telegraph
here alleges, a Freetrade State all through the
ten years from 1891 to 1901, what kind of a brassy
lie is the Telegraph telling when it alleges that
George Reid abolished the curse of Protection in
1896? Again, after holding up Tasmania and
Westralia for years to public execration as States
that groaned under the horrors of Protection —
after Gsorge Reid’s denunciation of all the States
apart from N.S.W. as the Five Fiscal Drunkards
—the Telegraph cheerfully ascribes the progress
of these States to their alleged possession of a
revenue tariff—not a protective one. And
Queensland, as it proves to have a larger per-
centage of increase than N.S.W., appears suddenly
as a “moderately protected country,” though
both it and S.A. beat Victoria hollow in the way
of heavy tariffs, and Tasmania —“ revenue tariff ”
Tasmania —has the heaviest duties of the lot.
The Sydney Telegraph has raised inaccuracy to
the" dignity of a fine art when it gets on tariff
questions.
A Wages-Board Difficulty.
The bosses of the Victorian woollen industry have
apparently banded themselves together to fight
the Minimum Wage Law. The wages in .each
trade that is brought under the Act are fixed by a
Board consisting of an equal number of represen-
tatives of employers and employes, with a Judge
who holds the casting vote. When the Wages
Board was appointed to deal with the woollen
industry, the employers’ representatives com-
plained that two of the members selected by the
employes were not bona fide workers in the
industry, and emphasised their protest by
retiring” in a body. The two members com-
plained of resigned, and the employes removed
all cause of complaint by replacing them with
others about whose bona fides as genuine toilers
in the woollen business there was no doubt, what
ever. But though the ostensible grievance-was
removed the employers’ representatives didn t
comeback ; their unanimous absence continues to
this dav Also, the employers refuse to elect new
representatives. The Act, of course, fully provides
for such oases as this. When the Turner Gov-
ernment drafted its Minimum Wage scheme, it
recognised that if employers could prevent the
formation of a Wages Board in any trade by.
simply refusing to elect representatives there
would never be any Board at all, and the Act
might as well be used as a pipe-light. oo it
was enacted that when either party declined
to be represented the Government might
appoint representatives to act for it. If actual
employers or employes would not act, their places
could be filled by the appointment of men quite
outside the trade —by anybody, in fact. Tho only
question now is whether the Peacock Govern-
ment will exercise this power so promptly as to
prevent any more conspiracies to evade the law.
If it doesn’t exercise its power, then the local Fat
Man will have found a way to dodge the hated
statute, and the Minimum Wage Act will perish
by the wayside, in a manner of speaking. If it
hangs fire over the matter, and only makes
the appointments after months of waiting, then
the same individual' will at least know that
he can hamper and delay the operation of the Act
with impunity. The action of the woollen-bosses
is really a conspiracy against the law, and when
less affluent individuals conspire in like fashion
they sometimes get into gaol. It isn’t suggested
even for a moment that the woollen-bosses should
be put in gaol; the public fully recognises that all
the Victorian gaol accommodation is wanted for
the sick poor, that they may do their six months
hard labor by order of the Carlton J.P. But still
there is room for Premier Peacock to show a cer-
tain amount of energy, and the country is waiting
to hear Alexander laugh the loud confident
shriek which signifies that he is about to fall to.
“SubJudice.”
N.S.W. Civil Service is at present a world of
strange and fearful moral odours. One particu-
larly aggressive sample, arising from Sydney
Technical College, this paper was moved to in-
vestigate, and called at the .Education Dept, for
that purpose. Under-Secretary Maynard, Per-
manent Head of the Department, said there was
no information to give. The Public Service Board
was holding an inquiry, and had not yet furnished
a report, so, for the time being, the matter was
out of the hands of that (the .Education) Dept.
The Public Service Board being only just across
the street, the investigator walked over, and about
four hours later managed to see a member of the
Board. He stated that the Board had concluded
its inquiry some considerable time ago, and
that all the papers, evidence, and report were
now in the hands of the Minister for Educa-
tion, and had been for about a fortnight. Asked
for particulars, the Board-man looked at the
investigator, with a pained expression such as an
archangel might cast upon a william-goat m the
Holy of Holies, and said, severely, “Its sub
judice! ” He could not help the fact that matters
were kept sub judice for years. He merely stated
the matter was sub judice, and if the investigator
wished to make any remarks thereon he was wel-
come to. He thought that the information that
the matter was sub judice should be sufficient
answer for anyone. This paper proceeds to make
four remarks accordingly:
(1) The sub judice plea as a means of choking off all
inquiry, upon all subjects, for all time or thereabouts, is
unspeakable impudence and presumption.
(2) The Civil Service bosses, in putting it forward,
claim rights and privileges unknown even to the Supreme
Court. The Supreme Court allows the publication of a
fair and impartial statement of any case while it is still
sub judice. .... .
(3) The Civil Service bosses keep things subjudice for
years (the Coast Hospital scandal has been sub judice
since January, 1900, and is still there, and looks as if it
might remain there ten years or more unless Press and
Parliament hurry it along). They claim that no paper
should allude to" a matter until it has ceased to be sub
judice. If the papers don’t allude to it the matter often
remains subjudice until it. is forgotten, and the people in-
terested are dead. At other times it seems to sort of
perish on the official premises, and if an inquiry never
comes to a formal end, the matter never really ceases to
be sub judice, and therefore no paper ought to allude to
it for ever and ever, amen.
(4) And when a matter ceases to be sub judice, and
emerges from the secret tribunal, and comment therefore
becomes allowable, the case will be closed, and as a man
can’t be tried twice for the same offence, it will be impos-
sible to reopen it.
The sub judice gag is really a daring and impudent
attempt to reach out for irresponsibility. Every
man in the Civil Service is a public employe and
any taxpayer is his employer. Imagine the
managing clerk of any private company, when his
emplovers wish to inquire into his or his subor-
dinates’ alleged misconduct, calmly telling them
they must not ask for any information, or expect
to know anything about it, as he is inquiring into
the matter and it is subjudice!

The Black Streak in Cornstalk Land.
A CORRESPONDENT of a Clarence River paper
thus describes the black blot which is spreading
in New South Wales :
When you arrive at the summit of the range Cudgeon
is reached, the home of the Kanakas. At this centre
there are 280 Kanakas stationed, and this black blot on
the border line gives a New South Welshman some vague,
idea of the black curse that exists so flagrantly in the
land of bananas and pineapples. There is no Sunday at
Cudgeon. One police oflieer is stationed there, and he
has as much show of maintaining order amongst the unruly
Kanakas when they seek to create a colossal disturbance
as he alone would have to maintain order in any of our
metropolitan cities. Kanakas (for that term refers to
every thing black or colored amongst the ebon tribe) are
employed on Robb and Co.’s sugar plantation at the
enormous (?) salary of £l2 per year. They live under the
most demoralising conditions. Their huts, generally
speaking, are filthv-looking, and in the doorway may be
seen a specimen of the white fair sex, while perhaps half-
a-dozen youngsters of various tints are scrambling about
the yard dressed in foul rags. Then you will see a
woman tumble out of one of tnese alleged-homes with a
dirty billy-can, making her way to the pub “ across
the road ” to replenish the vessel with the fluid
that has caused her surroundings and the hope-
less position that she has fallen into. Presently
a huge nigger as black as the ace of spades
may he seen belting his' wife (?) with a piece of wood,
while the youngsters scatter in all directions, and seek
protection in the by-ways and hedges of Cudgeon Truly,
the sights to be seen at Cudgeon are sufficient to make
one’s blood run cold, and yet we find such journals as
the Brisbane Courier fighting for the retention of
Kanaka labor in the northern State. Fighting for the re-
tention of slavery, for the Kanaka’s body and soul are
bought in Queensland for a period of three years for the
sum of £lB. Are there any abolitionists in Australia ?
If there are, here is a field for them. If Federation is
going to deal with the black labor curse the people of
Australia should hail the new era with delight. The Bris-
bane Courier, a few weeks back, published figures trying
to prove that the Kanaka was dying out, and that the
undertaker was solving the black labor question at the
rate of two a week. But what about the rising population
of semi-Kanakas that pass as Europeans and get entered
on the official records as Europeans? On the Tweed
River there is a horde of such a breed, so near white that
to all appearances they would pass as Europeans. These
individuals, possessing the white man’s cunning, and the
savage’s savagery, will develop into a race most unwel-
come amongst a civilised people if urgent and stringent
methods are not adopted to drive the Kanaka off the face
of this continent.

The attitude of the Federal Government on the
gratis Newspaper Postage question and other
matters, as defined by P.M.G. Drake, in intro-
ducing the Post and Telegraph Bill :
Seriator Drake replied that, roughly speaking, revenue
was £2,360,000, and expenditure £2,479,000. It would be
necessary, if the Postal Depaitmenc was to be placed on
a sound basis, that all services performed by it
should be paid for. At present, these were done in a hap-
hazard way, the Department rarely coming off best. There
were three causes militating against the success of the
Postal Departments in the past. One . . . was the free
carriage of newspapers. In three States they were carried
free, with the result that the Departments conveyed an
enormous quantity of m lil m i.tter and received no return.
Those who wanted newspapers carried free said either
that they were of highly educational value or were very
valuable in the work of administering the country. He
was prepared to admit both arguments to a certain extent.
But in neither case were they justified in making a
charge for those advantages upon the Postal Department.
If the newspapers had a great educational influence,
they should be carried by the Education Department. . . .
In order not to act unfairly towards some States during
the five years following the introduction of the tariff, the
department would be careful not to make any changes in
the rates for that period. In Victoria recently a bill was
passed instituting, the penny' postage, and some people
thought it was a piece of sharp practice. Well, for five
years at least, Victoria would have to stand whatever loss
was incurred by the change. . . . Particular attention
will be paid to the matter of free letters and telegrams
with a view to reducing the evil to a minimum.
The Post and Telegraph Bill doesn’t fix post and
telegraph rates ; it merely gives the Gov.-General
power to fix them. And Drake’s explanation
appears to amount to about this : The Federal
Post Office is losing money through carrying
newspapers free in three States—and it will prob-
ably go on doing so for at least five years. There
is chaos and more loss by reason of different
postal rates and charges in the different
States, and' a uniform system won’t be
introduced for at least five years. And
the Catfish Budget and similar productions
can still pass as newspapers—and presumably get
through the post free—provided they come out
weekly and make a few other trifling alterations
to comply with the new definition of a newspaper.
This is hardly what Australia expected. It
federated its postal departments to get rid of all
the costly and chaotic divergent rates and
systems; and now it strikes on a Government
which is seriously thinking of going to a lot of
expense and trouble to preserve the old anomalies
and muddles for five years or possibly more.

Freetrader Brown, of Canoblas (N.S.W.) said
a shocking thing the other day in the House of
Representatives. Brown is supposed to represent
Labor in some sense. He is also a Foreign-trader
of the most violent description, and he professes
to regard Protection as a thing which lays heavy
burdens on the people, and to want to lift these
burdens off the people. Yet he said THIS :
He had never been able to bring himself to a belief in
the protectionist theory, and until he did he would never
support it for any man or for any purpose. As a farmer,
he said that when protection was sneaked in in New
South Wales the price of wheat was never lower than
then, either before or since.
Exactly ! The State gained much extra revenue
under the Dibbs tariff, and the people got their
food cheaper. Coghlan has already shown that.
On pages 523-527 of his “ Wealth and Progress of
N.S.W.” for 1898-1899 he gives the prices of
almost all the necessaries of life in N.S. W. over a
long series of years. The list includes only eight
on which Dibbs put Protective duties, and they
all became cheaper. Then Reid came into
power, and took the duties off these
eight articles, and off three more on which
duties had existed long before Dibbs’s time ; and
out of eleven articles the price of one became less;
four articles became dearer; the other six re-
mained as they were. And among the lying
shrieks of the other Foreign-traders that Protec-
tion makes things dear, there arises the unsophi-
sticated voice of Freetrader Brown, denouncing
Protection because, under it, he didn’t get the
same chance to plunder the consumer as he had
in the good Freetrade days. Now, what is the
difference between BroITN and any other alleged
plunderer of the people—say the Golonial Sugar
Go. ?

Concerning the recent terrible Broken Hill
accident: —
When the average jury doesn’t find that the victim of a
mining catastrophe was “ accidentally killed ” it generally
fastens a tag of reproach on to the dead man—the only
one who cannot answer back. But the jury which sat on
the six victims of the ore-fall in the Broken Hill South,
besides blaming the practical miners of the party, blamed
also the mining manager and the Government mining
inspector for neglecting to see that all precautions were
taken. And the finding was thoroughly right—anyway,
so far as the men and the manager were concerned ; as
to the inspector, his position and powers are doubtful,
thanks to the mining regulations, which have been
repeatedly pronounced ultra vires. The men were work-
ing in what was really an underground quarry, which, in
course of time, would be filled in with timber and
“ mullock.” They were 12ft. to 15ft. or more from any
timber. That is, the men, as they broke down the ore
from the face (or other end of the working), mounted
on the loose stuff and worked on top of it until, when it
was removed, floor and roof (or “ back ”) were from 16ft.
to 20ft. apart. Now, the best—in fact, the only— way to
tell whether a roof is safe is to sound it. (f it rings solid,
things are all right; if it doesn’t, it must be propped, and
watched carefully. It is, therefore, never safe to have
the roof beyond'reach. But not only was this roof so
high and out of reach ; it was also not at all solid
ground. A big fall had occurred near there two
years before, and breaks and cracks and strains had
resulted. That it was treacherous was moreover
repeatedly indicated by frequent falls from the roof-
falls of small lumps, yet large enough to show that a
movement of some sort was in progress, most likely due
to atmospheric action. Further, the men received,
instead of the ordinary 9s. a day, 10s.—presumably
because it was a “ticklish” place. And notwithstanding
that the roof was so high that it could not be conveni-
ently sounded ; notwithstanding that it was in disturbed
ground ; notwithstanding the small falls ; notwithstand-
ing the extra shilling a shift; notwithstanding that
timber was kept so far back that if there was a fall the
men had only a faint hope of escaping, the manager had
not made an inspection of the roof for “ about a week,”
and the mining inspector, though he knew the place, had
not been in it for 10 days, and even then had not tried the
roof himself, though he regarded it as dangerous 1 The
verdict might have been even stronger, without
doing anyone any injustice. But what now? The men
took the extra risk for a shilling a day—presumably, that
is what it was for. They misjudged ; and it cost them
their lives. The management took risks, too ; it did not
have more timbers put in and the ore worked differently
because it, no doubt, believed that the stuff could be
worked safely and with the biggest profit this way. It
misjudged too. The men paid their lives. What is it to
cost the others ? Or do they escape ?

J une 1 did not see the long-deferred ‘ ‘ re-grading ”
of N.S.W. Public Service. The Public Service
Board, which some years ago stopped all annual
increments on salaries over £3OO, and proposed
instead to increase the screws of deserving civil
servants, receiving less than £3OO, by £lO a year,
saves six months’ money by thus delaying the
publication of the Gazette. The last increases
were due on Ist July, 1900, and Parliament
voted £14,000 for the current financial year. But
the P.S. Act provides that every five years the
whole of the Service is to be “re-graded,” which
gave the Board an opportunity to dodge granting
any increments on July 1, because the re-grading
was due. Having completed this general re-classi-
fication, the Board has announced that, except in
special cases, the new increases will only date
trom Ist Jan., 1901. The Board has cut it pretty
fine. The financial year has only two weeks or so
to run, and if the £14,000 voted is not withdrawn
from the Treasury by then the whole of it lapses,
and has to be voted over again by the Legislature.
In that case, the See Government’s finances
for this year will look £14,000 better than
they are, and they badly want to look better
than they are. A pretty row in Parliament may
be looked for. The civil servants reckon it a low-
down trick to wait until the very last minute ere
notifying the increments, and a particularly
scurvy one to knock off six months of the in-
creases and then to delay granting the other six
months’ increases.

Persistently rumoured that Seddon intends a
big stroke in the direction of assuming direct
State control of the making and sale of liquor in
Maoriland. The Temperance Party’s constant
tinkering with the liquor law has brought things,
by accident, to a state which it certainly never
contemplated, and which has thrown enormous
and monopolistic powers into the hands of the big
brewers. Such a position of things can’t last,
especially with a man like Seddon at the head of
affairs. When the subject of licenses in the King
Country—-the annual blister of M.L. Parliament —
came up last year, he distinctly stated that,
if the sale of liquor was legalised there the
control would be kept in the hands of the
State. The satisfaction thereupon expressed
by everyone except the brewers and the extreme
Prohibitionists has probably emboldened him (if
that were needed) to tackle the bigger question of
State control throughout the province. Richard
Seddon has done much to deserve the gratitude
of M.L. ; but if, after seeing the Duke round, he
takes off his gloves and puts his bell-topper away
in the pantry, and sees this question through, a
statue of Seddon facing the Bung ought to be
erected in every town in Maoriland.
♦
Brisbane Courier shed special tears of ink over
the escape of Edward VII when the Shamrock II
met with her disaster, and was of opinion that the
incident showed the royal family to be specially
watched over by Providence. The average
mechanical pietist is a strange being. If
Providence specially interfered so that the
Shamrock’s top-hamper just missed the monarch,
it would have been equally easy to prevent it fall-
ing at all. According to the cheap orthodox
moraliser, when Providence has an especial care
of anybody, it shows its interest in him by just
missing his head with heavy substances, as an
ordinary citizen might show his regard for his
best friend by occasionally firing a cannon at him
so that the ball would almost hit him, but not
quite. The Courier was doubtless quite serious
in taking the view it did ; yet, if anyone sug-
gested that Christian de Wet has been escaping
bullets for nearly two years, and that that was
still better evidence that Providence was
watching over him, the proposition would
doubtless have been set down as flat blasphemy.
Then the theory that the man on whom the heavy
object nearly falls, but doesn’t, is specially
esteemed by Providence, carries with it the im-
plication that the man on whom the object does
fall is specially objected to by Providence ;
which is rough on Nelson and other eminent
characters. And though the man that the falling
mast misses by two feet is alleged, by reason of
that circumstance, to be specially and unmistak-
ably under Divine protection, nothing is ever said
about the still more conspicuous Divine protec-
tion extended to the man whom it misses by two
miles, or 2000 miles. Altogether it is a tangled
subject, and a person better endowed than the
Courier with the gift of well-chosen silence or
carefully-selected absence would have kept clear
of it.
The N.S.W. Civil Service and Police Super-
annuation Funds being practically stone-broke,
Government has three alternatives before it: —(1)
To put up enough money in a lump (it would pro-
bably be borrowed money) to make the funds sol-
vent ; (2) To make annual payments out of revenue
to the fund till it is solvent; (3) To let the funds run
out and then pay the pensions out of revenue, as
is done in Victoria. A deputation asked State
Premier See about this matter last week, and got
this beautifully hazy reply
Mr. See said that this question was dealt with in the
Government manifesto. It was imperative that the fund
should at once be put in a condition of solvency. The
Government intended to deal with the matter. He
thought that the Government should guarantee what might
be termed the original intention of the Act. But he did
not go so far as to say that the Government should contri-
bute from three millions to four millions to rehabilitate
the fund. The proper guarantee of the Government was,
however, as good as the money, and in every case he
thought it was right that the demands should be paid.
Where a public servant faithfully and honestly performed
his contract, then the Government should carry out its
contract. The Government could not afford to repudiate
this debt. As far as he could he would do what was right
in the matter. One of the first things the Government
would do would be to endeavor to put the fund on a
satisfactory basis.
Good old basis !
“ A lawyers’ case,” was what the N.S.W.
Supreme Court recently called the malodorous
Heydon business. But all cases are “lawyers’
cases,” and the Court takes care of the Devil’s
Own. A man can’t even sue in formd pauperis
without feeing a lawyer. He must have a Judge’s
order to begin with, and no Judge will look at
his case without a barrister’s certificate ; while no
barrister, again, dares look at the case unless it is
sent to him by an attorney. Rules of court for-
bid. The cream of the joke is that the applicant
for leave to sue in form l pauperis has to declare
that he “is not worth £5 in the world.’
Yet out of the £5 he hasn’t got he has to spend
about £6los. in stating that he hasn’t got it! High
time that some of our legislators tackled the job
of giving the people of Australia something like
cheap justice. The question of cheap law is one
that can wait. As long as the country gets cheap
justice, it doesn’t really matter how much law
costs. No sane individual wants law while he
can get the other thing.
An increasing evil in the N.S.W. Public School
system is the exemption certificate abuse. A few
years ago this was unknown, but of late it seems to
have been encouraged by the department for the
purpose of relieving the pressure on the crowded
schools. The Act provides for sending children
to school until fourteen, but a smart child of ten
can often pass the examination which gives
exemption. Then he is taken away and in six
months has probably forgotten most of what he
knew. The two or three years before fourteen are
the most important of all—-not so much for the
additional education received as for the fact that
what has been already received is stamped firmly
on the brain. At the age many boys receive their
certificates their minds are like wet plaster from
which impressions may be effaced as easily as they
are given. If our educational system is to do good
the stamp must be kept on till the plaster has set.
Apropos of the O’Sullivan’s proposal to
place an amount on N.S.W. Estimates for the
purpose of producing rain by guns. Would it be
possible to induce him also to subsidise Astrology
and Alchemy, in orderto revive those lost sciences?
And, with a view to giving the neglected art of
witchcraft a lift, will he put a sum on the Esti
mates to set up the profession with a cauldron and
a blasted heath ?

\ Jean Gerardy (Sat. matinee).
The “Milestones of Life,” which might
almost have been perpetrated by Parson
Walters, was staged at Sydney Criterion on
Saturday night, and, like most moral
dramas, it propounds views of life which a
respectable pagan would scout with loath-
ing. The first act begins with two young work-
men enjoying a mutual wedding breakfast in peace
and amity, except that one is distinctly unsober,
and the other is given to talking in long, virtuous
speeches, with well-turned phrases and faultless
grammar. To them enters their employer, who
scolds the unsober workman in his own house for
his condition, and gives thegood workman consider-
able gas, and also £2O, which that person accepts
in a lowly and reverent manner. The boss then
goes away from the party which he has insulted
all round, and nobody kicks him, and nobody
talks of kicking him. The good workman
continues getting on in life till he becomes
Sir Somebody Something, the moral being that
every working man can do this if he keeps sober,
and is lowly and reverent towards his betters.
Meanwhile, the bad workman refrains from sup-
porting his wife till she takes to someone else who
seems more disposed to keep her in shoes and
corned beef; whereupon he murders her in a
spasm of virtue, and in the presence of her child.
For this act of incivility he receives a commuted
sentence of II years or so. When released he
is welcomed by the daughter who saw
him murder her mother.' She seems quite pleased
to meet him, and embraces him affectionately.
That kind of daughter is rather inexplicable, and
in real life would be considered objectionable.
* 9 * m
Miss Helen Burdette does well as the
erring wife who errs for reasons that seem chiefly
financial, and which are aggravated by her husband’s
tendency to wreck the windows of her humble
home and make havoc with the furniture. Miss
Roland Watts-Phillips is a bright star as a mother
with beer and piety strongly developed, while
C. R. Stanford manages the moral maxims that
fall to his lot with as much success as can be ex-
pected. He is the ideal workman who keeps his
hands white though working twice as hard as his
neighbors, and he manipulates the humble pick and
shovel with a superior air, like one who is digging
a drain in three dead languages.

To-morrow (Friday) night, grand onera
will cease at Sydney Royal, and depart for Bris-
bane per Musgrove specials on Saturday. Some
three weeks will be spent in the Land of the
Chow, .and, after that, no man, except George
Musgrove, knows. While it lasted, the grand
opera was a show where one might spend a few
hours in the ferocious old world, and it made one
feel glad to get back to the dull, prosaic 20th cen-
tury. This era may be commonplace, but it is a
lot more safe than the world of grand opera.
There is not much music nowadays, perhaps, but
it must have taken a lot of the charm off the
melody when it was a warbled intimation that
one’s dearest enemy had got one at his mercy, and
his mercy consisted mostly of slow fire. Com-
pared to that, even a sudden prose intimation
that the boss's younger brother will take up your
billet next week is a consoling thing.

For the last fortnight or so operas chased
one another so swiftly across the stage of the
Royal that it was quite impossible tq keep pace
with them. On Saturday night last a crowded
house listened to the last rendering of “ II Trova-
tore,” the great king opera of them all. The even
smoothness with which it went was a credit to
all concerned. Agnes Janson’s impersonation of
the vengeance-maddened old gypsy (how much
opera in general owes to the gypsy !) woman was
the gem of the evening. Madame Slap was in
splendid voice, but her Leonora had not the
Solished finish of Madame Jansbn’s Azucena.
'lie wonders how Count Eugenie Di Luna could
have had the heart to slay a man with, a voice like
Manrico Salvi. But this is one of the unpleasant
features of grand opera ; no matter how beautifully
he sings, the tenor generally comes to a violent
end.
