For some ten nights or so, from Saturday
next, a Musgrovian company will occupy the
Royal with the latest English comedy success, a
piece entitled “Nurse,” with Miss Jennie May-
nard as leading lady. Leading gent is Mr.
Henry Ludlow. After that it is generally
understood that the Brough Co. will again walk
abroad.
*m m m
At Sydney Palace Hawtrey does a daring
thing by prefacing a comedy written by a woman
with a curtain-raiser created by a man. However,
the man suffers in comparison. The opening of
the “Highland Legacy” is a dreary, lengthy
monologue, jagged with sudden exclamations of
“ What’s that.” However, when Tammy Tamson,
the best girl, and the gentleman made respectable
by Act of Parliament appear, things brighten up
considerably; and Hawtrey in kilts, and Percy
Walshe in rags and a dilapidated silk hat, save the
situation.
When the odour of sentimentality left by
the “ Legacy ” has sufficiently dissolved, the cur-
tam rises on the seaside parlor where Tom, Dick
Rn( ' Harry get so fearfully and wonderfully mixed
up. I here is a sort of resemblance between T.,
1 . and 11. and a set of Lancers. Every man hugs
the other fellow’s girl, and every girl wastes a
wealth of kisses on the wrong man. The great
charm of the comedy is that it doesn’t contain any
moral, or try to teach anything except the ex-
pediency of making sure before you hug. It
merely consists of three red-headed men who
get into the same hotel and lunatic asylum,
anti are ultimately disentangled through being
seen simultaneously in the same room,
when each man is claimed by his own
proper best girl. As the peppery old general,
whose indigestion and temper are the causes
of all the trouble, Hawtrey is satisfactory. As
the son of his father, disguised in the red beard
and hair of the husband of a strange wife,
Herbert Ross is excellent; while the other two
red-haired men, Heggie and Lytton, can divide
things equally between them. Among the ladies,
Miss Ruby Ray, as the nice red-haired com-
panion, and Roxy Barton as the girl with the
eternal giggle, are hereby invitod to look upon
this scribe as a brother.

Of the alteration of “ Florodora ” there is
no end. In a few weeks, at the present rate, Wil-
liamson will be able to take the opera back to
Melbourne, call it by another name (say “ Lauri-
dora ”), and score another gigantic success with
his new and up-to-date creation, produced for
the first time in Australia.

*m m m
“ The Night Birds of London,” it is
rumored, nearly went off Sydney Lyceum boards
the other week, but it was decided at a late hour
that the drama that was to have replaced it was
too mild, so the maligned social fowl still has his
address at the old perch. According to present
reports the Birds will endure for a few weeks
longer, likewise the potato-stall and the unreal
baby in the pram, and the two noble-hearted, small
children who escape the gas explosion by walking
down the house with the convenient steps cut into
its walls.
mm # m
Snazelle’s “Our Navy,” at Queen’s Hall,
Sydney, though damaged by the irruption of
Royalty and some amount of funeral, is still well
worth patronising, owing principally to the large
green waves that tumble from the top of the sheet
and then swirl upward from the foot. The cine-
matographed Boer war is necessarily viewed with
doubt, as the man who waved the British Flag on
the veldt could be taken just as effectively and
more safely and cheaply and conveniently waving
it in a field outside London ; but Snazelle’s large
green waves are above suspicion. Nothing will
persuade a peaceful harbour or a jug of hot water
to look like that. You can’t forge waves.
mm m #
There are some new things and things
almost new at Sydney Tivoli this week. Little
boy Eric is one item that seems likely to have the
effect of novelty, no matter how long his restless
little personality skips up and down the Tivoli
stage. Adelina Sarina, tne aerial gymnast, is in
her last week, and soon her shapely limbs and
magnificent arms will cease from ornamenting.
Frank and Jen Latona take part in a sketch,
wherein Jen, as a slavey, takes pills that will
give her an instantaneous knowledge of the piano,
said pills being certainly efficacious. She also
sings in a voice which may be said to suit her
kitchen occupation, inasmuch as it has an excellent
range. The polyphone does not strike this writer
as being very different from the now common and
old-fashioned phonograph. The first part of the
Rickards’ show still contains many unnecessary
items. Eva Lee continues with her “Plain Little
Everyday Girl, ” and if she does not brighten up
will presently be taken for the heroine of her own
ditty. ,
mm m m
The Melbourne dailies, as was expected
after their exuberant praise of Musgrove’s com-
pany, were quite severely critical in their first
notices of Williamson’s Italian crowd. For in-
stance, the Age, complaining of one artist in par-
ticular, said, “ Whatever may be the case in Italy,
in Melbourne the tremolo is not appreciated,” and
spoke of the “simply irritating” effect of a
“chain of shakes.” How does this compare with
the same paper’s notices of Louis Arens, whose
Wagnerian utterances shook like a jelly all the
time ? And how about the trembling basso of
Charles Tilbury (an Age idol) as compared with
the strong, straightforward singing of Travaglini ?
# ♦ ♦ *
A droll example of misguided criticism in
the same journal was the reference to the alleged
unnatural fairness of the priina-donna’s face com-
plexion : “ If Adelina Patti could afford to appear
with her face a rich brown, so assuredly can this
present Aida, and the spectacle of the jet-black
father'of an almost white-skinned daughter would
not then be presented.” But the world has gained
a knowledge of ancient Egyptian complexions
since Patti played the Ethiopian princess. Rev.
Haskett Smith, *in his illustrated lecture on
the Memphis revelations, shows a picture
of an Egyptian military commander and his
wife, which persons had been mausoleumed for
about 6500 years before the excavations at
Memphis Cemetery brought them within reach of
the camera. These interesting figures, seated side
by side, are quoted as evidence that among
civilised people the women have ever been “ the
fair sex.” The Egyptian officah aforesaid is as
black and nearly as naked as a primitive nigger,
whereas liis spouse is an olive-skinned lady with
much millinery on her. “ You would take them
to have been of different blood ; but the inscrip-
tion on the tomb states that they belonged to the
same race, and were, as a matter of fact, first
cousins,” says Haskett Smith. If the reverend
Egyptologist should ever again repeat his luminous
lectures in Melbourne, which he probably will
not, David Syme had better send bis staff along
for instruction. David’s staff would have just
about doubled the size of the audience the last
time Haskett Smith delivered his Memphis dis-
course.
m# m m
“In London Town,” is Bland Holt’s
latest opportunity for scenery and effects.
Somehow the name of Bland Holt was
omitted from the cast on Saturday’s pro-
gramme, but he made amends for this oversight
by appearing pretty frequently on the stage, dis-
guised as an able-bodied seaman of the famous
“ bull-dog breed.” The drama, in fact, is full of
deeds of valor by Bland Holt. He doesn’t seem
essential to the plot, but as a casual rescuer of Miss
Ross and a terror to evil-doers he commands loud
approbation. He is such a dutiful son that when
he stumbles accidentally against his aged mother
in a workhouse yard he insists upon restoring her
to freedom, without making formal application to
the vvork-’us authorities. The loving offspring
has no sooner assured himself of mother’s identity
than lie buys her an awful green silk costume to
comfort her declining years, and takes her off the
Parish in a cab, accompanied by Mrs. Bland
Holt, who appears as a warm-hearted Liddy
Bliss, and claps her hands accordingly. This
shows what a British sailor will do in time of need
for dear old mother.
• • • •
There are many impecunious characters
“ In London Town,” and some of the neediest are
not so virtuous as might be expected. A blind
father (Arthur Styan) potters around the metro-
polis in search of portable property. He appears
to be the head of a gang, and his system of
thieving attaches unjust suspicion to Miss
Ross, who thinks she is his daughter, whereas
she is really the child of a silver-haired
philanthropist (Walter Baker), and ought to be
luxuriating in Miss Harrie Ireland’s place. They
got mixed-up during their infancy, or later, owing
to Baker having married the bad blind man’s first
wife, or second wife, in the days of his poverty,
when he knew what it was to have the hunger-
pain, so he states. Unfortunately, Baker doesn’t
know ho is heaping jewellery on the wrong
daughter; neither can the sightless Styan see the
difference, but Albert Norman knows. Inci-
dentally, Albert is the father of the wrong
daughter’s improper baby, and he plots
mischief all the time. Considerable plot'
ting happens in slums, alleys, and streets
where tne raucous coster yells beside
his bloomin’ barrer, and in Hyde Park where the
midnight “ slop ” arouses the picturesque dosser
from dreams of sausages. Miss Ross grows weary
of unjust suspicion and tired of being beaten
by the unrelated blind parent, so she
arranges to fall into the river. Then
Albert Norman saves further trouble by pushing
her in, and the elegant form of Miss Ross floats
upon the painted stream until Bland rescues
her. Subsequently Norman attempts to hurl
Miss Ross from some London Roofs by Night,
but Bland rescues her again—Walter Baker
being too old and respectable to do any
rescuing in this drama and the mystery
is cleared up pleasantly. The villain falls off the
roof and kills himself, after the blind father has
been cast headlong downstairs, and the other
conspirators are mostly under arrest when the end
arrives. “In London Town,” at the Royal, looks
rather too badly built to be quite true to its origi-
nal form. Perhaps Bland Holt has been trying to
improve it, and the changes are for the worse, in
which case he will probably reconsider his altera-
tions. Brunton’s scenery and numerous matters
of management that help to detract attention from
the literary demerits of the drama can be praised
with great vigor.
*m m m
On Saturday night a crowded Athenaeum
Hall came to the conclusion that Bertram, the
conjuror, is a more easily dexterous man of tricks
than the late Dante, or any previous wizard seen
in Melbourne. Also he is a cheerful entertainer
in his stout and somewhat humorous way. The
royal and imperial magician carries a constant
chuckle through his two hours’ task. His skill
entitles him to chuckle a good deal, and no doubt
his expansive smile when he speaks of “the ladies”
—and he speaks of them 14 times per minute, or
oftener—is sound business policy. Nevertheless the
average male Australian would prefer a Bertram
who curtailed some of his unctuous patter and
gave more time to business. The things which
Bertram brings to pass might be left to talk for
themselves. They are amazingly funny. They
have been done before, or most of them have, by
the imitators who preceded him, but they never
were quite so marvellous as the old-hand makes
them appear. Bertram gives one the impression
of a man who has eaten profusely of buttered
muffins from bis childhood upwards, and has
thereby rendered himself slippery.
m sis # *
One week of the Italian Opera season at
Her Majesty’s has settled any lingering doubts re
the management’s capacity to produce two com-
panies of equal strength, and run either of them
for two or three consecutive nights without
damaging the voices. “ Aida,” a big opera to sing
in a thoroughly Italian style, was given again on
Saturday—the fifth time within eight days—and
was an all-round success. The two emphatic
ladies, Signora Benedetto and Signorina Marco-
mini are full of song a-id fury. A hasty
verdict on the acting of the mezzo-soprano artist
whose name ends with iis hereby cancelled. one
doesn’t sing so effectively as the plump little
Benedetto, but she acts nearly as well, and looks
her part better—which, however, doesn’t mean
that her appearance carries conviction with it.
We have to take Italian operatic figures as we see
them, and the voices as we hear them, and set one
consideration against the other. Larizza, the
robust tenor and interesting hero, promises to
maintain iiis top note in full robustness through
the campaign, and the other principals who were
good on the first night have improved their claims
to respect. Likewise the chorus, ballet, and spec-
tacle in general. “ Aida ” is a great show which
will call for a few revivals presently.
• m • •
“Lucia Di Lammermoor,” the tuneful
Donizetti opera that leads up to a famous sestet
and fades away into silence at the early hour of
10.30 p.m., was heard on Thursday and Friday,
and is billed for four nights this week. “ Lucia ”
makes an Italian attempt to introduce Scottish
color into the performance. The chorus-men
occasionally assemble in kilts and plaid chest-
protectors, and an elderly person named Raimondo
looks rather as though he considered himself a laird
of long ago. But tne opera doesn’t confine its sug-
gestions to any particular country or period. The
ladies who form a half-circle behind Raimondo at
the commencement of the third act, and subse-
quently fall back upon the scenery to give the
mad heroine plenty of singing room, are costumed
much the same as the gay revellers in “ Bohemian
Girl.” Moreover, the third act opens with a
Williamsonian Watteau Ballet, wherein the
brightest of the angelic dancing girls appears as
one of the boys and displays her long and beauti-
ful legs for all they are worth, in fleshings.
“ Lucia,” at Her Majesty’s, is a 3 festive as its
melancholy narrative will permit. Seven new
artists face the music in this opera, and acquit
themselves without a stain on their characters.
Signora De Vero Sapio was handicapped by a
very sore throat last week, yet she suc-
ceeded in scoring handsomely. Her voice
sounds sweeter than of yore, and every-
body who heard the tall imposing lady at
Melb. Town Hall some years back knows what
she can accomplish. The part of Lucia requires
some acting, and Signora Sapio, late of the con-
cert platform, proves to be a graceful dramatic
artist. The second tenor, Carlo Dani, is a highly
respectable warbler whofills the stage-lover position
creditably ; also there is a neat, presentable third
tenor in Cannonieri, but he gets killed behind
the scenes before he can do anything worth
mentioning. The aged Raimondo character has
a passable exponent, and the baritone brother,
Signor Bozzoli, is a nice-looking young man with
a pleasing voice. Probably he is very young
in stage experience, for he hangs his head
when he sings, and can’t bully the prima
donna as he should. On Saturday the third
opera, “Ernani,” will be staged, with the more
violent members of the co. in the principal parts.

Poor S’Africa ! The cable—
On account of rinderpest in Basutoland, all communica-
tion between that territory and the Orange River Colony
has been forbidden.
That is the culmination of miseries. First the
war, then typhus, then plague, and now rinderpest.
Plague, pestilence, and famine—battle, murder,
and sudden death, all have fallen on the devoted
country. Chamberlain, Rhodes and Co., with
their “ Never again ! ” are nearer right than they
know. Never again, indeed, will S’Africa be
what it was seven years ago.
“ North Sydney ” writes :
While N.S.W. Government has been finding plenty of
money for plaster columns, gimcrack silvered pawn-shop
balls, &c., and “ substantial luncheons to M.P.’s and their
wives, officials, leading bankers and commercial men,
&c,” the Govt. Dept, writes to various Schools of Arts as
follows : “ With reference to subsidy . . . due to 31st
December, 1900 . . . the claim in question must be held
over till the vote for 1901-2 has been passed by Parlia-
ment.” Meanwhile Parliament has closed during altera-
tions, and another six months’ claim is about due, and
creditors must wait, or else the bank account be over-
drawn, and interest paid. In one case—St. Leonards—
-10 per cent, discount off an account for £29 odd for
papers and periodicals has been lost because the subsidy
has not been paid, and soon the mortgage interest will be
largely increased under the deed, if the quarter’s interest
is delayed longer. Why does the Government offer a
subsidy, and lead Schools of Arts to reiy on it, if it can't
paj- the same ? All this is Reid’s Cash Basis fraud over
again. Under the old system all payments for each year
had to be charged to that year whether paid during the
year or not, therefore the Government paid up. It had
nothing to gain by delaying. Now, by putting this
whole financial year’s S. of A. subsidy into next year this
year’s public accounts are made to look so much the
better, because no account is taken of unpaid liabilities.
Good old Cash Basis 1

The Cobar Chesney mine, Cobar, have lately
placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful winding engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will be delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machinery was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*

All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on up-
to-date lines include a number of Luhrig vanners. The
South and Junction companies each have about a dozen,
no slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Coolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*

Federation, of course, has put things on a very different
footing from that of last year. The Commonwealth
Government has taken over the post and telegraph
revenue and part of the customs and excise, and it has
also relieved the States of corresponding expenditure. If
things had remained as they were the first four States
would have shown a larger increase, and the last two a
smaller decrease. Still, they are all affected in equal
degree, so the figures are useful for purposes of compari-
son. .
If these increases and decreases are reduced to
a common denominator of so much per inhabitant per
annum, we get these results according to the census
figures, now available for the first time :

Starting with N.S.W., the financial position of
that State is almost a record for utter badness. Not in-
cluding the extra receipts from the resumed area, which
are really money held in trust for the unpaid owners of
the property, we get these results

This means that legitimate revenue has increased by
£372 414 while expenditure (ordinary and loan) has in-
creased by £1,466,270, so things are £1,093,856 worse than
at this date last year. This is assuming that the pub-
lished expenditure at May, 1909, was the full expenditure,
and that no items were then disguised by being paid
out of trust funds. If the receipts fronT the resumed
area are included that makes the revenue larger, but in
that case the liabilities on account of the said area must
be put on the other side, which will somewhere about
balance things again. And in addition to the above,
there are the unpaid bills belonging to this year which
are being held over till next year under the Reid
Cash Basis dodge. The See Government tries to
make things look comparatively square by paying
£473 628 of war, plague and celebration expendi-
ture ’ out of trust funds, and by including the
receipts from the resumed area without putting the area s
unpaid liabilities against them. Certainly, this is not all
the Government’s fault, though it is largely so. A large
section of N.S.W. hollered for war—gory war. The aggre-
gate N S W. Fat Man especially hollered for war in the
hope of getting titles. The Lyne-See Government hadn’t
the courage either to resist the demand for war, or to tell
the country that war costs money here as everywhere
else, and that it would be necessary to double the
income-tax for several years to pay the bill. It hasn’t
the courage now to state that the bill has arrived, and to
demand an extra 6d. in the £ income-tax, even at this
late hour, to cover it- So it muddles the accounts in this
awful fashion by way of staving things off.
And this is how things are in Queensland, thanks
to war, drought, plague, the increasing interest bill
caused by lavish borrowing, and the cowardice of a
miserable Government, which dare not levy direct taxa-
tion to fill the gap :

Net result is that revenue has decreased £ 41 0,490 while
expenditure (ordinary and loan) has increased £165,69-,,
so things are £579.182 worse than they were at this date
last year. This isn’t so bad a situation in the lump as
that of N.S.W., where things are £1,093,806 worse, but it
is rather worse in proportion to Queensland’s population.
Queensland has a huge sum to pay for interest just at the
end of the financial year, so a deficit of £500,000 is prac-
tical!; certain. If Queensland had the N.S.W. Cash Basis
swindle, it could make things look all right. In N.S.W
the interest due for the second half of the financial
year is not charged till next financial year. The
money 3 has passed out of the N.S W Treasurer s
hands before the accounts are made up at 30th June, but
he reckons it as money in hand all the same; at least
Reid did so, and as far as the public knows the practice
still continues. But Queensland never learned the Gash
Basis dodge. .
Tasmania publishes no monthly expenditure
accounts, but these things are known concerning it : Its
revenue has slumped so badly that, even if it was still col-
lecting all its postal, telegraph, customs, ana excise,
t would show a decline of £30,945 or the five
months, equal to £74,263 per annum. Also that Mild
financial profligate, Bolton Stafford Bird, is back at the
Treasurv, in place of the careful Fysh and B ‘. add °”’
is reputed to be shovelling out money with a big shovel in
each hand. The anticipated increase of ordinary expen-
diture for the year is about £70,000.
Up to the end of May Westralia’s revenue was
£2 779,928; expenditure, £2,807,585; deficit, £27,657.
And since then war bills have been coming m gaily, and it
is telegraphed that a large shortage is expected at the
end of the vear. S.A. is also expected to show a small
deficit, and Victoria may come out about square, though
this is uncertain. Very little information is available
from either State, but war and Duke and drought have
struck them both—especially war. No one State had the
courage to tell its people that war must be paid for by
extra taxation, so they are all in a bad way.
* * * *
A few days ago Victoria and N.S. W. borrowed
locally £500,000 each, and the money was put up so un-
obtrusively that hardly any one noticed that anything
was happening. Victoria got its half-million at about
£3 4s per cent. N.S.W. floated a shorter-dated loan at
about £3 12s. 6d. per cent. This makes about £2,000,000
which N.S.W. has borrowed in Australia, during the last
twelve months, and the total local borrowings of the
seven States of Australasia in the twelve months are
about £5,000,000 or £6,000,000. In every case the money
has been got below —mostly much below—London rates.
And on 31st March, 1901, there was £22,728,858 of gold
and silver in the banks of the seven States, as compared
with £21,870,402 fifteen months previously. All this
enormous local borrowing, instead of depleting the
country of money as so many old-rut amateur-
financiers foretold, has only resulted in an increase of
£850,000 in the country’s cash resources inside 1J years.
The awful blunder of the Lyne-See Government in
borrowing that recent million in London at 4 per cent,
when money can be got here at so much less;
and the cowardice of the Turner Government in
allowing itself to be squeezed to the extent of £3 12s. or
£3 14s. per cent, in London for its recent renewal loan of
£3, ooo, ooo'without even trying the local market, stick out
more conspicuously than ever.
* * • *
The United States are overflowing just now
with money, and certain New York capitalists have
written to the N.S.W. Government pointing out that the
4 per cent, loan recently floated in London was an ex-
orbitant fraud, and that'Cornstalk loans could be placed
more cheaply in New York. If another easy borrowing
market opens up for this spendthrift land it will be the
worst disaster that has struck it for many years. The
average Australian “ statesman ” is prepared to borrow
everything that everybody will lend, and the more
lenders he can find the faster will he rake down' ruin on
the head of this sorrowful country. If Australia must go
on borrowing, let us do it locally, from our own people.
This country is already the abject bond-slave of the
British usurer; it doesn’t need to pawn its soul to an
American one also.

* * * *
The State Bank of South Australia—the con-
cerh which raises money at 3J per cent., and lends it out
to the local agriculturist at 4£ per cent.—has been in
operation for five years and two months up to 31st March,
1901, and still bears up against the Tory party’s forebod-
ings of evil. It has now £507,408 lent out on mortgage,
and the entire arrears of interest are £339. Last year’s
results compare with previous figures as follows

The South Australian State Bank has thus realised since
its commencement £6097 from premiums on 1-onds sold,
and £1663 ordinary profit. As it had to pay per cent,
on its capital from the very commencement (unlike an
ordinary bank which seldom pays any dividend on its
capital, in its first year), and as it lends out its
funds at much below' ordinary banking rates, the result
is not at all bad. The profits of the ordinary business are
growing larger every year. The expenses for the year
just ended were at the rate of 15s. 9d. percent, on average
capital employed, and as the margin allowed for expenses
and losses is £1 per cent., and losses so far have been .nil,
a good reserve is being steadily built up out of the
difference. The management of the S.A. State Bank
should now make it its object and fixed resolve to con-
tinue building up that reserve till the institution won’t
require to sell bonds in order to get money with which to
make loans—till its reserve of sundry millions will be
its capital, and the bonds can all be repaid, and every
pennv of money in the institution will be the
State’s money. Then it will be able to lend on
mortgage at £3 per cent., and about £2 10s. per
cent, of that will be profit, to further enlarge the
funds and extend the sphere of the bank’s usefulness.
Until the State Bank has grown so large that the private
mortgagee is extinct in S.A., and every penny lent on
mortgage in S.A. is lent by the State Bank, and every
pennv so lent is the bank’s own money—not borrowed on
bonds from anybody—the funds of the institution should
be allowed ta accumulate. After that result is arrived
at, it will be time enough for the State to annex part of
the profits for the benefit of the Treasury.
The Independent Order of Foresters, the Cana-
dian assessment institution which is pushing business in
Australia —a country which hasn’t yet become fully
acquainted with the assessment variety of life business—
was recently mixed up in a rather violent American law-
suit. In the plaintiff’s statement remarks like this
appeared : “ That the Order is managed by a clique of
Canadians, headed by Oronhyatekha, a half-breed Indian,
in an arbitrary, reckless, negligent, and extravagant
manner : that it is paying out of its mortuary funds
thousands of dollars to favorites for prizes, and to certain
favored newspapers for printing columns of matter
laudatory of its officials ; and that it has sunk over
700,000 dollars in a wild real estate speculation, and loaned
its funds to its officials secured by mortgage on their over-
valued lands. That the Order has not only paid these
officials extravagant salaries, but also made them munifi-
cent gifts of money out of its funds ; that its officials
have destroyed its records to conceal their illegal acts
. . that the Order has become practically insolvent,
there being outstanding and unpaid death and disability
claims against it amounting to over 300,000 dollars,” &c.
The management flatly denies all these charges, and the re-
sult of the case is not yetto hand. Apropos of the allegation
that the 1.0. F. spends its money recklessly in printing
laudations of its officials, how much space was filled in the
Sydney papers in the early days of the calamity by de-
scriptions of the Indian boss’s commanding figure, and
rare sweet smile, and tremendous bull voice, and things
like that ?
* * * *
The Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria, an in-
stitution which has been laboring in a heavy sea for seven
or eight years, has written off £106,406, being the amount
paid up on 94,623 forfeited shares, and already applied in re-
duction of losses ; and t'l 3s. per share on the other 155,377
shares, equal to another £178,654. This will leave the
concern with a paid capital of about £145,000 —the exact
amount depends on what -calls have been collected since
last balance-sheet was published. Unfortunately, this
writing-down doesn’t increase the income by a single
penny ; nor, as no dividends are paid, does it diminish
the outgoings, so the deficit (which was £2289 for half-
year to 31st December, 1900), apparently remains just
where it was, unless business improves. The writing-
down just about wipes off the visible existing deficit
(which was £177,560 at 31/12/1900), and leaves no margin
for any further disasters. And the Land Mortgage Bank s
disasters came along so regularly that it is difficult to feel
at all confident that it is at the end of them.
» * * *
The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Co. (Sydney)
The reserves consist of reserve fund, £12,510 ; reserve
for equalisation of dividends, £3500 ; and undivided pro-
fit, £4095. The management reckons them at £22,405 by
including another item—“ capital suspense account,
£2310.” What this may be it is very difficult to say, but
it doesn’t sound as if it was a reserve ; it rather suggests
shares that have hanged themselves on the premises.
Even without this item, however, the Intercolonial’s re-
serves show a verv remarkable increase in the last 10
years ; and the profit for the half-year just ended is a
considerable improvement on recent figures. The total
assets are £72,488, and against these there are only
£l5 586 of deposits and other liabilities to the public.
The’ other £50,902 represents shareholders’ funds, so the
co. is in the eminently safe position of trading almost
wholly with its own money.
• • * *
Melbourne Age very pertinently inquires why
the Victorian farmer doesn’t put more energy into the
work of establishing co-operative butter-factories, and
the profits private factories make out of him are certainly
enormous. The best profit is at the rate of 228 per
cent, per annum,on capital, and the very worst paying
private butter-factory returns 19 per cent. The average
rate is 85 per cent.
* * * *
The mine disaster at the South Broken Hill is
the first bad accident that has occurred since the Mines
Accident Relief Fund came into operation. UnderN.S.W.
State law, applicable to all but the smallest mines, a
weekly deduction of 4id. is made from wages. The
owners add 41d. ; the State adds 9d. This entitles the
widow or guardian to Bs. a week, and the children under
14 to 2s 6d. a week each, with £l2 for funeral exes. It
also allows of provision during sickness resulting from
mining accident.
* * * •
One of the men killed by the fall of ore in the
Broken Hill South mine left nine children, the eldest (the
only one able to work) 16. The widow will draw from the
new Mines Accident Relief Fund £1 Bs. a week ; half-a-
crown will be knocked-off as each child reaches the age of
14 Deceased had paid into the fund altogether 6s. 3d.
* * * *
The fact that the inquest on the victims of the
Broken Hill South mine-accident resulted in something
stronger than the usual verdict of “ accidental death, no
blame being attachable to anybody,” was mainly due to the
fact that the jury was led by E. J. Polkinghorne, ex-
president of the Miners’Association, one of the leaders who
were gaoled during the last strike. Both manager and Govt,
mines inspector were included in the jury’s condemna-
tion, with which the coroner (not a Labor-man) ex-
pressed his concurrence. An inquiry by a royal commis-
sion will probably follow.
• * • •
B.H. North has at last decided to shut down
and wait till the clouds roll by. This means another 100
men on the verv over-crowded Barrier labor-market, the
balance of the 200 employed at the end of last year having
been gradually thinned out before the final step was
taken. Until the lead market improves or something
happens in connection with ore-dressing experiments,
the co. will confine itself to the extension and improve-
ment of its appliances for re-dressing the ore left after
the first crop of silver and lead has been taken from it.
The North’s present misfortune is that, though it has a
large quantity of ore (no mine has improved more in that
respect) it is all average-grade stuff—there is no good
stuff sandwiched in between poor bodies. At most times
this is a good defect; but not just now, when it’s only
the ham part of the sandwich that can be eaten.
» • * »
Re the Lake George winding-up: Plant and
machinery, as in books, including new cyanide plant,
£56,662; yet the total is valued for purposes of
realisation'at £5000; The leases have no value but a
book one, and that is £98,509. Is it possible that the
machinery will only realise £5000? A disgusted share-
holder wants to know if the winding-up is definitely
settled, are the leases cancelled or forfeited and available
to any person paying the necessary fees, and if the
defunct co. can take away all the machinery. The co.
recently got a short exemption from labor-conditions.
Sooner or later the leases will be available. The law
allows a certain time for the removal of machinery, but
as it is worth three times more where it is than to take
away it is likely to remain while there is any prospect of
the mine re-opening. With the strictest economy and a
railway the mine would pay.
Associated Northern : A report made by W.
Griffith, a S’African mining man, has been published.
Understood that he has also given a report on Brown
Hill, but the latter has not seen light. Of the A.N., he
says the manager’s measurements, figures and estimates
of’reserves are correct, that the mining is good, sampling
accurate and extensive. Lode runs from north to
southern boundary, the oxidised zone is from 8 to 30ft.
wide, and the sulphide from 4 to Bft. Auriferous all the
way, but not payable for full length, the best shutes of
ore being in the vicinity of Nos. 1 and 3 shafts. Values
of sulphides and tellurides are put down to average
70dwt., not including phenomenally rich streaks, and
everv foot of the ore-body exposed has been carefully
sampled. Oxidised ores in sight, 21,000 tons, averaging
20dwt. per ton. Sulphide ore, 37,000 tons at 70dwt. per
ton, and 23,000 tons at 80dwt., and with the ore at
grass, bringing total up to 86,000 tons ; and when
331 per cent, is allowed off assay value for possible
mistakes and losses, there is a balance of 167,0000 z., value
£668,000. Estimated that this will realise half-a-
million profit. The shipping of rich ore to Fremantle is
recommended because of the danger of leaving it on the
surface, and the necessity of keeping development work
well ahead. Mr. Gritfith advises that the mine should be
developed to 800 ft. before divs. are declared, but stoping
for rich ores is not recommended. He is not very definite
about the character of the reducing plant, contending
that the best solution of the sulphide problem has not yet
been arrived at. Generally acknowledged that the
Northern Associated has a big future, ana that that of
the Brown Hill is as dismal as the other is bright. It has
had a magnificent past, and instead of being satisfied
with it, and admitting that the days of the mine are
numbered unless it can amalgamate with the Northern
Associated, innumerable lies are told to keep the market
up, to squeeze the “ bears,” and to enable certain people
to creep out.
* * * •
The Hainault battery crushed last month 670
tons of sulphide ore from the 500 ft. level for 4740 z. gold,
the tailings assaying 6dwt.—an average of 14dwt. per ton.
“ The result must be considered very satisfactory.” That
is the invariable phrase when it is a horrible disappoint-
ment. Often given out that this mine had thousands of
tons of ore at grass that would average over an oz. There
is nothing in 14dwt. rock in a mine like the Hainault. It
is worth working for its position and prospects, and that
is about all that can be said of it.
* * * *
Chaffer’s: Some more diamond-drill work has
been started on this mine—“which should prove very
interesting.” The interesting feature of Chaffer’s is the
fact that though it is surrounded by good mines, such
as the Golden Horseshoe and the Boulder Main Reefs,
it has never found any gold to speak of, yet it
must have spent about £lOO,OOO in the effort. About
time that it threw up the sponge. The chances are that
if there is a pay-lode in the mine its location will be dis-
closed by the workings of one of the good mines around
it, so what is the use of monkeying money away by ex-
pensive shaft-sinking and diamond drill boring ?
South Kalgurli: Treatment plant to be closed
down for two or three weeks for the installation of addi-
tional grinding mills and alteration of the furnaces,
which will largely increase the production. “ All round
the underground workings are in a very healthy state and
this, added to the increase in the treatment plant,
assures a very bright future for the property.” Does it ?
Not if vou look at the past. It has been crushing from
1200 to 1500 tons a month for about the same number of
oz., and although it has been doing this for over a year it
has never paid one little div.—indicating that it has taken
all the gold it could get to pay the mine’s way.
* * * *
Ironsides North (Hannan’s): Still getting mag-
nificent assays and realising some money by sale of water.
Unfortunately, it is too far outside the defined golden
area to have any hope of ever becoming a mine, and much
money might be saved by shutting it up.
Croesus Proprietary (Hannan's): Reported that
the lode has been sampled and assayed by the a«sayer of
Brookman’s Boulder mine for an average of 3oz. a ton,
taken for a width of 4ft. And emphasis is laid on the
fact that the assayer broke down the samples himself.
There is an ahsence of compliment in this for the mana-
ger. Assays are a delusion and a snare, and their publi-
cation should be stopped by threats of boilingjnl.

* * • *
The Duke of York’s visit has done the
cause of Monarchy in Australia more harm
than can be repaired in half a century. The
poor little Prince struck the always-irreverent
Australian as a weary puppet run by clumsy
showmen who let the working of the strings
be seen all the time. The public appearances of
the weak, frightened-looking little man were all
most painful to sympathetic onlookers, and especi-
ally painful was his appearance at Sydney Uni-
versity when that institution went through the
unspeakably-grotesque mockery of conferring upon
him an LL.I).-ship. However, the style of the
Duke’s acknowledgment of the “ honor ” accorded
him, humiliating as it must have been to all who
wish to see the University maintained as a bona-
fide seat of learning, was exactly the sort of thing
called for by so outrageously incongruous a kow-
tow.

Sydney Daily Telegraph’s recorder of
the Duke’s shooting excursion remarks patheti-
cally that success with the ducks was a minor
quantity—“they were not tame!” Eheu ! He
probably expected that the birds would waddle
up to George Frederick Ernest Albert with little
Union Jacks tied to their tails, requesting death
per favor of royal duck-shot.
* • * *
Among all the proposed statues to the
late Queen is there one which hasn’t gone on the
rocks for want of money ? The Bulletin doesn’t
know of one. Apparently Victoria is in a fair
way to have 12 or 14 half-monuments or quarter-
cenotaphs—in some cases the money is only about
sufficient to supply Her Majesty up to the knees
and all the rest of her is still unsubscribed—but
not one whole one. At Wellington (M.L.) a
special effort was made to get the coin together so
that York could lay the foundation-stone with
eclat, but even that prospect failed to bring in the
needful “splosh.” At latest advices the subscrip-
tions ran to about half the price at which a cheap
slop-monument could be procured, and the stream
of cash had dried up so utterly that hardly six-
penn’orth of financial moisture remained in its
bed.
* * * *
David Syme’s begging list for the Queen
Victoria national monument has just about reached
its*34th thousand shilling. It has taken many
weeks to scrape up the last £lOO, and often six or
seven inches of valuable space was devoted to
recording the gathering-in of thirty or thirty-five
shillings, full names being given for every “bob.”
The big success of many large funds the Argus
has taken in hand of late years makes failure all
the more galling to “the Boss.” David should
remember that the Argus appeals to a class which
profits socially and in business by bigcharityadvts.,
whereas his paper goes mainly amongst people to
whom a shilling is twelve pence, and who have no
little handbills to plaster on Victoria’s monument.
* * * »
Melb. Age printed a touching little story
of the Duchess meeting, in a cot at a Sydney
hospital, a small boy who had broken his leg
tumbling from a fence to which he had climbed
to catch a glimpse of their Royal Highnesses
‘•So you broke your leg trying to see me, did you ? I
suppose you are sorry you ever wanted to see me now,”
continued the Duchess, as she handed the hoy a bunch of
violets. Young James was too much astonished to reply,
but the look on his face indicated that he would willingly
have broken the other leg for her Royal Highness.
The Bulletin is only deterred from sacking its
Office Myth, the Little Boy at Manly; and giving
James the billet, by the probability that his look
was too freely translated. Possibly he thought he
was climbing to see something almost as interest-
ing as a circus, and the look implied a belief that
be had been shockingly imposed upon.

Major “Tom” MTnerney, back from the
Boer-butchery, with probably as much expex-ience
of the ins and outs of the business as any other
Australian, sneers much more fiercely than the
Melb. dailies admit in their interviews at the
charges of treachery directed against the Boers.
“Lies,” he says, “excepting in a few isolated
cases where there was only individual responsi-
bility.” Just so. There was basis for just as
heinous charges against the other side, but the
foolish Boers neglected to run a cable to Australia.
Despite all the care taken that none
but perfectly naice people should mingle
with Royalty, one or two s mcking things
happened. One afternoon off, when there
was no official function, the Duchess
arranged to visit a show place m a
Northern town, and was taken round by
the president of the affair—a jovial cus.
to whom everybody is hail fellow well
met. The Duchess said every thing was
very nice, which is the thing that Royalt)
is always expected to say ; an( ‘ i “ le
director thereupon remarked : _ ‘ But
there is one work of art you haven t seen
yet, and the people of Australia are more
fond of it than of any other. In fact,
they really worship it.” Tl.o Princess
Atay looked puzzled. “What is that?”
she asked. Then the boss laughed the
loud kookaburra laugh of the person
who rushes in where angels fear to tread,
and seizing the lady by the shoulder
swung her round on her heels to face her
own reflection in a long mirror. The
Buu.etin fancies there is. something in
the British Constitution to meet a case
of this sort. The offence, if it recollects
rightly, is catching hold of the Presence,
and is punishable by boiling oil, or
gradual freezing in the cold meat estab-
lishment, or by having all the bound
volumes of the back Parliamentary reports
of Australia dropped on your feet for 14
days.

The long-drawn out squabble among
the Wellington (M.L.) shopkeepers, every
one of whom insisted that the Jookal pro-
cession should amble right past his front
door, was ended without bloodshed.
though it was a close thing. Governor
Rmfurly, who isn't dependent on
anybody’s vote, wanted to explain
to the shopkeepers that the Jook
wasn’t hunt to be an ornament to
anybody’s fr< nt door ; but Sed-
don was wiser in bis genera-
tion, and set to work to spread
the Heir-Apparent out as far as
he would go. By getting him
to enter the city one way, and
to go another way next day when
he went to solemnly whack a founda-
tion-stone under pretence of ’‘laving” it,
and by taking him through some other different
streets on his departure, Seddon pacified the free
and enraged electors to some extent. There are
still certain back streets that have been done out
of their fair share of Jook. But that can’t be
helped.
• * * *
A voice from Maoriland :
Things are about as promising as any anarchist could
wish. Premier Seddon, as usual, aims at shading other
ceremonies, and wants a huge Maori corroboree at the
Hot hakes, and a 16,000-men military review in Christ-
church. (Melbourne only had a 14,000-men review at
Flemington.) This military display is to happen in Hagley
Park, which is as fiat as the Werrib.ee Plains, and very few
people, except the 4000 in the temporary stands, will see
anything. Otago is objecting to its own show losing the
military adjunct, if its troops are sent to Christchurch.
Some old Maori tribal feuds are being resuscitated, and
the Waikatos want to meet the Duke in Auckland harbor
while the Arohas want to do something else, and the little
band of Rotorua people may be left to. dance hakas on
their little own. Wellington shopkeepers in one street
refuse to close if the procession goes down some other
street; deputations are recalcitrating between Mayor,
Premier and Acting-Gov., and, altogether, there isn't
a speck of brotherly love left in the two islands.

The South Australian Bushmen’s Corps
has just been graciously pleased to express its
approval of Lord Methuen, and has notified the
British War Office in writing to that effect,
finishing up with what is virtually a recom-
mendation that Methuen should be rewarded
for his good conduct when in command of his
present S.A. patrons. Safe to say that no more
patronising and humorous communication than
this has ever reached the War Ofiice.

“ Grilaomi ” : Minister Perry’s abject idea
of cadging among poor State-school-children for
the price of Union Jacks brought a lot of miseiy
to many Cornstalk youngsters. I knew one
bright little chap of seven years whose parents
objected to the levy. He felt his position so
much that when the teacher publicly asked him
for about the tenth or twentieth time for his
“ voluntary ” contribution, he felt in his pocket
and declared that he had been given Gd. to contri-
bute, but had lost it. Cross-examination brought
out the fact that there had never been any 6d.,
and the culprit was caned by his shocked peda-
gogue for telling a lie.
* * * *
Botha’s alleged abject superstition in
appealing to the comet as a sign specially sent to
the Boers, has been justly reprobated. Contrast
it, for instance, with the following, taken from a
M.L. paper:
It would need no great stretch of imagination to believe
the comet was the herald of the royal visit.
No abject superstition about that ! Botha
tried to believe that the comet was a sign sent, to
a nation making its last desperate struggle for in-
dependence. The superior Maorilander reckons
that it heralds the visit of a tired little man, come
to look at a few arches.

INEBRIATE (who has been knocking at lamp-post

Adelaide contains more haunted houses to
the acre than any other city in Australia. One of
these —substantial, capacious and airy, not 3min.
walk from the G.P.O. —has, during the past five
years, been occupied less than five weeks. Occa-
sionally it is taken, and the tenant moves out
again before he has quite finished moving in. For
a long time the house which Speaker Holder has
lately occupied went begging at 10s. a week on
account of alleged spectres. _ If anything thinner
than Holder walks about its rooms it is haunted
right enough. Yet another man has got the use
of a house gratis for 12 months on his guarantee
that the spectre will be out of it by the end of
that period.
* * • •
Pathetic case the other day in Preston
(Melb.) suburban court. A woman got 14 days
for altering her marriage certificate, by adding
six years on the age, so as to obtain an old-age
pension. She pleaded that she had only restored
her right age, and that her statement when she
married was a falsehood, inspired by vanity.
Probably she spoke the truth. And after all
these years her sin has found her out in the shape
of two weeks in gaol and the loss of six years’
pension.
* • * * *
Prohibitionist apostle, F. B. Boyce,
drenching N.S.W. with watery argument, cries
long and loudly for a referendum. But his pro-
posed referendum is worded, “Do you vote for
prohibition—Yes or No ? ” Under which arrange-
ment the Boyce party would stand to win every-
thing and lose nothing. How would it be to put
the parties on an equality, and word the ballot-
paper thus:—'
1 Do you vote that people who drink spirits shall not
in future be allowed to drink them—Yes or No ? .
2. Do you vote that people who do not drink spirits
shall in future be compelled to drink them Yes or No
That would be a fair deal between Boyce and
Bacchus.

A delicious lot of artistic and other loot
brought from China by members of the N.S.W.
naval contingent, is being very privately exhibited
here and there in Sydney. Some members of the
contingent have made big dividends out of their
little picnic.
* * * *
Cardinal Moran’s excuse for absenting
himself from the York celebrations was that his
church had been alfronted by the Duke. 11ns
time the Cardinal happens to be absolutely in the
right. The Duke, it seems, declined in Mel-
bourne to lay the foundation-stone of a R.C. hos-
pital on the ground that during his visit to Aus-
tralia he would take no part in any sectarian
ceremony. But when he went to Brisbane he laid
the foundation-stone of a sectarian (Anglican)
cathedral. In Australia, thank Heaven, the
Anglicans are just as much sectarians as the How-
ling Ebenezers. The Duke’s advisers were quite
unable to understand that Australia is devoid of
the blessing of an Established Church.
* * * *
The Manly (Sydney) residents have (for
so far, vainly) been protesting against the exist-
ence, close to their beautiful ocean beach, of a
hideous merry-go-round nuisance—a lease from
the municipality to the proprietor of the horror
appearing to stand in the way of its removal. But
the granting of such lease was a gross piece of
vandalism, to begin with. One of the few remain-
ing beauty spots about Sydney should have been
the last place to have inflicted on it what even
amidst the most sordid and prosaic surroundings
—is a soul-and-ear-harrowing abomination. Of a
piece with this business is the manner in which
the approach by water to Manly has been. dis-
figured by the hideous hoardings of a coterie of
soulless advertisers.

The other day. a young man of 23, son of|a
N.S.W. country-newspaper proprietor, deposed

Lucky journalist !
Notable that the Sydney dailies, when
recording chloroform-deaths in local private
hospitals, never name the hospitals concerned. A
couple more such fatalities have occurred within
the last week or two.
* • * *
The Duke of York congratulated Inspec-
tor-General Fosbery on the efficiency of the
Sydney police, and' the Duchess went into rap-
tures over “ the beautiful horses ” of the troopers.
Had the Princess May been 64 years old instead
of 34 she might possibly have ventured to pub-
licly express her admiration of the men them-
selves as well as their mounts, for, as a corps of
serviceable and smart-looking horsemen, the
N. S.W. mounted police are unmatched on this
continent. Unlike the much-advertised N.S.W.
Lancers, they have no adventitious aid from
feathers, pennons and gaudy clothes ; their cold,
severe, close-fitting uniform is one that shows a
man up every time -for exactly what he is. The
mounted police are as the apple of the Inspector-
General’s eye, and it is now about as hard to get
into their ranks as it to enter Parliament.
And after all the drain there has been on the
Australian horse-market, those fine N.S.W.
Mounted Police horses note cost less than =£l2
apiece. There can’t be much in horse-breeding.

HE (to girl who is leaving for the country): "Don't you think you'll find it
very dull there?”
SHE: "OK no; I intend to do a lot of riding and driving while I am away.
And, don't he shocked, I am going to ride barebacked.”
HE: “ Shocked? Oh, dear no; but won't it make you frightfully brown?”
spite the oath of attorney Eddie, one of his
employers, that he (Eddie) had led Croft to
understand that the witnesses in question had
been paid. The case will convey a severe lesson
to people who have got into the free-and-easy
habit of swearing that they know a thing has
been done, whereas they only intend to do it, or
have been told that someone else has done it, or
intends to do it.
* * * *
So far, two-thirds of the amount needed
for furnishing the proposed additions to Sydney
Prince Alfred Hospital (the foundation of which
additions the Duke of York laid the other
day) have been collected. Several people, who
have given £lOOO each to the fund, made a con-
dition that a ward in the new wing should be named
after themselves or someone nominated by the,m.
The number of wards is limited, and will
be insufficient to go round. Considering that it
takes £l2OO, at least, to endow one bed out of the
32 in an existing ward (for which sum you can get
your name engraved over the one patient s head),
a person asking that £lOOO should buy the right of
claiming the whole ward is looking out for a re-
markably cheap advertisement.
• * * *
Six hundred kharki kangaroos landed at
Port Melbourne, t’other morning, amid the deep-
voiced plaudits of two policemen, four womm,
and a steam-roller. The Argus stirred the State
Government up at the last minute to have the
men welcomed home in a style “befitting the
occasion.” The- intention of Premier Peaccck
was evidently to allow the heroes to land quietly
and go off home in peace, but the Argus predicted
a tremendous crowd and a vociferous welcome,
and Peacock worked all night to get barriers
put up along Collins and Swanston streets
to keep the populace back. It was a
wet, sleety morning when the landing took place,
and the populace didn’t arrive. 'I he soldiers
looked as despondent as a string of wet sheep
straggling up the race from a wash-pool. Same
morning some of the wattle-and-dab Corinthian
columns on Prince’s Bridge were blown over. A
far bigger crowd rushed to see the heaps of splin-
ters than to welcome the soldiers.
A NATIVE ROSE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I ope’d a book in London town,
And from its pages fluttered down
A faded spray of Native Rose. . . .
Then did constraining walls unclose,
And then dissolved the fog-pall brown.
The dull, drab skies no longer frown ;
For under deeps of blue they drown,
Since, settling for an hour’s repose,
ope’d a book.
Nature pi i off her Northern gown
For Southern garb. From where hills crown
The gullies deep a fragrance blows ;
I feel the pang the exile knows,
Since in this city of renown
I ope’d a book !

A WOMAN'S LETTER
• r t\ * Sydney, June 11, 1901.
Mr Dear Arini,—
There’.; a passage in Scripture that’s always had
a curious charm for me-“ At that time there was
no judge in Israel, and every man did that which
was right m his own eyes.” I was always recall-
ing it while the Dukelet was here. No one seemed
to have anything to do, and they neglected it in
the way that best pleased them and most annoyed
other persons. Now, there’s some work gome on
m the city. Cables of withered lilli-pilli are in
the gutters; the “One People One Destiny”
legends, in perforated tin, are being taken off the
house-tops and stowed into basements amongst
dynamos and cockroaches and things; workmen
are taking away poles and fibrous-plaster arches
and telling of the loss Duke holidays brought
to them, and wondering if they can crib some of
the posts, upon which royal eyes have rested for
fowl-houses.
One of the last minor appearances of the
Duchess was at Children’s and Sydney Hos-
mtals, when she gave each patient to whom
she spoke a little bunch of violets. This
seems to be the conventional thing for
.British royalty when it visits the sick. It’s
a pretty act, a graceful present, and it doesn’t
cost as much as would a book or a toy also it
doesn t make the gloves sticky like a bath bun or
trmt. And as the patients don’t graze like the
old-world Babylonian king, the violets can’t
disagree with them. Now I know someone at
Botts Point will concuss my brain with Emerson’s
flowers are always fit presents because they are
theproudascrtion that a ray of beauty outvalues
a 1 the utilities of the world.” How’s that for a
bloated sentence ! But is this British royalty’s
reason for the conventional gift?
Indian royalty could give a lesson in gift-
. A friend of mine has a Rajah friend (no
A rim, I m not boasting, really, and I’ll still con-
tinue to call you a friend) who, when he wished
to dispense a few souvenirs, swooped down on the
leading jeweller in Calcutta and bade him
spread the best of his stock on the counter It
was done. He divided the glowing lot into two
equal parts with his walking-stick, and said, in a
coldly indifferent voice, “ Send me this half.”
British royalty, when it “ progresses,” seems to lie
in a state of bankruptcy, and as if it ought to go
into Chancery and be sold. 8
I started to tell you something of the Young
People s Industrial Ex. in last letter, but stopped
overwhelmed by the bigness of the subject!
ihe Duchess was run in to see a dupli-
cate collection of the late Queen Victoria’s
dolls staged against a painted background of the
palace where Victoria was born. The Duchess—
her parasol glittered with iridescent spangles—
looked at the group of Dutch-faced images with a
wlmt-a-taste-my-husband’s-grandmother-had ex-
pression, and said “ they are very nice.” She also
gave a decimal of a glance at the young-person-
made underwear and babies’ bootees, and then went
home to, luncheon. Some years ago an English
writer, Miss Frances Low, almost doubled the
Circulation of a London magazine by an illustrated
article Ojn The Queen’s Dolls.” She saw them and
their owner, and her readers lived happy everafter.
Apropos, the papers said never a word about the
unspeakable indignation caused by the uncere-
monious way in which the royal couple scuttled
from the Young People’s I.E after bestowing
upon it the hastiest of glances
Four photographers were commanded to Govern-
ment House to, take York “ fortagraffs.” Each
a,rtist thought lie alone had been honored, and
xyas a surprised artist when he ran up against the
Other three. I saw some capital portraits of
Duchess May, done by Mr. Hubert Newman of
George-street, who was one of the astonished four.
Ihe Duchess posed on the wide verandah, stand-
ing near a little table. One hand rests on a sil ver-
casket, the other holds a spray of roses. She
wears a long black dress, patterned with raised
velvet leaves, and a rope of pearls falls below her
waist. May was so pleased with that study that
she filled her best carpet-bag with copies. The
Duke also had his pictorial innings, supported
against a stone wall.
The Duke of York is said to be a good, quiet
little man, but many a happy home has he broken
up, and many a husband has he caused to curse
the day on which a male Duke was born. “She
left us last week,” said a haggard lady to me the
other clay ; “ said she was going to her mother’s
for an hour, but she never returned.” Of course
it is the last servant, not the lost wife or daughter
to whom allu ion was made. The large number of
Mary Janes who became extremely ill, and had to
go home to their mothers, a day or so before
George appeared, was extraordinary. Curiously
enough they had all ordered new hats to be ill in
and correct castumes of mauve and black to sup-
port the weary hours on their beds of anguish.
‘I ve lost one,” said one dame to another—she
spoke m hushed tones as if she meant a funeral •
And my sister has lost three.” None of the
Mary Janes could speak above a whisper when
they left—they were so hoarse and ill; but they
were all seen at the various processions a few days
later waving their mauve hats to May and won-
dering in large healthy voices whether they took
to her or George most.

Apropos of Mayors: The Mayor of Quean-
o,?, ya i': . a short > a ß£ rea sive-looking gentleman,
sought to enter a railway carriage bound Cooma-
wards, at Redfern, the other day, but the door
was locked, and from the inside one of five
laugh ty warriors explained, “You can’t come in
here; this is reserved for us.” “And who are
you, in all creation ? ” demanded the Mayor. “We
are the Australian Horse,” was the reply. “O,
you are, are you; well I’m an Australian mare
and 1 mean to get in. ” And he did get in, amid
horse laughter from the A. H.
A Brisbane correspondent writes :
I he women of Brisbane are raking in a shekel
or two to provide Lady Lamington with a present
on her departure. It is to take the form of a set
of toilet accessories—all gold, rather plain in
design, with a coronet and her name, ‘May,’en-
graved upon the brush backs, tray, puff box, etc.
I Ins is the sort of scheme which is apt to start
well and end badly through a drought among the
shekels, but let us hope for the best.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ At Healesville lives an old Irishman, formerly
employed by Lord Vontry, Hopey’s father-in-
law. Rocently Paddy left for Molb. to pay his
respects to Lady Hopetoun, whom he remembers
as a girl. When he camo back the neighbors were
mad with curiosity, but, manlike, Paddy needed a
lot of pumping. ‘ Was she nice to you ? ’ ‘ Why
wouldn’t she be?’ ‘What’s she like?’ ‘Sure
like any other woman.’ ‘ls she like me ?’ ‘You!
She doesn’t put on half the jam and side you do.
She brought me a cup o’ tay herself and tould me
to be sure to come and see her again. There’s
nothing half so quiet and homely as herself in
Healesville.’
“Of all the Senators who are sometimes
he rne *' with at luncheons and casual gatherings
Melbourne girls like John Downer best. ‘He is
such a dear old thing.’ Ho has a tongue of night-
ingale sweetness and an Asiatic gift of story-
telling. His little anecdotes, helped out by an
extraordinary play of feature, are delightful, and
one can t preach a sentiment that he can’t support
with a story (and a capital one, too). His big,
plain face is a happy hunting-ground for expres-
sions that take one over the trail of the story
before bps have told it. His wife hasn’t
much to say, but she says it with a fresh smile
mounted in fresh pink and white tones—and that
tells.
“So far as nuggetty build goes, Ella Russell,
of the Musgrove co., is completely outclassed by
Williamson s ‘ Aida ’ (Lina de Benedetto), who is
short as well as stout. First glimpse of said squat
signora with her swarthy stage complexion and
brief slave-skirts slashed at -me side to afford
peeps of a burly, brown-stockinged leg, comes
rather as a shock to those who can't appreciate a
colored heroine unless she ‘walks in beauty like
the night and, though bound in black and tan, is
nevertheless an edition de luxe.
The Benedetto, to do her justice, is a live
artiste, and her looks improve on acquaintance :
but even familiarity fails to breed complete blind-
ness to her ‘too, too solid flesh.’ When she faints
on her lover s bosom she does it by instalments, as
it to gradually accustom him to the strain, while
her prop though he’s a fine figure of a man
laboriously unloads his burden limb by limb’
T other night Aida’s black father (Fernando
Lattadou), in the course of a stormy, ancient dis-
agreement, hurled his substantial child uncere-
moniously to the ground, instead of letting her
down gently. In consequence, the brown girl’s
skirts parted at the slashed side, so that as she lay
she revealed ever so much solid shin and a strap-
garter buckled below the knee. Aida took her
time to revive from her swoon, but when her eyes
did open they froze with horror on to that garter
and the peep-show was abruptly veiled to the tun 4
of a gallery-titter.

w-'n a ' tonna £ oes °n her own queenly way
Williamson mounts ‘Aida’ splendidly. The

“Signorina Marchi, Lucia’s confidante ‘Alicia,
is a fine, handsome, dark damsel in effective low-
neck polonaise of crimson satin, with petticoat
and long puffed-sleeves of snowy chiffon. This
big brunette, in her toilette of red and white,
rather out-blazes, so far us mere looks go. pale,
auburn-haired Lucy in faint, shimmering hlue.
“Later, Lucia is more imposing as the bride,
with her chestnut hair puffed out under her veil,
and she brings things to a head when she lets
down her tresses, bares her arms, and comes forth
in a chiffon night-confection with lace angel-
sleeves and much broad white satin ribbon.
“The prize tenor, so far, is Carlo Dani, who
does Edgar (Lucia’s lover). He looks well, his •
acting is quiet and impressive, and his voice most
pathetic, especially in that Churchyard song.
Men, no doubt, are barracking for tenor Larizza
of the clarion tones, but women, I guess, will pin
their fancy to Dani, ‘most musical, most melan-
choly.’
“ The Maddens with their married daughters
(one black-gowned, t’other pink-cloaked as usual)
and faithful attache Campbell, witnessed Lucia’s
first Appearance (June 6th). The sons-in-law are
apparently not taking any theatrical or operatic
family outings at present. Baby Madden, a
plump white-muslined 11-year old, brought along
to imbibe the educative influences of Italian
opera, cheered up when joined by a curled con-
temporary. Aren’t private children placid and
cabbage-like compared with theatrical ones?
Nellie Stewart’s daughter, for instance, would
enter into the spirit of an ancient Greek play, but
then, she’s the Stage incarnate and to act
or watch acting is the breath of her nostrils.
Little Nancy went to the Melb. State Con-
cert with the Command singers. At first, she
kept in the background to the extent of hanging
out of the window of the photo-room and kissing
her hand theatrically to her mother. Next she
strayed on to the orchestra platform and took a
comprehensive view of the assemblage, Finally,
she struck a pretty attitude and glued her eyes on
Royalty. Had Mrs. Cornwall lifted her finger,
the histrionic mite would probably have tossed off
some charming little tableau in the style of
‘Young Australia, proffering the only rose that
ever bloomed in her arid wastes. to the Princess
Charming! ’

Scribe herself wouldn’t like mobs of strangers
with muddy boots and sich, to paw their way
through her humble bower. But wouldn’t the
Duchess guipure quilt have made a pretty
polonaise ?
“The Yorks’ coming hardly caused the tremen-
dous excitement that the Cliveden widow’s birth-
day did on iuesday last. It leaked out by degrees
that this was Lady Clarke’s anniversary (nobody
wanted to know which), and the shelling of Lady-
smith was a mere trifle compared to the pouring
in of good things at the gun-girt mansion. At a
dinner in the evening a tactful tin-soldier fired a
vol ey of cheery congratulations at the youthfully-
built widow, whereat the relict hid her blushes
behind a tower of asparagus-fern.

M ‘‘ The singing pupils of a certain influential
Melbourne matron—the Lady Superior of the
local Wagnerian school—are not nearly so warmly
disposed towards Mdlle. Dolores as they thought
they were going to be. It appears that they were
all assembled at the home of their instructress to
meet Mademoiselle, the scribess for a Melbourne
weekly being likewise present to take down the
notes of admiration which the songstress was
expected to utter when the young ladies had
warbled for her edification. But the Dolores
evinced no desire to hear the damsels sing. They
approached her one after another, with their little
bits of music in their hands, and tried to get her
interested in the subject of their trills and top-
notes, but the smiling visitor merely nodded her
head and said ‘yes,’ or ‘very nice’ to their re-
marks and presently took her leave. Then the
damsels—unheard, unhonored and unsung—beiran
to doubt whether it was worth while to want an
opinion on their soulful Germanic vocalisin'* from
one who had only studied in the frivolous schools
of France and Italy.—Yours affec.,
AKENEHI.

A Letter from a Lady.
“ Charlton,” George-street, Norwood, S.A. '
October 1, 1900.
Dear Sirs, —
About seven years ago I contracted a severe in-
ternal complaint, and was in a very critical state.
The doctor attending me told me that if I lived I
should be a sufferer all my lifetime. His treat-
ment was quite useless in giving me relief. After
two or three years I got tired of paying doctors’
fees for no benefit, and hearing of Warner’s Safe
Cure I gave it a trial. I took, in all, five bottles,
and am thankful to state I am perfectly cured.
The medical man was genuinely surprised at such
a wonderful result. I am now as well as it is
possible to be, and give Warner’s Safe Cure the
whole credit for accomplishing what doctors
deemed impossible. Hoping that ladies suffering
as I did will profit, I am writing to you now.
My mother considered that her life was saved by
the use of Warner’s Safe Cure in a somewhat
similar complaint.
(Mrs.) ANNIE HARRIS.
Messrs. H. H. Warner and Co., Ltd.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Adelaide’s Mayor has earned for himself
an eternal nickname—“ Kissy ” Ware.
Senator Pulsford is known in the Com-
monwealth Parliament as “Aunt Emma.”
It was only when H.R.H. the Duke had
reached so far as Sydney that Melb. Argus per-
mitted itself to say “the Duke looked quite lost
in his big busby.”
Duke George entered Sydney at a walk
and departed at a brisk trot. Also, the Ophir
entered the Heads at half-speed and left them for
Maoriland just as fast as it could get out.
Cabled that the King will reduce the num-
ber of his chaplains from 36 to 12. True patriotism
this. To lessen the cost to his country, King
Ned diminishes his chance of Paradise by 66 per
cent.
Emily Soldene writes in Sydney E. News
that in London some men pay £T2O per dozen for
silk underwear—suits of vests and pants. There
is nothing that good old gourmet Emily doesn’t
get to know, sooner or later.
Melbourne Paunch mistakes Senator
O’Connor, of N.S.W., for Daniel of that ilk, and
wonders why that able and well-informed gentle-
man should ever have been regarded as a shriek-
ing joke in his own State, and hilariously alluded
to as “ Dan.”
After all, Peacock’s failure to get the ex-
pected York-visit bauble may be a blessing in
disguise. It will give him time to change bis
name, if he so desires, to “Pekoe,” which would
sometimes be a much more convenient monniker
when you come to think of it.
The Australian Bushmen’s contingents
were originally suggested not by “ Major” Randal
Carey, of Sydney D.T., as so many | eople think,
but by one Levy, or Cohen, of London. The war,
as the world knows, was for the benefit of Levy,
Cohen and Co. Hence the word Cohentingent.
That accomplished and charitable phy-
sician, Dr. Charles Alfred Dagnall Clark, M. 8.,
London, who had practised at North Syd-
ney for 20 years, and died suddenly last week,
takes with him to the grave a keen medical in-
stinct and a vast accumulation of medical experi-
ence.
Mrs. Cairns, widow of Dr. A. Cairns, of
Chalmer’s Church, Melbourne—a prominent figure
in the fifties—celebrated her 90th birthday on
May 30. The Doctor arrived in Melbourne in
1853, and retained charge of the church until 1865,
when failing health compelled him to retire. He
died in 1881.
Veteran Colonel J. E. N. Bull, who died
at Goulburn (N.S.W.), the other day, aged 94,
was the son of an officer in the British army who
fought at Waterloo. The Colonel, when Captain
in the 99th Regiment, emigrated to N.S.W. in
’42. He was warden and goldfields commissioner
at Castlemaine (Vic.) from ’52 to ’69, and was for
many years a staff officer in the volunteer force.
The N.S.W. State politicians are getting
on. One potentate (who, by the way, didn’t snare
a York-time title), when in Melbourne during the
Royal celebrations, demanded the services of an
orderly. His request was granted, and when
driving through the streets he was preceded and
followed by sword-armed mounted policemen.
The statesman in question now manifests strong
symptoms of a resolve to introduce the orderly-
system to Sydney.
WHAT JOHNNY THOUGHT.
DAD (who’s had half-a-pint and counter-lunch out): “ I tell yer, Mary,
what with the procession, and the guns, and the arches, and the
’ luminations, and fireworks, it’s the biggest thing the country ever
did see.” (Turning to the youngest.) “ What do YOU think,
Johnny? ”
JOHNNY (hungry and speaking for all): “Steak-pudding.”
His “ Serene Highness,” Teck, the dark,
Hebraic-looking young man who accompanies his
brother-in-law George on the present tour, is
alleged to be looking out for some congenial
spot where a future Governor’s billet would be
possible. The impecuniosity of the Tecks led
to their London creditors terming them the
“ Ticks,” and as the present Serene Teck is no
better off than the rest a State Governorship
would not come amiss.
Surgeon-Captain Howse, of N.S.W. mili-
tary medical staff, who has just been gazetted
V.C. (for recovering a wounded man, under heavy
fire, at Yredefort, S’Africa), is an Englishman
who has been for a dozen years resident in N.S. W.
Dr. Howse, after being invalided to England, has
now resumed practice at Orange, N.S.W.
The Duke is described in the daily press
(in big capitals) as having been “ Greatly
Touched” by the cordiality of his reception in
Sydney. But, judging by the quality and number
of his presents (mostly autographed photos) and
subscriptions, he (like his illustrious uncle, Duke
Alfred), wasn’t “ touched ” for very much.
One of the sights of Melbourne is Wax-
ellency Madden’s son and heir in the uniform of
the Scottish Regiment. The corpulent young man
is as Irish as Jameson’s Whisky, but as sufficient
genuine Scots are not obtainable the regiment is
sweeping in anyone who will consent to look ridic-
ulous in a short petticoat and hairy knees.
When Cooper and Bailey’s steam
“pianner” and circus were in Adelaide over 2C*
years ago, a man, dressed as an usher, stood at the
entrance, and in a strong Amurkan accent in
formed the public: “This way to the serkel,
ladies and gentle-men ! ” The circus-man was
John Greeley Jenkins, present Premier of S.A.
Ex-Judge Martin, of Maorjland, whose re-
cent downfall was a nine-days’ wonder and an Old
Cats’ moral lesson, has gone back again to practise
law in Auckland. Martin spent a long and honored
life in working up to the top of the tree, and there
is a certain pathos in the shingle which he hangs
out as plain “barrister and solicitor,” v en his
best years are behind him.
“J.”: “Willy” Lynch, the Melbourne
solicitor who died suddenly in the Australian
Club, Melb., a few days ago, was a great collector
of works of art. He owned several genuine land-
scapes by Turner, which it is to be hoped will not
be allowed to go out of Australia., Only one of
our National Galleries contains an undoubtedly
genuine Turner, the “ Dunstanburgh Castle,” pre-
sented by the late Duke of Westminster to Mel-
bourne ; and that is only an early inferior picture.
On the opening-of-Parliameut with-prayer
question, Senator O’Malley “suggested that it
would be preferable that the prayers should be
read by a paid chaplain. His argument was that
some succeeding Speaker might be a per son of
loose habits, and that it would be objectionable to
have prayers read by such a one.” Now, a man
of “ loose ” habits might say prayers very nicely
indeed. The O’Malley no doubt was thinking of
—there have been such personages—a Speaker of

Composition is clearly not a strong point
with the Victorian Premier’s staff. At present
the corridors leading to Peacock’s offices are
placarded—“ The Premier only receives visitors
from 2 till 5 o’clock p.m. daily.” Some people
read this as meaning that Peacock is the only man
in all creation who receives visitors between two
and five. Others that he does nothing between
two and five except receive visitors. Third theory
is that Peacock receives visitors between 2 and 5
p.m. only.
