A CONFUSING COMPARATIVE.
Taltie's limbs are limbs, but Flossie’s limbs are
limber.
Queen Victoria is said to have had a great
reverence for her Stuart ancestors —they repre-
sented the doctrine of the divine right of kings,
and no despot ever believed in that theory more
deeply than she. She never allowed the Pre-
tender to be spoken of disparagingly in her pre-
sence. When anyone tried to explain that, if the
Pretender had got his throne and the divine right
of kings been maintained, she would never
have been more than a tenth-rate German prin-
cess—not even a reigning one at that—she brought
the lid down on the discussion with a slam.
Rev. Arthur Galton, the “ A.G.” of
fugitive Australian literature and ex-Premier
Dibbs’s appointee to the post of N.S. W. historio-
grapher, is mentioned as an early visitor to
Sydney. Mr. Galton, who was nephew of the
late Gov. Duff, began as an Anglican, went over
to Rome and became a priest of that Church,
turned layman and took Duff’s secretaryship, then
turned Anglican again and was ordained clergy-
man. His official renunciation of heresies, per-
formed in Windermere church, was a most
elaborate and sensational church proceeding.
A Tasmanian writes :
It’s an open secret that Premier Lewis of Tasmania re-
fused the C.M.G.-ship thrust upon him when he was in
Melbourne at the opening of the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment. Lewis is the very first Premier actually in office who
has been offered such a paltry thing. Gavan Duffy tells,
in his autobiography, how the same indignity was offered
him when he retired from the Premiership of Vic., but
that he said he wouldn’t have it, and then a K.C.M.G.-
ship was sent along. Lewis was pressed to take the
miserable thing, and was told that the new policy of the
Colonial Office was to make men begin at the lowest rung
of the ladder of titles, and that if he didn’t take the toy
offered him he would give offence to the Throne, and so
forth.
It is generally believed in Perth that
Hackett, John Forrest’s guide, philosopher and
friend, editor of the West Australian, and part
proprietor of that ponderous daily pyramid, will be
offered a title when the little Duke reaches Perth.
If Hackett accepts the bauble he will lose the confi-
dence of the democracy in the Sand-heap, and he
has been trying hard to gain that for a long while.
Hackett would probably have headed the W.A.
poll for the Senate had he chosen to stand, but he
preferred to direct local politics from behind the
throne, and edit his paper m a large, heavy, stone-
club fashion.
“At a recent London meeting of theHooley
shareholders the trustees reported that the money
in hand represented a dividend of 3d. in the £.
The only cheerful person present was Mr.
Hooley himself, who indulged in an independent
attitude, a light suit, a white tie, a blue shirt, a
bright smile, and subsequently a broad laugh. It
is printed that Mrs. Hooley’s pet Pomeranian
Eoodle is wearing gold-crowned teeth. Recently
e became fidgety, could not eat the choice
morsels prepared for him, and was evidently
suffering great pain. The vet. was called, and he
found the dog’s teeth had decayed—the results of
eating too much candy, the doctor said. The
necessary dental work was done with very little
difficulty. Now he eats his bone with relish and
munches dog-biscuits with evident satisfaction.”
Lady Broome writes in April Cornhill
about Westralia 16 years ago. She was then the
melancholy Governor’s wife. One of her stories
relates the tragic result of giving an old Irish-
woman (a soldier's widow) a couple of shillings.
“ I gave her some tea, sugar and—fatal mistake —
a couple of shillings. She got drunk, tumbled
into her own fireplace, and was roasted to death.”
It appears that the Perth papers of the day all
printed strictures on the crime of giving money
to indigent persons. Lady Broome received some
funny epistles, most of them of the begging letter
order. One of them requested a little money “to
purchase a coffin for my daughter, as her remains
are in a ridiculous condition on the top of the
house.” She also mentions a brassy interviewer
who stole all the washing of Government House
on his way out.
Dr. Garran.
Dr. Garran, M.L.O. and member of the N.S.W.
Public Works Committee, has passed away at the
advanced age of 76. All his life a valetudinarian,
his continued activity was little short of a marvel.
When he became expansive and confidential, he
unburdened himself of the lore amassed in a life-
study of physiology. His mind was a curious store-
house of medical facts and fancies. With the
quaint insistence of the ailing, he was never tired
of recounting anecdotal reminiscences of those
who were born only to suffer. But with it all he
was cheery and buoyant, and considerate for others
to the verge almost of absurdity. His life was
veritably as clean as an unwritten page. He was
sometimes pathetically regretful that he had
nothing of literary endurance to leave be-
hind him—his life’s work was contained in
some thirty yearly volumes of the (Sydney
Morning Herald. The doctor was a native
of London, born on November 19, 1825. lie
graduated at London University, taking the M.A.
degree in the philosophical branch in 1848, at the
early age of 23. The son of a Nonconformist
clergyman (speaking after a lapse of 15 years I
may not be quite correct in this assertion), young
Garran’s school and college days were not glad-
some, as he was made to feel all the disabilities
which Nonconformists suffered in those, reaction-
ary times. Things are vastly different nowadays,
when even the awful Stead is the companion of
princes. The ship in which Dr. Garran came to
Australia in 1851 bore also the despatch conferring
a measure of Responsible Government on South
Australia. A weak chest drove Garran first to
Madeira, where he spent 18 months as a private
utor. The writer remembers the Doctor's
keen criticism of the Madeira portion of Lady
Brassey’s “Sunbeam” book, sent to him, of
course, for a commendatory notice. After he
had severed his connection with the Herald he
could afford to talk frankly about the policy and
the methods of that paper. On one occasion, lie
told the writer that he had always tried to deal
with every public subject in a spirit of strict im-
partiality, to carry out a coldly judicious method
in everything. Looking back on his journalistic
work, lie said that he then felt he had
been mistaken. The public always discounted
journalistic opinion by some 50 per cent.,
and, had he his time over' igain, he
would thunder like a modem Boanerges.
Once, he remarked, when discussing politics and
politicians (with a justifiable pride in his own and
his paper’s political influence), that old “Jack”
Robertson said to him, “ You black-bearded ,
if you liked to write the back-stairs hi itory of New
South Wales politics, you could paralyse the
world.” The Doctor’s proper name was Gammon.
He never tried to conceal the fact, but explained
humorously that Mr. Fairfax would not hear of
his paper being edited by anybody called
Gammon. The name “Garran” was Fairfax-
bestowed. But tiie change was not needed.
The Doctor could have dignified any name.
Politically the doctor never did much or magnifi-
cently. He was too much of a gentleman to
succeed in the crookedest of crooked careers. But
he took himself very seriously, and gave a grand
example to the people who will never know what
a grand exemplar they have lost. Think of a man
resigning the position of Vice-President of the
Executive Council with these words: “It would
have been wrong of me to cling to a post after I
felt myself unfit to do the whole of its
duties, and it would have been unjust to
the House.” The Doctor was proud of his
post-prandial efforts—not without reason. I was
present at a dinner when he made the one
speech of the day. Carington was the gues', and
even he forgot to yawn when Garran talked.
Subsequently, the Doctor remarked, “ I cun make
an after-dinner speech, simply because 1 have ac-
customed myself to think on my legs.” Dear old
long, lanky, ungainly figure, with dreamy, far-
away eyes, and benevolent features, with a good
head—too heavy for the columnar support, flapping
coat-tails and wind-tossed beard, Vale ! Of the
many letters of yours that I have, I cherish one in
which I have underscored this pregnant sentence :
“Do not think of going into Parliament just yet!”
F. J. BROOMFIELD.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., exact date and exact
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“ L.C.” : Far away in N.Q, there was a
certain horsey magistrate who had long had an
eye on a likely-looking grey mare belonging to
Barney Flanagan, to whom the Jaypee had
vainly offered £2O for her. Barney got run-in
one night as usual, and next morning the sporting
beak was on the Bench. There was the usual
string of charges, to which Flanagan pleaded that
he disremembered. “Now, Barney,” said his
worship, “ you are charged with being drunk,
which will be £2 if the case is proved. If the
charge of obscene language is brought home to
you, it’ll bo £5. You are accused of assaulting
the police which, provided the evidence is suf-
ficient, can’t be less than another fiver, and
destroying Government uniforms ’ll be thirty
bob, supposing I find you did it. Then there’s
conduct calculated to lead to a breach of the
peace ” (here a paper was handed up stating that
the mare was for sale at £2O), “but owing to the
confused and contradictory character of the police
evidence,” went on his Worship, without turning
a hair. “ you’re discharged with a caution.”

“T.G.” : “ Wallaroo ” (8., 27/4/’01) says
he has known outback teamsters in wet weather
to make 12 miles in three days. This is fast
travelling compared to some journeys made by
teamsters, a few years ago, over Snake Plain on the
Nevertire-to-Warren road. Once drove over this
road in a buggy, and the black soil and dry grass
stuck so fast to the wheels that I had to dismount
every few hundred yards and “shave” the tyres
with a tomahawk. Nearing Warren and entering
a lane a mile long, we passed a teamster bogged
nearly to the back of his bullocks. Our light
vehicle worried fairly well over this surface, which
was only a crust over liquid mud, and we thought
no more of the matter until, when returning, five
days later, we found that the bullock-puncher had
onl y j us fc negotiated the single mile of bog ! Not
a track was to be seen ; the mud had closed up
like the waters of the Red Sea after Moses had
got his team across.

“ Wat Tell ” : lie places at which horses
and cattle will not stay. There are others from
which it is almost impossible to shift them.
Travelling between Windsor and Wiseman’s
h erry once, at midnight, and having a nervous fit
on me, I wanted to'gallop past an old ruin that bore
a very bad reputation. Whip and spur, however,
would not move my horse past the spot, and I had
to dismount and lead him. Afterwards learned
that others had experienced the same thing at
that place—which probably some of your readers
know well.

“D.H.R,” : Was one time working on
a station, delivering rations to boundary-
riders. Noticed that one old man used to order a
lot of ointment (Holloway’s). One day I asked
him what he did with it. “Oh,” he said, “I eat
it,” and he ate some while I was there by way of
evidence that he wasn’t elongating my limb. Not
long afterwards he was found sitting against a
sapling, with his pipe in his hand, dead. Beside
him was his quart pot half-full of tea, and the re-
mains of a meal. Evidently he died very sud-
denly, whether from ointment or not I cannot say.
I met other ancients whose tastes were almost
equally remarkable. The old, lialf-demented
“hand” out back is often a curious feeder.

enteric—health gone, money gone, billet gone, home gone, poor
old dad gone, self gone, an’ —an’ — now, ’struth, girl gone! "
birth. She came out to this State when very young, and

“ W.J.8.” : Re snake-killing dogs. Was
on the MacKenzie River last August, going
to the camp with my mate one evening, when his
dog caught a snake too far back from the head,
with the result that the biter got bit right in the
throat. In less than two minutes the dog had a
fit. His owner immediately and repeatedly
immersed him in the river, assuring me that it was
a certain cure. The dog seemed in great pain
during the night, and next morning his head
was twice its normal size, and he looked
a most remarkable dog. On the fourth day
the swelling broke where the snake bit
him. After this he recovered rapidly, and in
three weeks was killing iguanas and fighting
other dogs as if nothing had happened. The
snake was a brown one, six feet long. Should
like to know if the dog’s recovery could have
been due to immersion.
More Dunn:— 4
Dear Bulletin. —Re “Te Whai’s”
inquiry (B. ll/5/’Ol) as to the fate of
bushianger Dunn. If “ Banjo ”
says that Dunn never was caught,
he is wrong. He teas
caught. Cannot re-
member the year
or the month,
but he was
captured at Dub-
bo about 35 years
ago, after a des-
perate fight with
the police, in
which he was
badly wounded.
On being cap-
tured, he was
taken to the
Commercial
Hotel, in
Brisbane-
street.
There was
no lock-up
in Dubbo at
that time.
A guard was
placed over
him, and
his wounds
attended to.
The prisoner-
patient was to ill at . .
night that the man on guard relaxed his vigilance and
slept. During the night, Dunn escaped through the
window, and, next morning, the village was in a state of
commotion, ft was not until a day later that a wood-
carter rode into town and informed Inspector Hogg, of
Bathurst, who was in Dubbo at the time, that a wounded
man, hiding in a hollow log near the Gravel Pits, had
asked him for a drink of water. Thirty police were soon
on the scene, armed all round like a British gunboat, and
Dunn was finally captured. He was sentenced to death
at Bathurst, but whether the death-penalty was carried
out I can’t say. I am inclined to believe it was. —T.G.F.

“ D.H.R.” : Re Jap. and alleged shark in
Barwon (B., lG/3/’l). This may have been, as
“ Vril ” remarks, a Murray cod. Present scribe’s
father caught one at Mogil Gully, on the Culgoa,
in 1856, weighing 951b. Also saw skeleton of one
on Williams’ station, on Warrego, fifteen miles
above Cunnamulla, which was said to have been
1541b. weight, and certainly looked it. Have
known fish to be caught at Walgett ranging from
751b. to 951b.
“ D.H.R.” : Re effects of ring-barking.
Buff, in “The Physics of the Earth,” says:
“ Forests are the natural gatherers of moisture
from the air. Trees loosen the soil, shelter
the herbage, and hinder the water, on sloping
ground, from running off. They check the
currents of air, and thus retard the carrying
off of the moisture by drying
winds.” Also, because the
water, sucked up from the earth
by the roots, is breathed out
again by the leaves as vapour,
the air in forests always remains
moister than where there is no
timber. The Canary Isles, which
were once forest-clothed and fer-
tile, have been rendered barren
by reckless denudation. Witness
the same in Australia.

why birds “ tower ” when an eye is destroyed by
gun shot ? Have known a wood-duck so injured
go up hundreds of feet with a spiral motion, then

where it rose ; so near shooter as to allow of killing
with a stick. Sole wound on bird, loss of an eye.

“Lar.”: Knew a teamster’s strapping
daughter, out-back, the eldest of a large family of
girls. She was the old man’s right hand, and
when a beast was killed Mary Ann was “off-
sider.” It was a sight to see her and the dad
outting up the carcase—swearing at each other
the while, with their knives in their teeth. This
wench could also do “fencing” with any man in
the district; could kill, dress, aud cut up a sheep
in style, and when it came to scrub-cutting, or
ring-barking, or heading a beast —as the damsel
said herself— “she could run blanky rings round
every cove on the run.”

“ D.H.R.” : lie eaglehawks. Was one
time minding sheep, when I noticed an eaglehawk
sweep fiercely down, as if to seize something.
Went across and found bird and big gohanna en-
gaged in an active disagreement. Eaglehawk had
seized gohanna, but gohanna had got his tail
round hawk’s wing and was manfully holding up
his end of the hostilities. After a twenty minutes’
struggle there was one hawk less.


It is hard to understand how it pays any-
one to patent small articles. M‘Lean Bros, and
Rigg, Ltd., have brought out a handy little thing
which they sell under the name of the “ Duchess
Collar and Cuff Drier.” No one who sees it but
would buy, for the convenience is undoubted, and
the price is only sixpence. They must expect to
sell them by the thousand, but even then, how
does it pay ?*
CHILDREN
now-a.-da.ys have notoriously BAD TEETH,
because the White Bread they live on lacks the
Wheaten Phosphates (rejected with the Bran)
which go to make good, sound Teeth, as well as
Bone, Brain and Nerve substance.
CEREBOS SALT, used like common salt,
supplies these necessary Phosphates, and is,

Farming Under Federation.
“Under the settled,Federal tarriff, fanners win
have the markets of Australasia opener! to them.
This was the gist of the information a special
reporter gleaned whilst travelling through tiie
Singleton district. T . ,
“Yes/’ said Mr. William Long, farmer of
Warkworth, near Singleton, “our prospects are
undoubtedly brighter, and I now intend to follow
my business more actively than ever before, and 1
am thankful to say I can do so. ' „
“Was there a bar to your activity before'
asked the reporter. ~ ’ T
“ Yes, indeed, there was,” said Mr. Long.
“During five years, and until lately, I suffered
from stomach and digestive troubles, pains in
the back and bowels, and heavy, t nobbing
headaches. Farm work make my ailments worse,
which crippled my activity. T tried one
medicine after another without effect, until 1
read how a man who had suffered just like me
had been cured by Dr. Williams’ pink pills for
pale people. I sent for a supply, and began
taking them. Slight relief came with the first
few doses, and two boxes completely cured me.
I never have a heartache now; the ,pains in my
back and bowels have disappeared, and I feel
stronger and more energetic than I have done
for the last few years. My ill-health had always
been a drag upon my operations, but now, with a
good season, bright prospects are assured. I am
convinced,” said Mr. Long, in conclusion, “that
with renewed health and strength I can materially
extend my operations with increased financial
benefit.”
Probably, Mr. Long s troubles were caused by
weakness of the digestive organs, so that the
undigested food, when passed from the stomach,
caused irritation of the bowels. Dr. Williams’
pink pills, by enriching and purifying the blood,
supply the digestive organs with the requisite
strength to perform their duties, thus removing
the causes of bowel troubles. They also cure
anaemia, debility, scrofula,, rickets, consumption,
effects of fevers, etc. By toning up the nerves,
they cure St. Vitus, dance, neuralgia, sciatica,
nervous exhaustion, etc. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers and by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine
Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per box.

Referring to incidents at the Royal
picnic near Cambooya station (Queens-
land), Granny Herald, in the exuberance
of her veneration, gives us such informa-
tion as this:— *■
Viscount Crichton rode well.... “l his
shirt sleeves he could scarcely be distinguished
from a drover, and rounded up bullocks in
first-class style. He soon knocked one horse up,
and called for another.
Knocking up mounts in quick time ap-
pears to be the Herald’s idea of first-
class horsemanpshi. Further on:
There was no shamming about this picnic. It
was carried out ... in thorough bush style.
The tablecloth was laid on the grass. . . • u
of the visitors brought up a silver teapot . . .
much to the disgust of a station-hand. . . .
Who ever saw a tablecloth and a silver
teapot at a true bush picnic ? And where
was the billy can ? We are also informed
what their ladyships sat on, and that
“ the Duke was seated on a rough bare
log.” Has that log been preserved ?

It’s a curious sight in Cairns (N.Q.)
to watch the Salvarmy, led by a she-
captain, with three Kanakas, a dap, and
a nondescript yellow man (latter beats a
drum, the only instrument), inviting
those tired people to get saved. N° r "
manton goes one better ; the Army there
is composed of the postmaster, his wife,
son, and two daughters, and a Govt, in-
spector of something or other. Up Worth
a man must take to drink or to religion.

South Australia is having the
driest autumn on record. Adelaide has
had under 4in. of rain this year, and the
farming districts in the lower North still
leas. Though most recent seasons have ended
gloomily for the wheat-grower, no other tor M
years has opened so badly.
“Will. J.” : Was talking to a Carlton
man recently who was trucked along with 300 un-
employed from festive Melbourne to Birchip,
where the water-scheme, or a portion of it, is
still in progress. Said the 300 (or thereabou ts)
were still shovelling at that place, though many,
including himself, had left in debt. The business
was piece-work. Old Govt, hands could struggle
up to ss. a day, but others, again, weren t making
half that. He was ready to jump a farm billet tor
10s. and tucker, so that he could get back to town
MUSGROVE'S CONDUCTOR.
again per rail. It is whispered about, that all
sorts and any sort were chosen (and trucked free)
just to clear Melbourne for the advent of royal
Jawge.

“ E.H.M.” : Lingo referred to by “Peter
X.” (8., 4/5/'01) was introduced into Queensland
from Sydney by some boating men in 1890, and
known as the “lotus language.” Writer and six
others spoke it fluently at the Boys Grammar
School, Brisbane, for nine months, before the key
was discovered. The jargon was afterwards
familiar to every guttersnipe in Queen-street.
THE LATE DR. GARRAN.
Photo, by Hubert Newman, Sydney.
At Peak Downs (Q.) recently, a cheerful
drunk, with a long heave to starboard and a short
roll to port, dropped himself down an abandoned
shaft 70ft. deep. A rescue party was soon on the
spot to recover the corpse or half-ditto. .Tones
went down, “Did you shove me into this-
hole?” queried the corpse, as Jones landed on
it. “No, I didn’t.” “Well, give me hold
of that rope till I get up and knock blank out of
the cove that did,” and up he went without scratch
or ill. Next thing Jones heard away down the
shaft was the sound of war on top, where the
saved one was wading in at his rescuers. The
luck of drunks and children !
“ Wrinkler ” re
bushman’s keep
I have also been in' sur-
ve3'-camp, and agree with
your writer (25/5/’Ol, p.
14). The expensive item
now is meat. Sugar was
formerly the highest
item. But surveyors get
special rates for sheep
and beef. Some —not-
ably one I was with -will
not take them under the
market value, in case of
having to rasp the lessees.
But a bushman, if he can
eat rabbits, fish, ducks,
wallabies, galahs, cocka-
toos, native companions,
&c., need not spend more
than 3s. 6d. per week
(provided he - can cook).
If camped near a slaugh-
ter-yard or station, for
legging a few sheep, and
helping to run up a bul-
lock and clean the offal,
a man may feed self and
dogs and he thanked for
doing the amiable. lam
now on the track in Vic-
toria. If I were to tell
you of the stacks of
tucker obtainable at one
class of resting-place, for
cutting a few sticks of
wood, you would make
parenthetic remarks. I
did half-an-hour’s work
on Saturday. This is
Monday, and I have whips
for Tuesday, and so have
six others i There ! Just
off to swing the axe for
another 30m., in case of
rain.

have I struck ? ”

THE MAN ON THE BRIDGE: A PICTURE TO KEEP TOBY UP TO THE MARK.
And for ever and for ever,
As long as the river flows,
As long as the “ayes” outnumber
The Opposition “noes,”
The moon and its broken reflection
And its shadows shall appear
As the symbol of Fortune smiling
On its wavering Toby here.

The coupon system is attracting great
attention in Melbourne. The largest deputation
on record, consisting of several hundred members
of the public (including a large number of ladies),
waited on Mr. Peacock on May 30th to advocate
the merits of the Co-operative Coupon system and
to urge its continuance. The various speakers-
represented all classes of the community, and all
were unanimous regarding the great benefits
which the system conferred on the people.*

POLITICAL
POINTS.
Of the seven supporters of ex-N.S.W.
Min ister Cook’s amendment on the Address-in-
Reply in the House of Reps., five were N.S.W.
men.
Queenslander Bamford coins the first
fitting phrase for the creed of the Commonwealth
Parliamentary Labor Party : “ I am a fiscal
atheist.”
Wanted, a Parliamentary Commission to
fully investigate and report upon the solicitor
Heydon-Moss-N.S.W.-Supreme Court-affair. The
results would probably make very brisk reading
indeed.
“ Are you in favor of the Early Closing
Act ?” is a test question for N.S.W. State candi-
dates ; and probably the wriggly candidate will
answer, “ Yes, with amendments.” -He dare not
say no, and yet he wants to ring in “ No” voters.
Electors should demand “ What amendments ? ”
Just look at this, now !
London, Wednesday afternoon.—Australians in South
Africa are strongly urging the selection of Major-General
Pole-Oarew as Oommander-in-Chief of the forces of the
Australian Commonwealth.
Since when has the four-and-sixpenny Boer-slayer
constituted himself advisory board to the Federal
Govt. ? And—in confidence —who paid for that
cable ?
Why should not some bright, well-edu-
cated, liberal-minded young Australian, who takes
no stock in the contentions to which the two
stodgy present candidates (Jessep and Alfred
Allen) are addicted, cut in for the State election
for Waverley (Sydney)? That so inexpressibly
dull a pair of politicians should continue to hold
between them a permanent lien on such a con-
stituency is one of the curiosities of politics.
The pro-Kanaka crowd in Queensland are
displaying feverish anxiety to get in as many as
possible of their cheap and reliable brown savage
ere the Commonwealth puts its slow foot down.
During last three weeks four “ blackbirders ”
arrived at the sugar ports in Q, bringing 350
slaves for Cowley’s friends. One “recruiter”
returned riddled with bullets. Another sign,
probably, of the islander’s anxiety to get to the
sugar plantations. Presumably it was the dis-
appointed niggers who weren’t allowed to come
who did the shooting.
BULL'S ATTITUDE.
“ The news furnished yesterday that the Royal assent had been refused to the Sugar
Grants Guarantee Bill passed by Queensland Parliament, because of the insertion of a
clause prohibiting alien labor in the sugar factories, has caused dismay amongst some of
the Q. representatives. They are inclined to think that either the influence of the sugar
interests has been brought to bear to block the Bill, or that the Imperial Government
views with disfavor the adoption by Australia towards the Japanese of any attitude re-
sembling hostility or the passing of discriminating legislation against them. Supposing
the latter assumption to be correct, members are anxious to know what view the Imperial
Government may take of legislation that may be passed by the Federal Parliament on the
game subject. Japanese, they contend, must be dealt with the same as other aliens, for
they are considered to be as great a menace to the well-being of the Commonwealth.”
—Daily Paper.
BULL : “ Won't live with him!’ you unchristian young pup! I tell you
you will, and must, for I have to keep sweet with his father! ”
July 3 has been fixed for the N.S.W.
State General Election. When he made this
announcement Premier See had only settled on
his candidates in 50 electorates out of 125, and so
has not left himself too much time. Out of the 80
odd members counted on as supporters of the
Lyne Ministry not 40 are “selected” as Govt,
nominees so far. Eight members of the old party
are now in the Federal Parliament, and the Labor
Party, which the See crowd is not opposing, holds
about 20 seats. So that the present Ministry will
have to secure a large proportion of new blood in
order to hang on to its billets.
S.M. Herald states that N.S.W. Com-
missioner Oliver, in reporting upon the sites sug-
gested for the Federal capital, selected “the
Canoblas, Yass, and Bombala ” as, in his opinion,
tho three most available. This would be true if
the Herald let people know that Mr. Oliver
emphatically placed Bombala first. The Herald
being a Sydney importers’ organ, isn’t taking any
Bombala, but seeks to Canobble public opinion.
A point stressed by the real White Aus-
trahan Commonwealth legislators, and proven to
the hilt by first-hand evidence from the pearl
fishers, is that the white divers desire immediate
protection, not from the Jap-Malay and other
brindled workers, so much as from the wealthy
mean-white syndicates who import and employ
these cheap aliens. If Australia is to be made
white without bloodshed the Fatman’s God of
Cheapness must be severely sub-edited.
At a recent half-hearted welcome to some
returned South African battlers at Wellington
(M.L.) Premier Seddon vociferously yelled for an
Eighth Contingent; but, instead of applause, the
only response was the whistling of a faint breeze
down the narrow street. Since then he has left
the subject severely alone. Even the Jingo press
of Maoriland cries out now, “ We’ve done enough
for the Empire, &c.” The Fat Man is beginning
to squeak about “ the scarcity of labah,” and the
Fat Man is an advertiser.
According to a list published some little
time ago nine candidates were announced for the
Marrickville seat at the coming N.S.W. State
election ; nine for Paddington ; eight for Balmain
North ; seven for Balmain South ; five for Denili-
quin ; five for Condobolin ; five for Glebe ; five
for Lismore ; seven for the Manning ; six for New-
town-Camperdown : six for Raleigh ; ten for Sher-
brooke ; seven for King (Sydney); eight for Lang
(Sydney); eight for War ringah; seven for Young;
and so on. What a prospect !
So far, the Commonwealth Parliament has
not discovered its clown—or rather its two neces-
sary clowns—in the House of Reps.—one for each
party. An attempt was made to fasten the office
on King O’Malley—his big hat and spread-eagle
ways enticed the unwary—but they soon found
that they were trying to appoint a bull-ant to a
performing sheep’s billet and dropped the O’M.
in haste. In the Senate equal uncertainty pre-
vails ; but it is hoped that the cap and bells will
find a wearer shortly. At present, those articles
are hovering in the air.
Parson Ronald (Federal M.H.R.), a
liberal-minded preacher of much good horse-sense,
recently asked a North Melbourne audience what
justice there was in depriving the ordinary old-
age-pensioner of his 7s. or 10s. a week because he
drank, while Victoria pays £340,000 a year in
large pensions to retired Judges, civil servants,
&c., and allows them to drink themselves into a
sudden and violent tomb without question if they
feel that way. The Age, which loathes Ronald,
said that he shrieked and perspired while saying
these things, but it didn’t attempt to controvert
them. And history is full of the names of good
men who perspired occasionally. Dry-skinned
men aren’t necessarily the wisest.

If N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick wants to
put an end to something that is growing into a
particularly odorous scandal, let him keep a strict
eye on the granting of “improvement leases” to
squatters—the very newest piece of machinery
for getting the best of the selector.
A rumor that the pay of
Federal members might be
raised to £700 a year roused
a bitter protest from Mel-
bourne Trades Hall on the
ground that the increase would
absorb £44,000 a year—equal
to work for 440 men at £2 a
ir-nn ’ or a 8' e pensions for
lbJO persons at 10s. per week.
And on the same principle, if the
State replaced 1000 experienced
engine-drivers at £4 a week by
boys at 30s. a week it would save
£130,000 a year, which would em-
ploy about 1300 men at £2 a week
only the State would probably
lose £500,000 a year in accidents
and damages. It would pay
almost any State in Australia
to hire the best man available
to be Premier and Treasurer at
£44,000 a year for his single'
salary. A financial genius could
save N.S.W. alone about £500,000
a year without laying extra bur-
dens on anybody—but the country
pays a cheap rule-of-thumb man
to drive its State engine, and his
way of driving it is commensurate
with his salary.
A question has arisen in Maori-
land as to whether there is such a thing
as criminal—apart from seditious and
blasphemous—libel in the province. The
Criminal Code of 18915 contains no refer-
ence to criminal libel. It, however, includes
a saving clause to the effect that any laws not
specifically repealed and not inconsistent with
the Code shall still bo valid. The English
statute dealing with the subject was adopted
in M.L., and is still unrepealed. The point
was raised in a recent case in Wellington. When
the defendant, who was unrepresented by coun-
sel, was called on, Stout, C.J., advised him to
plead that the indictment disclosed no
crime. The prisoner did so, and
Stout declined to give a decision, and
referred the matter to the Court of
Appeal.
Dear Bulletin.— Touching Fe-
deral site and water supply. May I be
permitted to point out that Bombala has
a water catchment which would supply a
greater population than even London, and this
without any engineering. The water is simply
there. It can’t be nearer than there. —N.
Hutchinson.
Dear Bulletin. —Apropos of Premier
See’s proposal for a Convention to draft anew
Constitution for N.S.W. How would these prin-
ciples do:
1. Abolish the Legislative Council, and have but one
Legislative Chamber, on the lines of the existing
Assembly.
2. Substitute for the ornamental and useless nominee
Governor of existing system a Governor elected by the
whole State.
3. Give the Governor the power of veto on all Bills and
on expenditure.
4. Give the Parliament power to take a referendum
upon all Bills and expenditure vetoed by the Governor.
5. Let Ministers be elected by Parliament.
Yours democratically and economically.—T.F.R
Premier Lewis got back to liis Flyspeck
to find trouble awaiting him. There was no end
of a row in Tas. Assembly over the appointment
of Hudson from Maoriland as general manager of
railways, vice Back, resigned and gone to
Queensland to manage the Chillagoe mine and
railway. Minister of Works Mulcahy, in whose
department the appointment lay, met with
a heap of abuse in the House for not having
appointed Engineer-in-Chief M‘Cormick, who was
also a candidate; but the fact was that Premier
Lewis and Mulcahy wanted to appoint Mac.,
while Treasurer Bird and Chief Secretary Collins
held out for Hudson. The Govt, won by eight
votes on a motion of censure moved by R. C.
Patterson, Mac.’s old chum in the S. Australian
railways.
When Kingston was at the height of his
power in S.A. five years ago the local National
Association couldn’t fight him even in the country
districts. Kingston, a wonderful worker and
born organiser, campaigned almost every town-
ship with his speeches on “Current Topics,”
worked up a majority of at least 12 in the popular
House, and once even had a Liberal majority in the
Council. As the result of recent elections the
National Association has put four Tories into the
Property House in place of Baker, M‘Gregor,
Charleston and Kingston, and three in the
Assembly in the place of Batchelor, Holder and
Glynn. The Stomach Party now absolutely con-
trols the Council and is gaining tremendously in
the Assembly, and yet S.A. democracy only needs
an enthusiastic, bold, brainy leader with the
pluck to lead.
About S.A.’s recent by-elections :
Semi-millionaire Brookman and wealthy tobacconist
Dixon, nominees of S.A. Toryism, badly beat two Labor
candidates on June Ist, and are new additions to the
steadily-accumulating Ass. Party in S.A. legislature.
Keogh, a spurious democrat with a mixture of radicalism
and toryism in his programme, defeated Labor
candidate Healey in West Adelaide, and in this district
two doubtful representatives now succeed such big
guns as Kingston and Batchelor. All the successful
candidates are advocates of private control of tramways,
and the Snow party, which spent much money in
securing their election, gleefully anticipates eventual
support from all three. The Labor and Democratic
party, once solid and enthusiastic under Kingston, sorely
needs reorganisation under an active and determined
leader. Kingston’splacehasneverbeen filled. Theonlyable
democrat in S.A. is Jenkins, but he has remained "silent
at a time when active advocacy of Liberalism was badly
needed. The old-time Kingstonian majority in S.A.
Legislature is fast disappearing, and even S langdon y
thon’s self-ADVERTiSBR seems preparing to rat in la
coalition. A significant indication of the * at
lukewarmness of the masses is the very hght polling »<<
the just-concluded contests. S.A. Liberalism badly
a leader, but he isn’t in sight.
A FITTING MEMORIAL.
DONTCHERKNOW ESQ. (loq.) “I don't care for
anything hut women and horses. I’ve given
instructions for my skin to be made into a
side-saddle after I die.”
Next month will see the N.S.W. Old-Age
Pension Scheme in operation. Most of the ma-
chinery is yet in a state of chaos, even the ap-
pointing of the men to deal with the heavy job
not having yet been accomplished. The chairman
of the Old-Age Pensio.i Board was recently
gazetted as Mr. E. Hanson, Director of
Charities (charity and old-age pensions are
still indissolubly connected in official
minds). Gazettes and proclamations don’t
count for much in N.S.W., and a few days later
another Chairman was selected, in the person of'
Mr. Noble, of the Registrar-General’s Depart-
ment. All the district boards have to be finally
chosen, and by the time the original applications
have been attended to, and the pensions are avail-
able, a goodly percentage of the aged persons
applying will be dead.
Rev. Father Timoney (Anny-chaplain) in
a letter to Sydney Catholic Press :
We were preparing to encamp when the crack of a Lee-
Metford was heard. One of our troopers fell to the
ground. Lieutenant Waters, of Tasmania, approached
the trooper to inquire what had happened, when the
poor young officer shrieked a.s he fell dead. Lieutenant
Sale, of West Australia, advanced towards Waters’ body,
when he, too, fell wounded, and I am afraid mortally.
Five horses, one after another, were killed. At last the
men became mad with rage, and rushing forward they
saw a young Boer lying in the grass. Twenty bullets in
an instant had pierced his body. The Boer had one of
our rifles, and had fired the last cartridge in it, and the
last he was to fire in this world. Very little is known
about this daring Boer. He was seen leaving town in the
morning with a blanket and a rifle. He said he was going
to scout on foot for a change. He had evidently made
up his mind to die, and he certainly sold his life for more
than its price. Very great indignation was aroused
among the men, who, after he had long ceased to live,
used the bayonet in a most shocking manner to disfigure
and rip up the corpse.
Such are the things that don’t appear in the daily
papers.

from the above dreaded complaints. lam a painter by
trade, and, thank God, 1 am able to resume my occupation
without any return of my former illness. lam well-
known in and around this district, and all my friends
congratulate me on my speedy recovery, as they all ex-
pected me to succumb, after being so unsuccessfully
treated by some of the best medical faculty that Sydney
could produce.
For the sake of suffering humanity I present you this
grateful testimonial, trusting that some poor sufferer may

see me at the above address. Wishing you prosperity in

the glad tidings to poor suffering humanity,
I remain, dear Sir, yours ever grateful,
SAMUEL SEARLE.

Who Samuel Byrnes is.
The writer is glad to take the hand of Mr.
Samuel Byrnes and give it a hearty squeeze.
That we are parted for the moment by ten
thousand miles of seawater doesn’t count. May
you live right along and prosper, Mr. Byrnes.
In this grumbling old world, more full of aches
and pains than an American water-melon is of
black seeds, it is jolly to hear a man sing out, “ /
am first-class every way ; and, as for my health, it
couldn't be better."
This is great, especially when we understand
what went before it. For several _ y’ears Mr.
Byrnes was in bad form. Dyspepsia it was, and
a very nasty variety of that abominable complaint.
He got but little sleep —so he writes—and was in
pain most of the time. He called in the doctors,
one after another, and asked them what he was to
do.
They agreed on the main point, and they were
right. Indigestion, liver disorder, and the nerve
troubles which are thrown in as make-weights—
the doctors said these things once got rid of, our
friend would be all right.
And they did their best to bring it to pass —
these worthy men. They gave him drugs—the
same, no doubt, that have been so often and so
vainly given.
“After the doctors gave me up,” says Mr.
Byrnes, “ I tried everything I could think of, or
others recommended to me. At first, I felt sure I
would come upon something helpful, but I never
did, until somebody told me about Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. Even after reading what was printed in
books and papers, as to the merits of this prepara-
tion, I still shook my head.
“ ‘ Not likely to be any better than the rest,’ I
said; ‘the chances are all against it.’ For, you
see, my heart was, as you may say, down in my
shoes, and I was not in the mood to take hope
from any testimony that could be produced.
“All the same, I began taking the Syrup; I
don’t know why. The good effect was almost im-
mediate. I stopped casting up my food, and com-
menced to feel stronger and better. Without
troubling you with the story of how I got on step
by step, I will merely say that the medicine
seemed to build me up, and put me together bit
by bjt, until I was sound and well as any man
wants to be.
“I have lived here sixty-one years, and many
people in this neighbourhood know what I have
said to be true, and were as much astonished at
my recovery as I was myself. lam now seventy-
one years of age, and hale and hearty'. For this
wonderful blessing I thank God and Mother
Beigel’s Syrup.
“ As the reader looks at my signature and says,
‘Who is Samuel Byrnes?’ I present him my
compliments, and reply that, if we ever meet, I
shall be glad to tell him by word of mouth much
more than I have written, and to testify all day
long for the remedy that made me the man I am.”
Samuel Byrnes, Lemon Grove, Penrith, N.S.W.,
September 1 st, 1899.
Apparently, the war is only just beginning!
It is over a year since Bobs entered Bloemfontein,
and shortly after the Free State was alleged to be
completely “ occupied.” Now the cable tells that
Boshof, only 70 miles from Bloemfontein, has been
relieved after a long siege by the Boers, the garri-
son, for the last two months, being on half rations.
To declare that Australians are not musical
is, to say the least of it, rather extreme. Just
take, for example, the well-known music books
published by Allan’s, of Coll ins-street, Melbourne.
They were only started a few years ago, but the
all round sale has risen to something like a
hundred thousand copies every year, we
are getting four new numbers, viz., 53 to 56. No.
53 contains fourteen popular items for American
organ or harmonium ; No. 54 consists of twelve
easy pianoforte solos, each named after an
Australian wild flower; No. 55, which is al
pianoforte number, contains eight operatic so
including dear old “Maritana,” “ Trovatore,
“ Lucia,” “Bohemian Girl,” etc. ; and No. 56 is a
capital collection of pianoforte duets of medium
difficulty. These books are only Is. each, and all
persons whom they may concern are invited to
send to Allan’s for a catalogue. The prodigality
of musical firms in the matter of catalogues is
quite alarming.
The deaths of three Vic. old-age pen-
sioners were reported on Monday last week. Two
of the cases seem calculated to alleviate the
Treasurer’s alarm re the spread of the old-age
pension list. William Brown, aged 80, was an
inmate of the hospital, or other “ home ” at Ingle-
wood at the time when his pension was granted,
whereupon he went forth, a gentleman at large,
and camped in a hut until the police took him to
the hospital to die of general want of attention.
Tom Dee, of Heathcote, had a wet and joyful
time with his pension, and was found defunct in a
Daddock. Dan Delaney, of Trentham, “ was
found dead in a sitting posture in a hut.” A pen-
sion had been granted to poor old Dan, and the
first instalment was due on the day he ceased to
feel the need of it. These three cases in one day
ought to silence even the Akgus protests against
the “ reckless ” character of the O.A.P. system.
The Tory doctrine that pensions would be re-
quired for an ever-growing army of deathless
ancients is not borne out by facts. A sudden 10s.
a week income kills some of them, and doesn’t
appear to cure the others.
“ H E.” : “Second week’s debate of the
Commonwealth Senate brought out some of its
bigger guns, and oh ! what a contrast to the old
Vic. Leg. Council which used to meet in the same
chamber. Even with the bores it possesses to
lower its average, the Senate is miles above either
of the Vic. State Legislatures in all-round ability,
and as far as democratic sentiment is concerned,
it leaves both standing. The men of the lowest
calibre, strange to say, come from the States
which had most to choose from, and when Millen,
Pulsford, Mary Jane Gould, Walker, Fraser,
Zeal and Sargood are excluded from the Senate,
it is a body of which Australia might be proud.
When one listens to the tone of the debates in this
chamber, the fatuity of the Labor parties who
opposed Federation because of the Senate,
becomes truly manifest. Its speeches are more re-
miniscent of the sentiments of the Trades and
Labor Councils than of any other bodies. It is also
a straight-out fighting chamber, and to listen to
the utterances, Ministerial and Opposition alike,
on the subject of the Kanaka, it is only necessary
to close one’s eyes to imagine somebody is reading
the leading articles from The Bulletin. The
Senate does not rest content to say ‘Kanakas
must go,’ it says ‘ They are going ’ ; and through-
out tne debate were thinly-veiled threats to
England on this and the High Court question.
The speeches of Play ford, M‘Gregor, Dawson,
O’Keefe, and Keating, the smart young politician
from Tas., all seemed to breathe the sentiment
that colored labor was to be kept out of Australia
‘no matter what happens.’ In the face of such
strongly-expressed views, Sargood, who is never
very fond of a fight, was discreetly quiet;
Simon Fraser made a blundering interjection
every now and again, to be floored by the Speaker
on every occasion ; and little Zeal, ixe the cap-
tured Soudanese, apparently went over to the
enemy, for he did nothing but make strongly
Protectionist remarks and cheer the revolutionary
sentiments of men who would have been torn to
pieces in the old Vic. Leg. Council. On the fiscal
question, tne majority of those who sit on the
Ministerial side have announced themselves as
‘ fiscal atheists,’ but they are going to vote for a
Protective tariff all the same, and manage to
score off their opponents in the frequent sallies.
The Foreign-traders are solid enough on the
subject of Foreign-trade, but no two give a similar
definition of their fetish, and every other speaker
opens by demolishing all the arguments of 1 is
predecessor, and giving totally different reasons
for his own adherence. The Foreign-trade
Senators are like a score of Kilkenny cats, only a
shade more various.in their views.”

“ Nothing is more offensive than a white
. coat and vest with black trousers. — Edwabd VII.
Bull : “ What I’ve got I’ll hold.” Sam :
“ And what you haven’t got you’re after.”
Writes the American Israelite : —
Dishonest practices in business may be, as they no
doubt are, as frequent among non-Jews as with Jews, but
when Solomon Moses does a dirty trick it hurts all Jews,
while John Smith may be a great scoundrel without
bringing discredit upon Christianity.
A magistrate visiting an outlying N.S.W.
coastal locality had to journey a few miles by
water, and hired a fisherman to. pull him. Later
on, his gondolier faced the music on some slight
charge to the tune of 5s. and 4s. lOd. costs. Ar-
rived home, the beak asked what the fare was.
“Five bob and four and tenpence,” said the
fisherman, without a smile. The man of law
chuckles as he tells the yarn —“Leniency is its
• own.reward —I nearly fined him a pound.”
Loud literary shout from one Captain
Joseph, published in Rockhampton Capricornian
' (1 Wl):
The beggars (Boers) were firing explosive bullets at us,
which were cracking in the air as loud as pistol-shots.
The real explosive bullet only blows up when it
strikes something—it is the Concussion which
makes it explode—so Joseph had better try again.
Moreover explosive bullets are so rare as to be al-
most non-existent. Probably the nearest anybody
of the Joseph family got to meeting a real explosive
bullet was when Potipliar lost his temper and
blew up in Egypt.

The Bulletin regrets to have given cur-
rency (25/5/’l) to a statement that W. Munro, of
Cairns, employed white labor only to get a large
profit from sugar-cane. Munro’s labor was chiefly
Chinese. The erroneous statement appeared in
Q. WORKER.
The Australian in S’Africa and China has been
conspicuous by his resourcefulness and suddenness of
mind. To have plenty of promptness alwai's on hand is
the best guarantee of success, and this happy condition
is best attained by having a bottle of D.C.L. whisky ever
ready for one’s emergency. The man who stands by
D.O.L. alone will never fall. Taken moderately, it pro-
vides healthful support in the time of business-worry or
social-tiredness. The man who carries a flask of D.C.L.
in his pocket need not die of snakebite. Hence its title,
The Whisky of the Wise.*
The British list of Boer war casualties
ought to include the six members of the Butler
family who figured in recent cable news.
Quartermaster-Sergeant Butler, of Woolwich,
having received orders to get ready for service in
S’Afriea, went mad, declared war against his
children, killed five of them, and left the sixth
desperately wounded. Why is Butler such a
murderous name ?
If N.S.VV. Premier See really proposes to
try bis hand at appointing a batch of new
M’s.L.C. he will add one more to his many mis-
takes. The See Government is just going to the
country, and in a few weeks it will find out
whether the country wants to again entrust it
with the power of appointing M’s.L.C. There-
fore any attempt to make appointments now will
look as if See knows defeat to be certain, and
wants to snatch a few small spoils of office before
the inevitable disaster strikes him. Atkinson,- in
M. and Dibbs, in N.S.W., both tried a very
similar device, and, by way of an omen, neither
Atkinson nor Dibbs ever saw office again.
The See-ites, or Progressives, and the Lee-
ites, or Liberals, are in a difficulty over the Labor--
party in the matter of the approaching N.S.W.
elections. At last elections, the Labor men were on
the Lee (or Reid) side, and the Lee party natu-
rally did not oppose their re-election. Now things
are mixed. No one knows how the Labor men
will vote in next State Parliament. They don’t
know themselves ; the caucus will decide, but
until after the election they don’t know who will
compose the caucus. If the Lee crowd doesn’t
fight the Labor men at the polls it may be per-
mitting its enemies to get back unopposed. If it
does, it may be fighting against its friends ; also,
by splitting _ the vote, it may let a real enemy, a
See-ite, get in where he otherwise wouldn’t. The
See-ites are in the same fix. If Ministers were
elected by the Assembly it would be necessary for
the Labor men to declare themselves in favor of
somebody, but as things are—well, just listen to
Lee and See talking in their sleep.
The choice of candidates for the coming
N. State elections often partook largely of
Gilbertian opera. Frinstans: M. and N. threaten
to fight to the death for the seat of Sydney
Nowhere in the Lee interest. M. and N. are
invited to meet the committee. M. and N. rush
round the members of the committee to “feel”
their chances. M. discovers that he will be
adopted ; N. finds he is out of it. M. thereupon
states that as a thorough Liberal he will loyally
bow to the will of his party, as lie recognises that
the party is greater than the individual. N.
writes stating that, though a thorough Liberal,
he cannot surrender his independence, the noblest
birthright of every British subject. Then N.
hurries over to the committee of the Progressive
(or See) party, and petitions for its nomination,
just as M. would have done had positions been re-
versed. The two programmes being the same, M.
and N. fit either side equally well.

About the best brand for Sheep in the market
is Kemp’s Branding. It goes further, and is considerably
cheaper than any other. It will dissolve in the scour,
and should oerlainly be used by those who hope to get a
good price for their wool. E. Rich and Co., Sydney and
Brisbane, hold stocks.*
Among the sundry little “ frisks ” attributed to
the “ Jook ’’ when he was a sailor-man is that of tattooing
his royal limbs. Presumably as the giddy pictures thus
produced are warranted not to wash out, the Jook in his
rational middle age regrets that he didn’t use Tattoo Oil,
which is growing in favor among wool-producers the
world over for marking, because it can be easily washed
from the wool and is innocent of pitch, tar, lead, or any
deleterious substance. Years ago this journal remarked :
—“ The Bulletin doesn’t know whether this is a suitable
dye for the human skin, but now that the British
aristocracy is seized with a craze for pricking patterns
into its arms, legs, and other parts, The Bulletin would
like to hear of Tattoo Oil being tried upon a Duke.”
N.Z. Loan and M.A. Co., Ltd., reports for
week ended 8/6/1, a somewhat better inquiry for wheat
at *2s. Bd. per bushel for prime grain. F.A.Q., 2s. 6d. to
2s. 7d. per bushel; chickwheat and other descriptions
show an alteration on low rates. The slight increase is
said to be due to news of some difficulty experienced by
certain charterers in completing loads, and is not expected
to continue once the clearance is effected.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Nominations for the two big things of A. J.C.
Spring-rnfeet closed Monday, when 117 subscribed to the
Mile (Epsom) and 96 to the Metrop. Strangely enough,
Epsom total exactly equals last years, and Metrop. is only
9 less. December Stakes has 98 against 86 ; Champagne,
143 against 123; Derby, 131 against 107, and Leger, 123
against 103. Victorian noms. for Epsotn and Metrop.
respectively, number 17 and 18 and include top-notchers
Paul Pry, United States, Malster, Hautvilliers, Revenue,
Wakeful, Aurous and George Frederick. La Carabine
and Kinglike are in the Metrop., while the crack South
Australian, Footbolt, is on the Epsom list. Names of Sea-
horse and Record Reign came from M.L. for both races.
The home State will play a strong hand with Ferryman,
Simile, Cyanide, Parapet, San Fran, Fighting Mac., Un-
daunted, Nymagee, Kulaui, Sequence, Paradox, Rabato,
Lucknow, Gauieon, and Reviver—in both..' Watchdog
and The Chief are in the short event only, and Hautboy,
April Fool, Australian Colors, Royal Purple and Ibex are
content with the longer one. Westralian Derby-winner
Warrior II figures in both.
■ . * * * •
The two Cups nominations (closed Monday),
from the Cornstalk State total 100, and others are not to
hand at time of writing. Simile, Nymagee, Cornhill,
Kihglike, San Fran, Fighting Mac, Hautboy, Grasspan,
Lord Rudolph, Bengonia, Sequence, Paradox, Lucknow,
Gauieon and Rabato, are among those entered for both.
April Fool, Australian Colors and Lygon, favor Melbourne
Cup only. Ferryman, Cornhill, Hoop-iron, Nymagee,
Fighting Mac, Allinga, N,ewry, Beanba, Lucknow, Kenley,
Acrasia, Australia, are pretty freely distributed over the
other more important events. Caulfield and Flemington

“ Punt ” : According to Mudgee (N.S. W.) Post
Mr. Rouse put it this way at the recent settling over Gun-
tawang races : “ Mo.’s methods offer a premium to owners
to scratch or run crooked. It is only, fair that those who
have to keep and train nags should have first chance with
doubles and straight-out bets, at longer odds than the
Sublie. Owners would then back their mokes freely, and
to. would benefit by getting the strength of the various
stables.” Rats! If this practice were followed, Mo.
couldn’t open his “book” till owners have had their
“ cut.” which, like sensible men, they often put off com-
ing for till the last moment, as then ’’tis known what they
are bumping against, and whether the horse is fit or
otherwise. Besides, there’s frequently a wide disparity
between what layers and owners consider fair odds.
Punters don’t anticipate stables nearly so much now as
in the past. Bitter experience has told them to hold off
till something comes from the right quarter ; hence the
fact that Mo. isn’t finding his game quite the good thing
it used to be. ;
» * * *
“ Gun ”: If you “ follow the money ” at Melb.
Suburban race-meets these times, you’re mostly right.
Instances of what one well-known Mo. calls “ rehearsals
of form ” are so common that glaring cases pass unnoticed.
Recently Martyr ran in one race apparently without a
hope, and, a day or two later, started red-hot for Epsom
Jumper's Flat event and got home cantering. Next race
saw Pirillie responsible for something similar. There are
reversals and reversals. F’rinstance, Locksmith and
Peeress went under at Epsom, and yet on the following
Monday accounted for much stronger fields. Explanation
here is that as Locksmith only goes when he likes, ifo
matter whether the money is on or not, he happened to ■
“ like ” on the “ jocks ” birthday, and that the extra
distance of the Birthday Handicap just suited Peeress.
Still, there’s heaps of cronk running that no amount of
“ explaining” would render swallowable.
• * * *
Cabled that English Derby, last week, went to
Volodyovski. At 2 years the colt ran nine times—with three
displacements, five wins, and one second. The late Lord
Beresford had him leased then, and the owner only got
the nag back from the executors by a law suit. Volo-
dyovski started favorite. Subsequently English Oaks also
went America-wards. The winner—Cap-and-Bells—be-
longs to Mr. Foxhall Keene, who handed over the prize-
money (£4500) to English and Murkan charities. This
was a complete Yankee victory, for Cap-and-Bells—bred
and owned on t'other side of the Atlantic—had an Ameri-
can jockey up.
* * * *
Vlodyovski, the “ American,” so-called, is
an English-bred horse, owned by Lady Meux (whose
name spells Brewery, and is identified with hun-
dreds of British pubs.) trained in England, and
merely leased to an American sport. The Prince of
Wales, now Edward Rex, was the owner of Vlodyovski’s
Sire, and of his two brothers, Persimmon and Diamond
Jubilee, who each won the Derby for H.R.H. To bracket
this year's champion with Iroquois, the colt that won. the
Derby for Pierre Lorillard in 1881, is rather absurd.
Iroquois was about as American in blood as any thorough-
bred racer could be. The owner was American, and the
colt was brought to England by an American trainer, and
prepared for his engagements in a way which was then
quite foreign to old Newmarket ideas.
* * * •
“ Spare-me-Days ” : Ikey Mo, who dearly loves
to lay till the cows come home against something “ in the
bag,” cuts up very rough when the hoof is at the end of
the other leg. A Sydney owner not long since had two
horses in a race, and getting hold of a couple of notorious
tale-bearers, gave them £5O each to put on A, with strict
injunctions as to secrecy. As was expected, the tale-
bearers immediately rushed round the books, telling them
to day against B to any amount, and, as a guarantee of
good faith, kept showing the five tenners they had to put
on A. The bait took, and the owner got a big poultice at
very remunerative odds on B, who cantered home on his
own. •

Belmore C.C. (Sydney) Federal Cup (100 gs.),
with £5O from stakes, contested t’other day, fell to
veteran Rooty Hill courser, Sam Bladon, whose Brad-
don’s Blot and Beeline whacked winner’s and runner-
up’s money. Braddon’s Blot is by Leopold out of Besant,
and is a second-season dog, scaling 661bs. Last year he
got away with N.S.VV. C. A. Challenge Stakes from 118
others, including most of the best in the Commonwealth ;
and M.L. Beeline is bred the same way. She weighs
641b5., is in her third season, and has no equal on a hare.
Once “in” nothing can supplant her. In Beeline’s
initial season she gathered in N.S.VV. Oaks, Moonee
Valley Waterloo Purse, N.S.VV. Bracelet, and was runner-
up in Bendigo Plate. Last year she appropriated Moonee
Valley Plate and Belmore Bracelet.

/^^^^ orn -^ P u f?ibst Peter Jackon is at Roma
(Q-) sanatorium, under the care of Dr. L’Estrange, who
has him well looked after on a good farm. Writing to a
Sydney' friend, Peter says he now tips the beam (overcoat
and everything on) at only list. 111 b., which would make
him at most lOst. 101 b. stripped. For years he has kept
just on the 14st., and eight months back scaled 15st. odd.
Though he can’t walk 20 yards without feeling tired,
Jackson is still sanguine of recovery. Both lungs are
now affected and the digestion impaired ; but if a big
heart can pull Peter through, he ought soon to be “ on
deck” again.
•■* * •
“Old Timer”: Re par. (B. 8/6/’01) anent
English boxer Billy Smith’s death. It’s marvellous what
an outcry is made when a pugilist dies as the result of a.
boxing-contest, yet dozens of deaths may occur on the
racecourse or the football field without more than passing
notice being given them. Throughout nearly 170 years
(from 1700 to 1867) of bare-knuckle fighting, at the rate
of many fights a. week, history only records half-a-dozen
deaths of prominent bruisers. Owen Swift, a “ dead-
ring” for Roberts, now on trial in connection with Smith’s
misfortume, fought Anthony' Noon twice between 1832
and ’34, and won in 65 rounds the first time and 74
rounds (occupying 2h. 6m.) on the second occasion.
Noon never recovered consciousness, except to ask
if he had won. If the answer had been in the
affirmative ’tis odds on that he would have re-
covered, as he gave out prior to the battle that
he’d. sooner die than lose. Swift’s next victim was
“ Brighton Bill ” (Phelps) at Royston. On Dec. 11, ’55,
Madden met Jack Jones, and the latter succumbed to the
punching he received. This match was made from Jim
Ward’s house. The night previous to the battle, Mrs.
Ward dreamt that Jones was brought back dead. On
July 23, ’5O, Tommy Griffiths died after a fight with
Paddy Gill, but through an act that hasn't a parallel in
the annals of the P.R. It is said that one of the Broomes
(Harry or Johnny) wanted Griffiths to “go down,” but
he refused, and one of his seconds, bribed, placed an
overdose of nicotine on the sponge, which he put in his
principal’s mouth. Griffiths never recovered. Most
tragic of all was the death of Sandy M’Kay (the best
heavy-weight Scotland ever produced) at Sel’cey Forest,
Northamptonshire, June 2, ’3O. Simon Byrne was Sandy’s
opponent. At the end of an hour’s battling Sandy
became unconscious and died. Three years later Simon
Byrne himself was killed by “ Deaf ” Burke.

“ Spare-me-days ” : Melbourne detective Chris-
tie, who leads the army of detectives in attendance on
the Dookoyork, was a good all-round athlete in his
younger days. He rowed a good pair of sculls, and was
very handy with his fists. It was in defeating Christie
with the raw ’uns that bookmaker Jack Thompson built
up his reputation as a slogger. Latter was in partnership
with Jem Mace in Melbourne, and Mace had knocked
a lot of science into the long fellow. Thompson was
matched against Christie, and almost as soon as
the men put their hands up Thompson got a lucky
blow home, which practically ended the fight. How it
would have gone had Thompson not got the hit home
early in the fight it is hard to say, as Jack was certainly
not built to stay. Anyhow, that one slog made a reputa-
tion for bookmaker T. Without a doubt but for Jem
Mace’s visit to Australia, the latter-day Australian world-
beaters would never have been heard of. Larry Folej'
was then a natural fighter of the bull-dog order, but
under Mace’s tuition he developed into the most soienced
boxer this country has produced, and that is saying
a good deal. Foley taught Peter Jackson, Slavin,
Fitzsimmons, Griffo, Jack and Jim Hall, Mick Dooley,
George Seale, and others. Jackson in turn brought along
Steve O’Donnell, Billy M'Carth}', Billy Smith, and Dan
Hickey—all of whom are now in the States, where they
have made a name for themselves in the ring.
* • • •
Depends on how you view it. English files tell
that recently, in Paris, several bouts of La Savate
(the kicking style of fighting) were contested-, and two
“ Murkan ” boxers wound-up with an affair under Qpeens-
berry rules. A punch on the “mark” put one of them
out, whereupon the chief of police decided that there
should be no more English style in France—that Frenchy’s
method only would be tolerated. Which means that,
though it will be all right to kick an opponent’s head off,
or plunk a No. 10 foot into the middle of his"stomach,
there must be no pummelling with a pair of, heavily-
padded hands. Every man to his own taste. Likewise
every nation.

m three matches “ down under,” related by cable this

doubted and pronounced “ tail ” will render it fairly easy
game anywhere, though a few members may often make

Annual lacrosse match between S.A. and Vic-
toria will be decided on Adelaide Oval this Saturday
(15th). Victorian team has few veterans, though White
is captain as in former years. Notable absentee from
S.A. team is the 17st. champion, Julian Ayers, by far the
best goal-keeper Australia has seen. On paper, the match
is a good thing for S.A., which has six men in its team
who have been playing for years.

Saturday’s senior-grade Rugby at Sydney bristled
with the unexpected. Up to then results had been very
much in and out, but latest happenings topped all.
’Varsity and Newtown (first and second last on the list)
match looked almost any odds on the students finishing
at least a fair margin to the good. It ended without
either side notching a point, after a hot, high-tension
struggle from end to end. Western Suburbs’ triumph
over South Sydney by 13 to 9 was another big set-back ;
while North Sydney’s success against filebe (5 to 3) proved
a still greater staggerer. Eastern Suburbs, tipped at the
outset as sure absolute winners of the competition,
couldn’t do better than get away with a draw in their
engagement against Balmain, albeit the latter had
gone under in all previous struggles and was absolutely at
the bottom of the list —every other competing club having
secured at least one match, ’ “ Uni.’s” and South Sydney
are first and second in points, as named. Relative form
up to now makes it about as hard to guess at the uVi-
mate winner as to prophesy next century’s weather.

S.A. and Victoria meet at football on Melb.
Cricket Ground this afternoon (15th). Former State
sends over its best available team, the only notable absen-
tee being J. Reedman. Victoria has always held S.A.
safe in recent years, but the game has revived wonder-
fully in Adelaide of late, and the team from the pious city
will make a battle.

tician talks through his imported hat about how
Protection reduces the workers’ remuneration
with all his old energy.

Be that recent international trouble at
Tientsin in which some British infantry—attacked
“ with bayonets and bricks ” by French and Ger-
man soldiers—fired and killed one man and
wounded seven. Li Hung Chang was right when
lie said : “ Keep quiet and sit tight; the foreign
devils will soon begin to fight among themselves.”
The disastrous Bill Lyne has a scheme on
hand for taking the Federal Parliament round
bodily to look at proposed Federal capital sites.
This is almost the most awful idea in Bill’s record.
It would take at least a week—probably a
month—to inspect one site intelligently. Any
useful inspection must cover soil, water supply,
means of access, facilities for drainage, &c., over
a huge area, and all the Federal members can do
is to go at great expense to a proposed site, eat
and drink there heavily, hear certain reports
which they could have read just as well in Sydney
or Melbourne, and go away again. A whole
session wouldn’t be sufficient for any useful
inspection. The Federal Parliament has other
things to do, and if it can’t trust its permanent
offioials to supply it with information like this it
had better get new officials.
Randolph Bedford, “Randolph the Reck-
less,” doesn’t often get a paper out, but when he
does it is something to be remembered. Two
numbers of the Clarion are now on sale, the Vic-
torian number containing 68 pages and about 300
pictures. The Cornstalk number has 84 pages
and 360 pictures, all for one shilling, or post free,
one and sixpence. Victoria is dealt with from
1804 to 1900, and N.S.W. from 1770 to last year.
Within 24 hours recently Maoriland was
struck by two of the most disastrous fires that
have afflicted that much-burnt land for many years.
At 2 a. m. on the morning of May 30 Mt. Vic-
toria, Wellington, was in a blaze. The fire started
in a carpenter’s house, and, helped by the high
wind, inefficient water-supply, and the wooden
nature of the buildings, ate up 24 houses and
cleaned off nearly three acres. Wellington is built
on a fault—that is, a vast mass of very hard rock
resting on much softer, stone. The action
of the water wears and rots away the soft stone,
and when sufficient is rotted away, Wellington
slips down anything from the 63rd to the 32nd
part of an inch. Brick or stone buildings are
liable to be wrecked thereby; so most of
the houses in the windy capital are wooden.
Fortunately, no lives were sacrificed ; the worst
loss was the library of Public Librarian Rowe,
which consisted of over 5000 volumes* The
chances are that, unless someone presents
the erudite little M.A. with another, he will
pine away and die. About 1 a.m. next morning
a fire was discovered at the Grand Hotel,
Auckland ; and the ensuing scenes were hideous.
The fire blazed up the staircase, and the people in
the second and third storeys were cut off. There
appear to have been no fire-escapes, and the un-
fortunate wretches had to either bum or dash
themselves to pieces against the pavement below.
Three children of the proprietor perished in the
blaze. A housemaid was severely burned,
and then leaped from the window; she
died from the burns and from injuries re-
ceived in the fall. Mr. Fred. Ayres, of
the Bank of Australasia, also failed to escape.
The hotel was considered the finest in the
northern capital, and was being prepared for the
reception of the Ducal party. It was very old,
and went like matchwood. Indeed, so sudden
was the flare-up, that the “boots,” sitting in a
back room, had just time to rush up to the second
storey and back —he couldn’t even reach the third
floor where the three children slept—before the
fire was absolutely beyond control. Owing to the
preparations being made the place was practically
empty. What sort of a holocaust would there
have been bad it been full, as it usually is?
Presumable the Duke will now be fixed up at the
Northern Club, hard by.

Golden Horseshoe : Sydney E. News says that
Sydney and Adelaide shareholders are disappointed at the
announcement of a ss. div.—they fully expected 10s., as
the mine is believed to be making a profit of £55,000 a
month. The News is possibly ignorant of the fact that it
takes £150,000 to pay a 10s. div. on Horseshoe capital.
Last year, it made a profit of over £300,000, after allowing
nearly £20,000 for depreciation and over £21,000 for
income and duty taxes. It paid three divs. of 65., absorb-
ing £225,000, leaving a balance of £111,685 ; subsequently
another ss. div. was declared, making 20s. for the year.
Production: 67,095t0n50f oresenttothemillfor67,2l9oz.;
33,418 tons cyanided for 24,6130 z. ; 25,959 tons slimes
filter-pressed for 13,1590 z.; 10,023 tons of sulphide ore and
concentrates sold to smelters realised £75,193, after pay-
ment of smelting charges—bringing total value of gold
produced in year up to £470,627. That was the biggest
production from any W.A. mine. No doubt it
will be maintained "for some time to come for
how long, ’twere hard to say. It is much
easier for a camel to go through the eye of a
needle than for a man to go through the Horseshoe
Mine. Local newspaper men slavishly record anything
they are told about this and some of the other mines at
Hannan’s. A few months ago they reported that the
main lode was again dangerously near the Great Boulder
boundary. The shares rose £3 in face of the information,
which was probably a lie. Now the yarn is that the lode
is not only dipping further into the Horseshoe ground,
but that the main lode of the Great Boulder itself has
started to underlie in that direction. As big a mine as
the Horseshoe undoubtedly is,' it may be taken for
granted that it is not worth four millions of money, the
value represented by the present price of shares; also,
that some of the yarns recently spread about it were in-
vented for the purpose of inducing some people to over-
sell the shares when they were under 1 10, and to force
them to cover now that they are over £l3.

Hannan’s Kapai: A London co. that struck a
little rich vein outside of the Golden Mile some two
months ago. But it seems to have vanished as suddenly
as it appeared. Gable to London : “ Sample from winze
assayed Sdwt. 15gr. Frequent assays have been made ;
confining operations at present to prospecting. Con-
sidered most advisable to follow pay chute. ’’ Quite right.
Keep on the gold ; never leave it if you can help it; better
still, shut up the mine. It never should have been
opened, but that is no reason why it should not be closed.

Silver-lead in W.A. : The last discovery was
80 miles from Onslow, and numerous samples forwarded
to Perth have given satisfactory results in lead, silver and
copper. W.A. is desperately hard-up for these metals.
It would make all the difference in the world to the Fre-
mantle smelting works if they could obtain lead or copper
ores in the country instead of shipping them from
Broken Hill and elsewhere. In the absence of a smelting
base it is extremely doubtful if the works can be made to
pay, despite all the Govt, encouragement received.

“ Trotter” writes from North Queensland, and
The Bulletin takes it with a peck of salt: —“ I thought I
had seen copper before visiting Cloncurry. (North Queens-
land), but I hadn’t. For a radius of 100 miles this district
is simply one mass of ore. You can pick up 30 and 40 per
cent, stuff anywhere on the surface. Coolullah, on the
Leichhardt, and 70 miles from Cloncurry (owned by
Power Brothers) is a magnificent show, some of the ore
going 80 and 90 per cent.—large masses of copper. The
parcels (picked at random) sent from Monakoff, 14 miles
from Cloncurry, went 40J per cent., 34 per cent, and 13J
per cent. But the Hampden, 66 miles further south,
takes .the biscuit. There’s 116,000 tons of an average
grade of 15 per cent, copper, valued at £1,260,000, avail-
able for extraction. The total quantity of ore raised,
stacked and sampled on the mine up to the end of last
year was 625 tons, of an average grade of 40 per cent.,
2691 tons of 13 percent., 2271 tons of 6£ per cent.—or a
total of 5587 tons, containing a gross value in copper of
£52,811. Along the track bound for Normanton I met,
this month, five antiquated teams, travelling eight miles
a day, loaded with ore, and it makes me smile. In any
other land this country would be all life and bustle, with
a railway and a decent township. Here you have millions
lying under the sod which would give employment to
thousands of men and open up a fine pastoral district,
and the country is a deserted wilderness. When the
railway does come along this scribe ventures to gamble
that this district will be the greatest copper-producer in
Australia. By the way, some of the gold specimens I
have seen in Cloncurry are very handsome, and valued at
£4 2s. 6d. per oz.” [And The Bulletin wonders if these
Northerners, who proved so wide awake at Mount Morgan
and the Towers, are really as sleepy as “ Trotter ” takes
them to be, or if they took the stranger in.]

The British Australasian has done its best to
establish itself as a mining authority, but its best is
hideously bad. It spends a lot of money in cables re W.A.
and othermines, and devotes much space to special articles,
usually written by correspondents who have the boom
bump too strongly developed. Six weeks ago it published
a special article on the Glenfine South—that the hope of
mining in Victoria was centred on that field ; that it was
the greatest gold producer in Vic. ; that there were only
35.000 shares in the co., which at the time of writing were
quoted at £B, and that the fall of £3 was the result of an
intrusion of mullock in the lode, and concluded : “ It is
certainly the best mining investment in Victoria to-day,
and when the lode is struck at the 310 ft. the shares will
not be purchasable at twice the present price. What
will the B.A. say now that the shares are down to one-
fourth the price they were then ? Probably, it will be as
dumb as a tinned oyster.

Tarcoola, S.A. : Goods news comes from Ade-
laide concerning this field. 90 tons crushed from the
Tarcoola blocks for 2000z5., and the fact that the battery
only worked 6J days is recorded, as if that made any dif-
ference, The Govt. Geologist directed attention to the
field as a promising one eight years ago, and it is wired
that his opinion has been thoroughly borne out by the
discovery of rich and permanent reefs there. Nothing of
the kind. It is too soon to call them rich, and altogether
premature to regard them as permanent. Announced
that although the Tarcoola Blocks has probably got
the pick of the field, there are many other promis-
ing shows, and every sign of permanence is given.
Not mentioned in what way. Adelaide enterprise is
patted because in 12 months it got a battery to work on
a goldfield 300 miles from any railway. It is even
declared that the success already attained at Tarcoola
will help along the transcontinental railway, and as allot-
ments are about to be sold, the S.A. Govt, will benefit
considerably. This description only recalls others from
the same source re long-forgotten S.A. mnshroom gold-
fields.
