North Mt. Lyell: Upwards of 100 men em-
ployed at the smelting site on excavation and construc-
tion works, and much is said of the magnitude of the
buildings, etc. The plant will include eight 75-ton re-
verberatories, and a converting plant, 'an electrolytic
refining plant, and a copper sheet and wire mill. Also
proposed to establish a new industry in the province—the
manufacture of explosives.
• • • *
The versatile Horatio Bottomley is coming out
in another character—that of an author. The Sun
(London) announces that it is about to commence a new
feature, viz., regular contributions by Horatio Bottomley,
under the comprehensive title of “ The World, the Flesh,
and the Devil.” Bottomley wears the belt as the boss
boomster of the century. He is an inspired bounder.
His greatest inspiration was when he was desperately
hard-up, and conceived the idea of calling the share-
holders of the Hansard Union together for the purpose of
returning them the modestsum of £250,000 that they had
lost in the scheme of which he was the founder. Without
a bean he did it;~ami4st the applause of- the- crowd who
assembled, and some of the widows present shed tears of
gratitude. They never got any money. Bottomley gave
them Shares in His Westralian and other “wild cats,” and
they took them at such prices, and with such liabilities,
that after atimetbey realised that Bottomley’s tnaginifi-
cence was really only adding an insult to an old injury.
He has been allowed to play the same trick in other forms
over and over again, until it would seem that there are a
great many people in London that would prefer to be
without their money than without their Bottomley.
As the Critic puts it, “ The World ” probably represents
the people he has fooled, “ The Flesh ” the race-
horses which he purchased with the boodle, and “ The
Devil” the individual to whom his “wild cats” were
sent after they had done their duty for their author.

An interesting advt. by the Jungle Syndicate
appears in the latest Bull newspapers to hand. The
Jungle boodlers of Broad-street, London, invite a £52,000
subscription for the Gamelsoo Concessions in the aurifer-
ous Denkira district of Gold Coast Colony, where the
assays of ore from “numerous native shafts” show
results ranging from 6to 50dwts. per ton. As a guaran-
tee of good faith the Jungle directors (of Broad-street,
E.C.) have fixed their joint remuneration at £lOOO per
annum, “divisible as they shall decide amongst them-
selves,” plus 5 per cent, of the divisible profits in each
year after 10 per cent, has been paid on the share capital
—“ provided the total amount of such further remunera-
tion shall not exceed the sum of £5000.” The modera-
tion of these directors raises a fear that they will end up
in the workhouse. They only want 2 per cent, per
annum on the capital, profits or no profits, and not over
£5OOO a year besides !

“ Yril ” : Re copper-impregnated water at Cop-
perfield, Q. The process of settling and collecting copper
contained in the water was to fill a sluice-box arrange-
ment —about 200 feet long—with scrap iron and old tins
of all descriptions. The water ran over these and crusted
them with a coral-like formation of metallic copper—not
the green oxide. This mode of precipitation is now
dropped and the copper - impregnated water simply
pumped to waste. Scrap iron ran out, jam'tins got to a
premium, and the management not having enough capital
to fight the jam interest, which kept up the price of tins,
reluctantly closed down.

When a uniform Federal banking law is drafted,
for the sake of common sense let it abolish the publication
of “ quarterly banking returns,” and provide instead that
the full balance-sheet of every bank, building society and
public co. shall be printed in the Government Gazette,
The quarterly banking-return idea was taken from a
British law of about 60 years ago. It worked well enough
in Britain. In those days the business of most British
banks was confined to the United Kingdom, and a return
of all business in the U.K. was really a full and useful
statement of the bank’s position. In Australia the busi-
ness often spreads over several States. In many cases it
spreads to England, Maoriland or Fiji-right outside the
Commonwealth. Each State’s quarterly returns only set
forth assets and liabilities in that State, and convey no
useful information whatever. Even a return of assets and
liabilities in the Commonwealth would convey no useful
information in very many cases. The silliest feature of
all is that the return includes the amount and rate of last
dividend, and the amount of profits reserved it was
paid. Therefore, a bank like the Queensland National
or A.J.S. can keep stating the rate and amount of the
last dividend—paid five or six or seven years ago—and
the amount of its then reserves, and the State gravely
publishes these things as new and useful knowledge. And
an extra clerk is required in almost every bank to prepare
this foolishness.

Some time back, it was cabled that the
Boers had “murdered” a Dr. Walker, shooting
him three times, and then battering in his skull
Following is the account given by Capt. H. G.
Casson, South Wales Borderers :
As I was in command of the post captured at Modder-
fontein, I trust that, in common fairness to the enemy,
you will allow me to offer an unqualified denial to the
statement. Dr. Walker was hit once only, and by a stray
bullet. He died the same afternoon from the natural
effects of the wound. Every possible kindness was shown
to the wounded by the Boers. The Boer commandant
expressed to Dr. Walker his sorrow that he should have
been wounded, and the Boer General his deep regret for
the occurrence, while burghers, when conversing with
my men, also spoke to the same effect.
Will the Jingo papers, which shouted in huge
black letters about the alleged “murder,” kindly
reprint.
The Mormon apostles travelling Maori-
land in the interests of the Joe Smith push
are experiencing a bad time. Mobbed out of
Christchurch, they fared no better at por-
ridgebyterian Dunedin, where they had to take
refuge in the police-station from enthusiastic
admirers carrying a testimonial of loose bricks.
Mob waited for them on following evening, led by
a few rival fancy religionists, ripe for “ con-
troversy,” and, as the Mormons failed to appear,
the crowd turned on its own leaders, and egged
them with a large, generous hand. All this on
the Scotchbyterian Sawbath. Things got so
bad that the newly-elected Mayor and a posse of
police were sent for ; and the former, resplendent
m a new “ lum ” hat and his Jook-reception toga,
came on the scene with the Riot Act. The hat
and the Riot Act were promptly confiscated by
the crowd, and the police had to remove his Wor-
ship in some haste, lest the playful gathering
should take his shirt also.


The peace and quiet of Bunbury (Wes-
tralia) were recently broken up by the advent of
an eminent practitioner from the eastern States.
Bunbury stood him for awhile, and then one day
the local corporal of police propped up a dis-
hevelled heap of beer and mud in the police court,
and told a tale to the bench. He said the medico
never paid his board, for one thing. He had
hired an empty shop on credit and described him-
self as the “eminent hygienic specialist ” and
announced that he took phrenological and patho-
logical observations, but the force never saw him
take anything but beer, and lie took it without
payment as a rule. He offered to cure advanced
cases of consumption on the spot in the three-
penny bar, and take it out in tanglefoot, and
Bunbury was so desolated by the specialist raging
up and down its streets to heal everybody for
drink that it couldn’t get its proper rest. The
court gave the hygienic specialist three days, and
told him that unless he found honest pick and
shovel work after that period he would get some-
thing extra.

A returned Melbourne demonstrationist
informed writer that one day of the shivoo he got
outside the city proper and couldn’t find his way
back again. So he wandered around the suburbs
eating sandwiches like bricks and drinking stale
beer until his legs gave out. That night he slept
under the bushes in a private garden at Toorak.
and at dawn was chased by the head gardener, in
a pot-hat and patent-leather boots, with a rake.
He ruined his demonstration bags climbing sud-
denly over a spiked fence about 16ft. high just as
the gentleman in the hat jabbed the prongs of the
rake through his regulation straw tile. When he
reached the city the whole population was munch-
ing its breakfast on the roofs, and a bobby Bft.
high told him the streets were wanted for the
Dook, and if he didn’t go home, or climb on to a
roof, he’d run him in. He went home.

“ Melb.” : I can’t give away bed tickets
for the Salvation- Army Metropole. Deadbeats

The following incident is vouched for by
five sensible men :—A party had been staying in
the vicinity of Newcastle (N.S.W.), and during
their stay a strong attachment arose between one
of the men and a girl living at the house. It
was the last night they were to be together, and
she sang Tosti’s “Good-bye” with a great deal
of point and fervor. The next day the men went
out fishing and camped on an island. That night
about midnight they were startled to hear some
one singing “Good'bye” outside, and recognised
the voice. On going out they saw on the sand
outside their tent the print of two bare feet—the
great toe and first toe being webbed in both cases.
They reached land, and the first thing they heard
was that the girl had died that night. They told
their story to the woman who attended her after
death, and on viewing the body both feet were
found to be webbed !

“ Mr. W. G. Hearne.
“Dear Sir, —Your medicine has cured me of bronchitis
and asthma, from which I suffered for upwards of seven
years, during which period I was scarcely ever free from
coughs, and frequently the difficulty of breathing was so
distressing that for nights in succession I had to sit up.
I write you this acknowledgment from a sense of duty, as
in my case every.other treatment had failed. For a year
previous I had been getting very much worse, and at the
time I obtained your medicine I was confined to bed
suffering from a most violent cough, expectorating blood
and matter, and apparently 'beyond hope of recovery.
The first dose of the medicine gave me welcome relief,
and I steadily improved as I continued the treatment,
until I became, as I am now, quite well.—Yours sincerely,
H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney.”

return of the Asthma.”

Pis an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can be .ab-
solute masters of every phase and branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists of the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison,

for instance, whose fame is universally known-class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see ami
learn something of the real character of their wide pi ac-
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in-
dividual like the writer. .
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Gailfe
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence- department is a veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be Imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are m
1 What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “ I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder

of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of the un-
I told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this

! Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
i most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
, vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions—that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
I Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
I cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
I men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
] certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
i mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
| irig that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
I be, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
bility.
a What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent .continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why ?
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
-1 tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows.
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker w r ith a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “ Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Rioord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.

waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-

The stream of manuscript-musie that poured in on the
Jook tor approval was one of the sore trials of his private
secretary. Latter's struggles to keep his temper and yet
explain to Australia generally that the Jook wasn't a
music publisher wore pathetic.
If the quarantined Ada Reove accepts an engagement
this trip it will be from Rickards—that seems almost
certam. Rickards holds out the tempting offer of a
three-figures salary for seven appearances per week,
which mean more than 10s. a minute for all
the time she is on the stage, Ada’s health must be badly
run down if she can’t do with a little work on those
terms.

Marie Lloyd was so disappointed at her seeming failure
to get firm grip of the audience during her first nights in
Melbourne, that she proposed to ask Rickards for a
release from her £l3O a-week engagement. If she hadn’t
caught on later, she would probably have asserted her
right to be taken ill. Success is positively essential to
the business of a nervous lone-hand artist like Marie
Lloyd. In comparison with a “ star” actress in a stage-
play, she has nobody but herself to blame for a distinct
failure. She would simply sa.v “ They don’t like me,”
and cry her eyes out. '
An Australer in England writes Tins Bulletin : “ In
Newport Town, Monmouth, I found as manager of the
Empire Music All a neat and natty old gentleman of 69
years, Major Gallagher, who was through the Maori fight-
ing in 1861-65. Also in the same town encountered W. T.
Carter, ex-M.P. for Williainstown (Vic.) He was barrack-
ing the dockers and coal-miners for the Conservative
candidate, and in the face of the Coal Tax talked him into
the seat by a narrow majority. The men thought W.T.O.
a wonder—and I thought so too ! ”
The Freemasons of Parramatta entertained the Masonic
inmates of the various local asylums at the town’s Masonic
Hall. Among the guests was an erstwhile brilliant actor
high up in the Thespian world. A recital which he gave
proved that he had not lost all the power of the olden
time.
“ Adelaidean ” ; Had a shilling’s worth of “ Macbeth ”
last n'ght; .Nance O’Neil has lost some of her condition
and picked it up in ability. Her acting* was superb. The
local gods found Shakespeare very home-like and familiar
the opening line of ‘‘Macbeth 0 being “What bloody
man is this ? ” They recognised the adjective and
applauded tremendously.
George Musgrove began life selling shilling tickets, in
the pit-box, at the Melb. Opera House, under Lyster.
Musgrove’s father sold the two shilling tickets, for the
upper circle. George also had the monopoly of the opera
books. His first start in management was when he took
Lyster’s Opera co. to Maoriland.

Read the Records of Recovery.
The Case of Mr. S. W. FINNIE,
{For the Sydney Star.)
Mr. Sidney William Finnic made an
appointment for our reporter to call at
N<>. 32 Bay-street, Ultimo, one evening, so
that he would have plenty of time at his
disposal to furnish a complete account of his
wonderful recovery from a complaint which
had caused himself and relatives a great
amount of anxiety. Mr. Finnic is now a
splendid specimen of manhood, being over
thirteen stone in weight, and with a com-
plexion which testifies to the robust health
he enjoys.
“You do not look a likely subject to
speak with authority about sickness,” said
the reporter.
“No ; but if you had seen me a little
while ago, you would have had a different
opinion,” was the reply. “My direful
illness was brought on in a very simple
manner—through treading on a piece of
glass. That little accident was the pre-
cursor of subsequent complications, from
which my sufferings can barely be expressed.
My foot received a terrible gash, which
necessitated a number of stitches. I lost a
large quantity of blood from the wound, and
had to go about on crutches for six weeks.
When I could dispense with my crutches 1
found myself in a dreadfully weak and
nervous condition. I remember getting an
awful shock one day when passing a house,
with a hearse and mourning coaches outside.
It was a place where a friend of mine used
to live, and, on making enquiries, I found
that the poor fellow had died of the plague.
My nerves were incapable of withstanding
such a dreadful shook, and I went home
eeliug as cold as ice, and trembling from
head to foot. On arriving at my own place
1 was the colour of a ghost, and I quickly
fainted away. When I recovered conscious-
ness, the pulsation at my wrist was light
and fluttering, and my mind was depressed
and disquieted. My thoughts were in a
state of confusion, and I was totally be-
wildered. I had to obtain relief from my
laborious duties and take to bed, as I was
thoroughly broken down. My face became
p dlid, and wore a strange expression, with
tt.'e pupils of my eyes dilated and yellow
circles underneath. Sometimes my breath-
ing was very feeble, and my skin was cold
and clammy. When laying down 1 could
hear my heart beating against my ribs, and
I would then sit up to get a little ease. As
soon as I resumed my former position the
palpitation used to come on again. Short
stretches of sleep came occasionally, and
then I had terrifying dreams, from ‘ which
I awoke with a start in a condition of great
nervous excitement, and while still only
partially awake I had impressions of dimly-
seen objects or imperfectly-heard noises,
which became fresh sources, of horrible
images and ideas. It took mie some time to
regain my normal balance after these experi-
ences, and 1 was often afraid of my own
shadow. Then my digestion went all wrong.
No matter, how light my meals were, the
food obstinately remained heavily on my
fitomach, causing exasperating pains, which
were only relieved when the victuals were
vomited. Before being siok I used to belch
sour gases from the stomach, the taste of
which was never out of my mouth. Pains
in the back of my neck and under my
shoulders caused me a lot of misery, and my
headaches were a Bource of great torment.
Fits of dizzinesß were frequent, and when
sitting on a chair I felt as though it was
going round and round with me. Even-
tually I could not sleep at night, and the
vomiting became more frequent, leaving my
head in a whirl. All my strength and
energy had left me, and I was reduced over
two stone in weight.”
“What did your doctor think of your con-
dition?”
“ Apparently he did not know what to
think, for his treatment was not benefiting
me in any way I was afraid it was the
plague I had, and a number of other folks
were of the same opinion. In fact, when I
at length went to consult another doctor,
the neighbours were grouped together dis-
cussing my sickness, and on my return they
were still there, eagerly waiting to hear the
verdict. To our mutual gratification they
were informed that it was not the dreaded
plague.”
“ Did the second doctor manage to restore
your health ? ”
“Xo ; he was just as unsuccessful as the
first, although he gave me a great quantity
of physic. I became heartily sick of his
advice and medicine, because there was no
sign of an improvement. I had lost heart,
and did not expect to pull through my illness.
I was in this state of deepondency when I
was brought faoe to face with Clements
Tonic, w A friend sent me half a dozen
bottles, and guaranteed me a cure it I took
them I shall never be able to wipe out my
obligation to that man, for a few bottles of
Clements Tonic completely put me right. A
couple of bottles cured my nervousness and
brought me peaceful sleep. My aches and
pains all went away, and I could soon eat
heartily. In about three weeks I was as well
as you see me now, so it is not necessary to
say any more. I have every reason to be a
strong advocate of Clements Tonic, and you
are welcome to publish my opinion and
statements far and wide.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Sydney William Finnie, 32 Bav-treet, Ultimo
Sydney, in the Colony of New Sou.h Wales, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that I have care-
fully read the annexed document, consisting of
three folios and consecutively numbered from
one to three, and that it contains and is a
true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full per-
mission to publish in any way my statements —which
I give voluntarily, without receiving any payment;
and I make this solemn declaration conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by virtue of the
provisions of an Act made and passed in the ninth
year of the reign of her present Majesty, intituled
“An Act for the Dfe-e effectual abolition of Oaths
and Affirmations taken and made in the various
Departments of the Government of New South
Wales, and to substitute Declarations in lieu thereof,
and for the suppression of voluntary and Extra-
judicial Oaths and Affidavits."

Messrs. Kutnow & Co., Ltd., London.
Dear Sirs.— l have recently seen your advertise-
ment and offer in the London Morning Leader, and
shall be glad if you will send me a bottle of your
Carlsbad Powder for use in my family. I first heard
of your preparation from Sgt. Gardener, R.A.M.C.
(St. John Ambulance Brigade, Birmingham (City)
Division), who has just returned in charge of invalids
from South Africa. He was for some time stationed
at Princess Christian Hospital, Pine Town Bridge,
Natal, and had also served at several other base
hospitals. His first dutv on arriving in Durban, in
April last, was to assist in burying four victims from
Enteric Fever. As I was about sending out nine
more men (they sail on the Lismore Castle on the
7th), I asked him to address them. In the course of
his remarks he urged them to lay in a supply of
Kutnow’s Powder. He himself had attended to
HUNDREDS of CASES of ENTERIC, and the only
medicine he partook of was KUTNOW’S POWDER
twice or thrice a week as instructed on the bottle.
He had never been “ SICK OR SORRY ’’ for an hour,
and he attributed this fact to the bi-weekly use of
KUTNOW. In following Sgt. Gardener I suggested
that the men should lay in a stock of KUTNOW, and
I trusted they would benefit from the advice given
and would return to England, looking as strong and
hearty as he did.
Yours faithfully,
J. R. STAMP,
Supt. Sec., St. John Ambulance Brigade,
Birmingham (City) Division.

Along the Castlereagh.
BOBBIE’S “TROUBLE"
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Norah came home, Aunt flared up at first;
but her heart is softer than bread, and she
did not insist upon Norah going away again.
Norah is Uncle Jim’s first wife, and the
Woman with the Past. She came back one
dripping night, years after all thought her
dead, to claim her son Bobbie, and she has
been on the selection ever since. Norah
is a good worker, faithful to Aunt’s children, and
much sought by the motherless kids in the dis-
trict. After Norah came home Bobbie grew like
a sapling; he was almost a man before Uncle
Jim’s first weaners were ready for the store. He
was supposed to attend school at Conroy’s Flat,
but he preferred the breezy ranges where he and
the young Brannigans played at bushrangers.
Between them they owned an iron pot, which
Bobbie would place over his head, like Ned Kelly,
as he stood statuesque and defiant at the mouth of
Warrigal cave. The Brannigan clan then assaulted
him with clods and loose bark until he cried out,
“Don’t throw at me legs !” —which was a sign of
surrender. Then young O’Shea Brannigan,
freckled and foxy-haired, would don the iron pot
and rush upon the clan like a whirlwind, shouting
“I’m Ned Kelly! yous can hit me where, yous
like ! ” There is a fine position in the “ foorce
awaiting O’Shea Brannigan. Nine of his family
are policemen ; the remaining ten have degene-
rated into publicans. .
“Rat” Hennessy was Bob’s dearest friend,
and the Castlereagh boys bitterly envied him
his close intimacy with Rat. Rat _was the
mysterious man whom they only saw at intervals.
They knew he travelled to the out-back stations,
crossed the Darling by the punt at Wilcannia,
drifted with mobs of horses from The Towers to
Bourke, bartering, coaxing, and thieving. He
knew blacks as a boy knows locusts. Ihe
police regarded him with suspicion. Many
times Bobbie begged of Norah to let him go
north and earn his living as a shearer or drover;
but Norah, with a wild fear in her heart, cried out
against the idea. Norah Sloan, a drover s daugh-
ter, knew the Bush as a wild-duck knows. the
reedy shallows of a lagoon. Deep in her gipsy
heart lived that queer aching for the dry tracks,
the morning camp on the treeless ridge, the blue
wood-smoke on the gully-edge, . the wheeling
pigeons that hover across the still billabongs. But
she dreaded the thought of Bobbie going away.
“No” she said, “ Bobbie wasn’t old and hard
enough to fight his fight just yet. Maybe in a
year or two she might let him travel. I hen
she would go to his room and put his clothes
straight. Whenever Norah was troubled she dgo
into Bobbie’s room and straighten things up.
And Bobbie grew more and more restless. He
loved his mother and the Castlereagh, its lagoons ■
and magpies ; but his blood sang the song of the
Open Road. Rat Hennessy had glutted him
with stories of wonderful trips with cattle, and
tales of how money was made and spent in Bris-
bane and Sydney. And in the spring Bobbie was
missing for a week. Norah was frantic. Heave
th’ young imp alone!” said Uncle.
glad ter come home first night it rams hard.
One morning there was the thud of hoofs along
the river-bank. Presently Rat Hennessy rode
up the homestead track, followed by Bobbie.
Bobbie was riding a dark little beauty of a mare,
who seemed*to hold a touch of Arab m her small
bones and lean head. Uncle stood and watched
if* mother’s about I’d like to say good-bye
to her, father,” stammered Bobbie.
“ Hurry - up, then ! ” squeaked Rat. His
feet twitched in the stirrups. “This good-bye
business is rot .. . sends a man to chokee. Hel
lfc Uncie took a step towards them. “Whose
horse is that yer ridin’, Bob ? ’ he asked sharply
“Mine,” snapped Hennessy. Nobody s
giving’em away just now.”
Uncle examined Bobbie s mount a little craftily.
“Two straight bars over D; thats Wooloombi
station brand. Things are lookm up, eh, Rat'
Then he turned to 'Bobbie, his teeth set: Cret
off that damned horse ! ” T t 1
“Plug him!” Rat moved towards Uncle
Jim, as though to ride him down.
“ G-et off that horse ! ” thundered Uncle. .
Bobbie appeared to be fighting a mob of invis-
ible devils; the Spring was in his blood and the
North was calling in a woman s voice, and the
North road stretched away, away. He turned
from Uncle uneasily. “Good-bye, father, leli
mother that ” . _ , c , .
“Listen!” said Rat. A far-off hoof-beat
echoed across the Flat. “We’re trapped.
Hennessy rushed his horse up the track, taking
the slip-rails at the end as if he was riding a
steeple-chaser. Before Uncle could seize the
bridle Bobbie was off too, shouting farewells from
a bend in the river. , . ~ .
When Norah came home that night, weary
from her search amongst the homesteads, she
asked Uncle for news of Bobbie. Uncle drew a
long breath and said, “Well, Norah my gel, a
cove called this morning an’ told us that Bob had
gotasteddy job in Queensland. What use
worryin’—he’ll come home first wet night.
• * * *
It was strange how the troopers began to hang
about the paddock. If Aunt or Norah went to
the fence they discovered a trooper leaning casu-
ally behind a tree. Sometimes one would enter
the house suddenly without anybody heapng him
ride-up, and start a friendly conversation with
Aunt while his eyes kept wandering round for
any stray bit of letter-paper from Bobbie that
might be blowing about. At last there was a day
when two came with a warrant for Bobbie s arrest,
and a month afterwards there was news that Bobbie
had been taken at Bourke on a charge of stealing
Paddock Queen, the property of Michael Feeney,
of Wooloombi station, on the Qastlereagh. Bobbie
got three years, and they sent him to Wilcannia
ga Norah didn’t see the papers, but someone had
to tell her. Aunt blurted out the news one night
when Norah was putting the children to bed.
Norah sat still in the chair with an undressed
child in her arms. Aunt said, “ Three years isn t
long, Norah. P’raps it’ll make a man of him.
You may be sure they’ll take to him, him being a
likeable lad. Most men take to him. Of course
it’s hard. But I’d bear up, girl. Damn that
Rat Hennessy !” Then Aunt looked at Norah s
face, and went into the other room to quieten the
children. , , 4 . ,
Norah sat so still that the child m her arms
slept. Somehow her dark gipsy hair became un-
loosened from its fastening, and tumbled about
the child’s face. Uncle came in and stood awk-
wardly beside her. “ Are yer asleep, Norah, my
gel?”
She answered, with her face close to the child’s,
“ No, Jim, I’m not asleep.” , .
“I’m thinkin’,” said Uncle, shuffling his feet;
*« l» m thinkin’ of takin’ up a bit more land, so s
when Bob comes out I can give him a good start.
Gaol’s a hell of a place,” he said, scratching his
forehead —“ a hell of a place. Well, good night,
Norah, my gel! ”
“ Good night, Jim.” . _
Nobody seemed to go to sleep that night, x ast
midnight, when the rain was thundering on the
roof, Uncle Jim heard a low sob from the room
where Norah was—such a sob as sometimes comes
from a hurt child, and, immediately after, it was
repeated at his side. Aunt had heard it, and was
crying too.
• • * *
Three years soon slipped away, and Uncle was
doing well. A few months before Bobbie came
out he opened an account at the local bank, and
thereafter received blue official envelopes ad-
dressed “James Sloan, Esq.” Whenever any-
body called, Uncle would turn the conversation
on banking matters and “ my friend th’ manager.”
One afternoon Aunt saw a man in a grey suit
walking towards the house—with Bobbie. Uncle
Jim and Norah were away at the siding. Bobbie
seemed slightly dazed when Aunt spoke to him ;
he appeared to have forgotten her. He stood
holding the verandah-post and wiping his hot face
with a handkerchief. The man in the grey suit
explained that Bobbie wasn’t quite that
he’d be all right after a day or two. Hedidnt
stay, but went back to the station.
It was dark when Norah came home. _ Aunt
met her outside, and told her that Bobbie had
arrived. She took a deep breath and made quick
gestures with her hands. “Where is he? Has
he been asking forme?”
“Of course he has.” Aunt gave her the lie and
the State-orphan Aunt had got to help
with the children, was reading aloud from a paper
something she was going to recite at the Bran-
nigan’s ‘ ‘ social. ” Norah slipped round the veran-
dah to where Bobbie was sitting in a chair, his
face to the river. She approached him like a
woman who had pictured their meeting through
long, aching nights and days. ...
“Bobbie!” She knelt beside his chair, and
tried to draw him towards her. He leaped up
white-faced, and fell back crouching against the
verandah-rail. “ Take away your handcuffs !
I’ll be quiet. . . . What’s the good shoving
a man about; if I hit back, it’s solitary. . . .
Yous trapped me all-right Where s Rat
Outside Bourke . . . Yous fired on me. . . - Bad
luck for me—it didn’t, eh? Ha! ha! Wish
those warders wouldn’t sneak about . . . sending
me up for solitary. I’ll behave. Don’t bang me
He stood wide-eyed and trembling, his hands
thrown-up as though pushing back some ponder-
ous shape, the sweat of fear streaming down his
“ It’s me —your mother. I won’t hurt you,
dear. ” She stood up, and tried to soothe him.
“Stop!” he whispered. “No talking after
lights are out. ’S’h ! ” He turned his white face
towards her, and for a fraction of time intelligence
stayed in his eyes. “ Hush . . . Mother !”
Then reason vanished like a harlequin through
one of the million traps in his brain. His hands
went up again to keep off the dreadful gaoler. .
Uncle and Aunt stood inside, talking in whis-
P< From the stillness of the house came Maggie’s
voice, flat and monotonous, reciting her “piece”—
“I know not whether laics be right ,
Or whether laws be wrong,
All that I know, who lie in gaol,
Is that the wall is strong,
And that each day is like a year —
A year whose days are long.
“ For they starve the little frightened child,
Till it weeps both night and day;
And they scourge the weak, and flog the fool,
And gibe the old and gray ;
And some grow mad, and all grow bad,
And never a word may say.”
And Bobbie stood slouching against the veran-
dah-rail, wagging his head. “I’ll say anything,”
he whimpered, “ if you won’t bang me against the
wall.”
Norah sat down on the boards, with hands
clutching her knees. “They’ve drove him mad !
. . . Oh I . . . God forgive them 1 ”
m m m *
It is strange how the kids have taken to Bobbie
since he came out of gaol. He doesn’t rush about
as in the old days, but sits on the creek bank play-
ing a mouth-organ. If you speak to him, he smiles
meekly, but does not answer. He eats and sleeps
well, but always as if he was watched. Norah has
no trouble with him ; and, when the Fear takes
him, he always comes to her for shelter. Uncle
Jim will sit, sometimes for an hour, watching him
until he can watch no longer. Then he goes away
softly, muttering, “By Gawd ! ”
N.S.W.
ALBERT DORRINGTON.
Some See-Lee Syllables.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
State politics are very mixed,
As anyone may plainly See,
When ’twixt the two opposing lists
No greater difference exists
Than Tweedledum from Tweedledee.
When prosperous times bring sweet content,
And surpluses grow easi-Lee,
The Premier smiles in manner bland,
And proudly says, “ You understand,
lam the country’s saviour—See ?"
When cadging deputations come
And ask for votes so sturdi-Lee, .
He thinks how he may gain support,
And answers them with cautious, short,
And hypothetical “ We’il See.”
“ The Ship of State sails well,” he cries,
“ With a wet sheet and a flowing See.”
When “ ’Bout ship ! ” the commander says,
What happens if she misses stays?
Grim, threatening rocks are on her Lee.
His adversary takes the stump
And rates him with scant courte-See ;
The Premier gets upon his feet
And answers him (in Scotch) with heat,
“ The scoonril’s tale is a’ a Lee.”
The billowy main is treacherous ;
Dark secrets hath the deep, deep See. . . .
“ My skeletons I safely keep ;
But may not jobs as sound-Lee sleep
Beneath the soft and verdant Lea ? ”
For difference between L and S
The people do not care a D .
They only murmur, in a glow
Of cynical contempt, “ We know
It’s all a thing of £ s. d.”

A Sydney Man
Mr. James Hudson, 10 Lennox-street, Newtown, this city, says: “I work in the Bondi sewer.
There is a constant drip, drip there; and when working it mostly happens that one is wet through.
Some parts are better than others; but while working in the shafts one is continually damp, and this
affected my health. My trouble came with a bad cold in my kidneys, which caused terrible pains in
the back. I did my work, but it was most fearful torture for me to do so, as you can well understand.
I was exceedingly bad with these pains, when I saw Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills advertised, and it
struck me by the name of the remedy that it should be good for my complaint. I procured a box at
Newman’s Pharmacy, in King-street, and they cured me in a fairly quick time. I could not take
them constantly, being afraid to use them while working in the wet sewers; but I managed to take
two doses a week. The nature of my work causes me to nave slight returns of the pain ; hut I take a
dose of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, which never fails to oure me. lam never without them in the
house.”
The household duties which
devolve on most women tax the
Kidneys. Stooping positions,
straining the back, and the cares
of the home will in time affect
the most healthy of womankind.
Every woman will find quick
relief and permanent cure in
the use of

Doan’s Backache Kidney pills.
They act on the Kidneys only, and never
fail to relieve the aching back and cure every
form of urinary disorders.

A nurse, residing at 428 Jones-street, Ultimo,
states: “I have been a sufferer from itchiness of

diately.”
The lady gave us these facts twelve months ago,

used this Ointment for various purposes since, and
noticed its splendid curative powers, and must
acknowledge that it is the finest remedy for skin

Doan’s Ointment is splendid in all Diseases of

Marketin’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Did you ever get your livin’
In the market-gard’nin’ line—
Turnin’ out of bed at midnight
In the wet or in the fine ?
All day long you put-in slavin’,
Gettin’ ready with your load,
Then you snatch a few hours’ sleepin’
Ere you start upon the road.
Wife, she gets the coffee ready,
And some hot scones crisp and brown,
But it’s pitch-dark, and it’s rainin’,
And it’s twenty miles to town.
With a sack across your shoulders
And another o’er your knees,
You are doin’ what you can do
For your warmth and for your ease.
Patiently the old horse trudges,
Teachin’ you a lesson bright—
But you feel right at your lowest
In these late hours of the night.
Dreary looks the sleepin’ oity
As you trace the echoin’ street;
And a sad and lonesome object
Is the bobby on his beat.
But it’s down at Paddy’s Market
Is the dreariest of all;
Waitin’ for the place to open,
How the weary minutes crawl!
When it’s bargainin’ with Chinkies,
Or a “ Strike-me-balmy ” “ bloke ” :
“ Whaffor ?—too much ! Me no givee 1”
“Bli’me, mate, I’d quick be broke I”
When you’ve ploughed, and hoed, and harrowed,
Watched and tended, night and day,
And the price you get is nothing
But “just givin’ stuff away.”
Then you swear you’ll “chuck ” the gard’nin’,
And you’ll try some other “ game”—
But the summer follows winter,
And you’re at it just the same !
But there’s nights the stars are glorious,
And the moon’s a picture grand—
When the quietness and beauty
Make it seem another land.
From the ridge the velvet darkness,
That envelops all the town,
Makes the city lights resemble
Bright stars that have fallen down.
And there’s times you strike it lucky,
When the stuff’s a “ rippin’ price ”
When the “ strike-me ” bloke’s a brother,
And a Chow smells almost nice !
Then you swear you’ll stick to gard’nin’—
It’s as good as any “ game ”;
And though summer follows winter,
You are at it still the same.
N. S.W.
ULOOLA.
That Face!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was when I had half-time schools a couple of
hundred miles from Sydney. I got travelling-
expenses before leaving the' city ; and, when I
reached the township nearest to my destination,
was 9s. 6d. to the good. This went for grog. I
had a pretty glorious night, and a lovely head for
breakfast.
On Sunday, the local board-secretary drove me
to my destination, and I took up my quarters
with one of the best families. Hitherto it had
only been suspected of cattle-duffing. The bad and
medium families had been convicted, and had
either served their sentence or were in the middle
of it.
On Monday morning I set out for the school,
six miles away. I rode along a bridle-track
between walls of dense scrub, with, at intervals of
100 yards or so, big gum-trees near the track. I
was rather startled to see—projected from behind
the first of these —a man’s grinning face. I spoke,
and the face disappeared like a flash. Next tree
the same face—a hideous, distorted countenance-
showed itself. I shook up the moke, in order to
shake off the face, but it was no use. The thing
peered from behind every big tree I struck.
“ Well,” I thought, “last night’s grog may be
at the bottom of it ” ; so, instead of returning to
my quarters in the evening, I rode into the town-
ship to interview the publican. Told him I’d been
‘ ‘ seeing things ” —didn’t mention what —and asked
if he thought his liquor had anything to do with it.
He was indignant, and called in a big pound-
keeper who acted as chucker-out, and who wanted
to fight me for a sovereign. “ Would make me a
present of five bob if I stood afore him for six
rounds.”
The publican said: “Why, darnit all, man, I
remember yourself saying it was good tack.”
This was true. I had been led into complimentary
remarks about the stuff—as half-a-dozen loafers
now suddenly remembered —and testified. The
pound-keeper reckoned it a fair thing for me to
shout for all hands and apologise, which I did.
Then he wanted badly to know what I had been
seeing, but I wouldn’t say. In a fit of depression
I wrote home and told about the “face.”
Next morning I started for the seminary, and
again the awful face dodged along; but this trip
it was surmounted by something like a decayed
sugar-mat. It was dreadful!
After school I wrote-out my resignation, and
told Prigg—the man I lodged with—what I had
done, and why. He guffawed, and said, “ Why,
bless your innocence, that’s only me wife’s brother
Jim ! He’s been balmy for years, but he’s harm-
less as a child. It’s only tricks he’s up to ! ”
I endured the face for a month, but didn’t like
it, as it interfered with my digestion and sleep. I
was wearing to a shadow.
Now, when I was a youngster, I’d had a
tick in the top of my head, whioh must have
gone close to the brain, as it nearly settled
me. I broke part of it off, but neglected to
extract the remainder, whioh kept on boring.

YARRUM.
Mickey.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mickey was a born black-mailer, and I lived to
know it.
It was this way. When we were boys together,
I was guilty of the crime of my life, and Mickey
was the only living soul besides myself who knew
of it. I wanted to fetch a colt in from the country
and Mick volunteered to lend me a hand. I
mounted the first moke I came to, which hap-
pened to belong to the bailiff, and Mickey rode an
animal that had worked all day for a butcher.
It was just after dark when we set out. When
we got the colt out on the road, and headed for
home, he went at a terrible rate, and we looked
like losing him at a cross-road.
I left Mickey to bring up the rear, and sent the
bailiff’s horse along to head the young ’un.
I just managed to come up with him about half-
a-mile from the turn-off, and then there was a
neck-and-neck go. After a bit, I heard something
crack; and my horse and I sprawled over the
road, in against the bank of a cutting.
When Mickey came upon the scene, he wanted
to know, and I told him of the crack. Then he
fot a light, and found the animal’s leg broken,
t must have been an old break.
I made Mickey get a big stick and soratoh the
bank well to make it appear that the crock had
slipped and rolled down. Animals were in the
habit of rolling into that cutting. I had been
riding without a saddle, so I just nipped the
bridle off, and we double-banked to town.
In next issue of our local paper I noticed that a
man claimed five shillings from the municipal
council for removing a dead horse from the road.
Then I breathed freely again—but not for long.
Mickey took to borrowing threepences, and he
came so often that I resented the attacks, and re-
minded him that I was neither an elder brother
nor a parent.
One night I was going to a circus, and Mickey
—who thought he’d like to go, too —wanted to
borrow a shilling. I reminded him that I had
only the one; and he, after speaking of the inci-
dent at the cross-roads, said it would be just as
well for me to let him have it. Mickey was pre-
sent at that circus—l wasn’t.
One Sunday I was going to church, and he
wanted to borrow “ thruppince.” I had only one
on me, and that was for the plate. I told him I
hadn’t one, and he said it might be as well for me
to find it. I found it.
A few days after I was going down town to buy
a pair of fancy pigeons, and had the half-crown
with me. Mickey turned up, and wanted “ the
lend of two shillin’.” I couldn’t see it for a bit.
Then Mickey indicated the bailiff, who was step-
ping up the street, and I had to go without my
pigeons.
Another time I had to burgle my little sister’s
money-box to supply his wants.
Mickey was getting intolerable. Altogether, I
had lent him about £2, and it never struck me
until the other day that I might have settled the
whole matter with the bailiff for any sum from
twenty-five shillings down.
After a while it occurred to me that my only
hope for peace lay in murdering either Mickey or
the bailiff, and after mature consideration I de-
cided that Mickey would have to quit. I had not
quite settled on the particular poison, when a
fever came along and took him. Often since then
I have thought what a splendid thing it was for
Australia that he didn’t live to be a man.
I visited him when he was near his end, to make
sure that he hadn’t sold his interest in me to the
hospital-nurse, or somebody.
Just before he went-out he looked up at me and
whispered : “ You’ll—have ter —100k—after —me
dorg—or I’ll tell —the bailiff —about .”
YARRUM.
A copy of “A Policy for the Common-
wealth,” The Bulletin’s particularly suc-
cessful pamphlet, will be posted from this office 'o
any Australian address on receipt of Bd. in stamps
(of any State).
Earl of Dundonald, the swaggah officah in
command of Buller’s cavalry before Ladysmith,
seems to have taken the Boer tactics to heart. He
proposes that the future British defence of S’Africa
shall be maintained on free and easy lines, the
men being allowed to work for themselves in their
own time, wearing any kit they choose to adopt.
This from a man of Dundonald’s training in the
school of plumes and pipeclay amounts to an aw-
ful satire on Australian military fiddle-de-dee.
Gadara, somewhere nearTumutand Adelong
(N.S.W.), has now entered the running the
billet.of Federal capital. But if the Federal Ter-
ritory is to be large enough to prevent the
increased land-values (created by the expenditure
of the Commonwealth money on the building of a
Federal capital) from spreading over the border to
be mopped-up by the private landlord outside,
then it should take in three or four counties.
And when two or three pin - point villages,
within half-a.-dozen miles of each other,, howl
at each other for separate possession of the
capital, the situation is ridiculous. There is
no reason why Gadara (apparently named after
the New Testament place, which the swine left in
such haste), or Tumut, or Bombala, should not be
a speck on the surface of the Federal Territory.
But they can’t be the Federal Territory, unless
the Federal Legislature has lost its head.
